Tahjir: Marking Land Does Not Confer Ownership; Priority and Exceptions
Definition and rulings of tahjir and its distinction from ihya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tahjir (marking) and ihya (revival), and state the legal effects of each, including the three-year priority rule.

Core Idea
Marking land gives priority for three years; only actual revival confers ownership.

This passage explains the concept of tahjir — the act of marking dead land to indicate an intent to revive it. While tahjir gives the marker a priority right over others, it does not establish ownership. The passage also details which acts constitute tahjir versus actual revival (ihya), and the three-year limit on priority. Additionally, it restates the prohibition on privatizing land near inhabited areas that is needed for public use.

Guided Path
1
Define tahjir and its etymology
Tahjir comes from 'hajr' meaning prevention, or from placing stones ('al-hijara'). It is the act of marking boundaries with stones or digging a well to assert priority.
The marker (al-muhajjir) does not own the land.
2
State the legal effect of tahjir
Tahjir gives the marker priority for three years. After three years without revival, the imam can reassign the land. If another person revives it before three years, that person becomes the owner.
The three-year limit is based on a saying of Umar (rad).
3
Distinguish acts of tahjir from acts of ihya
Digging a well or placing thorns around the land is tahjir. Plowing, building a dam, or digging a canal is ihya. Irrigation combined with digging canals is also ihya.
The distinction affects ownership: only ihya confers ownership.
4
Apply the rule to land near inhabited areas
Land that is close to an inhabited village cannot be revived for private ownership; it must be left as pasture or a place for harvests, similar to a river or road.
The imam cannot give away public necessities like salt mines and wells used by the community.

ومن أحياه إلخ: يعني من أحيا الموات بإذن الإمام ملكه عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا يملكه من أحياه، ولا يشترط فيه إذن الإمام؛ لقوله علي: "من أحيا أرضا ميتة فهي له"، أحمد والترمذي وصححه و به قالت الثلاثة، إلا عند مالك: لو تشاح أهل العامر يعتبر الإذن، وإلا لا وله قوله عليه: "ليس للمرء إلا ما طابت به نفس إمامه"، والمراد به المباحات إلا أن الحطب والماء والحشيش خصت بالحديث، فبقي ما عداها على الأصل، والحديث محمول على أنه أذن لقوم بأعيانهم، أو المراد به إذا كان بإذن الإمام جمعا بين الحديثين. [رمز الحقائق: ۳۸۸/۲] كان المحي ذميا شرط الإذن اتفاقا، ولو كان مستأمنا لم يملكه اتفاقا من "العيني" و"الطائي". ملكه: أي الموات وبغير إذنه لا يملكه. وإن حجر لا: أي منع غيره منها لا يملكه بالتحجير، وهو إما من الحجر وهو المنع؛ لأنه يمنع غيره، وإما من وضع الحجر؛ لأنهم كانوا يضعون الأحجار حولها تعليما للحدود كي لا يشركهم فيها، لكنه هو أولى بها، فلا تؤخذ منه إلى ثلاث سنين، فإذا لم يعمرها أخذها الإمام منه، ودفعها إلى غيره، وإنما قدر ثلاث سنين؛ لقول عمر الله ليس للمتحجر بعد ثلاث سنين حق"، هذا من طريق الديانة، وأما في الحكم، فإذا أحياها غيره قبل مضيها ملكها؛ لتحقق سبب الملك منه دون الأول، وإن حفر لها بئرا فهو تحجير، وكذا جعل الشوك حولها، ولو كربها أو ضرب عليها المسناة ، أو شق لها نهرا، فهو إحياء، وإن فعل أحدهما يكون تحجيرا، وإن سقاها مع حفر الأنهار كان أحياء [رمز الحقائق: ۳۸۹/۲] ولا يجوز إحياء ما قرب إلخ : [فيترك مرعى لأهل القرية ومطرحا لحصائدهم. (طائي)] لتحقق حاجتهم إليه تحقيقا عند محمد، أو تقديرا عند أبي يوسف على ما تقدم، فصار كالنهر والطريق، ولهذا قالوا: لا يملك الإمام أن يقطع ما لا غنى للمسلمين عنه كالملح والآبار يستسقي منها الناس تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٢٥/٨]
Whoever revives it with the permission of the imam owns it; if he marks it, no [he does not own it]. And it is not permissible to revive what is close to the cultivated land. 'Of the mawat' 'Whoever digs a well in mawat, whether for a drinking trough or for irrigation' 'i.e. he does not own it' 'And with it (Abu Yusuf's view) fatwa is given as in Zakat al-Kubra, and it is the apparent riwaya as in Sharh al-Tahawi. Al-Zayla'i said: 'Shams al-A'imma relied on the saying of Abu Yusuf.' (Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: 7/78) al-Sarakhsi 'And if he narrated' 'Whoever revives it etc.': meaning whoever revives mawat with the imam's permission owns it according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: whoever revives it owns it, and the imam's permission is not required; due to his statement (the Prophet): 'Whoever revives a dead land, it is his' (Ahmad and al-Tirmidhi and authentic). And the three (other imams) said this, except that according to Malik, if the inhabitants of the cultivated area dispute, permission is required; otherwise not. And he (Abu Hanifa) has his statement (the Prophet): 'A person only has what his imam is pleased to give him.' The meaning is the mubahat (ownerless properties), except that firewood, water, and grass are specified by the hadith, so what remains is on the original ruling. The hadith is interpreted as referring to permission given to specific people, or it means when it is with the imam's permission, to reconcile the two hadiths. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/388) If the reviver is a dhimmī, permission is required by agreement. If he is a musta'min, he does not own it by agreement, from al-Ayni and al-Ta'i. 'Owns it': i.e. the mawat; without his permission he does not own it. 'And if he marks it, no': i.e. if he prevents others from it, he does not own it by marking (tahjir). This is either from 'hajr' meaning prevention, because he prevents others, or from placing stones, because they used to place stones around it to indicate boundaries so that others would not share with him. But he has priority, so it is not taken from him for three years. If he does not cultivate it after three years, the imam takes it and gives it to another. The three-year limit is due to the statement of 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him): 'The marker has no right after three years.' This is in religious matters. As for legal ruling, if another revives it before three years, he owns it because the cause of ownership is realized from him, not the first. If he digs a well for it, that is marking; likewise placing thorns around it. If he plows it, or builds a dam, or digs a canal, that is revival. If he does one of these, it is marking. If he irrigates it along with digging canals, it is revival. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/389) 'And it is not permissible to revive what is near etc.': So that it is left as pasture for the villagers and a place for their harvests (al-Ta'i). Because their need is realized actually according to Muhammad, or presumptively according to Abu Yusuf as mentioned, so it becomes like a river and road. For this reason they said: The imam does not have the right to give away what Muslims cannot do without, like salt and wells from which people drink. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/425)
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage covers two interrelated rulings: (1) reviving mawat with the imam's permission grants ownership according to Abu Hanifa, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say permission is not required, citing the Prophet's hadith 'Whoever revives a dead land, it is his.' Abu Hanifa counters with the hadith 'A person only has what his imam is pleased to give him,' reconciling by interpreting the first hadith as referring to permission. The relied-upon view is that of Abu Yusuf (no permission needed for a Muslim). For a dhimmī, permission is required; for a musta'min (temporary resident), he does not own it even with permission. (2) Tahjir (marking land with stones) does not confer ownership but gives priority for three years; if unproductive after three years, the imam can reassign it. Acts of revival (plowing, building dams, digging canals) constitute vs. mere marking (digging a well, placing thorns). The passage also restates the prohibition of reviving land near cultivated areas, preserving it for community use (pasture, threshing), likening it to a river or road that cannot be privatized.

Key Terms
التحجير (al-taḥjīr): Marking land with stones or digging a well to assert priority; does not confer ownership.
الإحياء (al-iḥyā’): Cultivating or developing dead land (plowing, building dams, digging canals, irrigating) to the extent that it becomes productive.
الذمي (al-dhimmī): A non-Muslim subject of an Islamic state who has a covenant (dhimma).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the legal consequence of digging a well in dead land?
A) It constitutes ihya and grants ownership
B) It constitutes tahjir and gives three-year priority
C) It gives immediate ownership if the well is deep
D) It is not permissible

Key Takeaway
Tahjir (marking) gives only a three-year priority right; ownership is established solely by actual revival (ihya) of the dead land.

Reviving Dead Land: Ownership, Tahjir, and Proximity
Ruling on ownership, tahjir, and proximity in reviving dead lands

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi intra-madhhab dispute on the requirement of Imam's permission for ownership of revived dead land, distinguish between tahjir and ihya, and apply the rule prohibiting revival near settled areas.

Core Idea
Revival of dead land confers ownership without Imam's permission per the relied-upon Hanafi view; tahjir gives only a temporary priority.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rulings on reviving dead lands (ihya al-mawat). You will learn the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the necessity of the Imam's permission for ownership. The text also clarifies the difference between mere enclosure (tahjir) and actual revival, and the restriction on reviving lands too close to inhabited areas to preserve community resources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
What is the act of reviving dead land, and what is the contested point? The dispute is whether the Imam's permission is required for ownership after revival.
2
State each position and its proof
State Abu Hanifa's position (permission required) and his proof (hadith: 'A person only has what his Imam approves...') and the Sahibayn's position (no permission needed) and their proof (hadith: 'Whoever revives a dead land, it is his.')
3
Understand the reconciliation and the mufta bihi
The text reconciles by interpreting the permission hadith as applying when the Imam grants permission to specific individuals, and the revival hadith as applying generally. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the Sahibayn, as it is zahir al-riwayah.
4
Distinguish tahjir from ihya and the three-year priority
Tahjir (enclosing) does not confer ownership; it only gives a priority right for three years. If another revives the land before three years, that person owns it. If left unrevived for three years, the Imam may give it to another. Actions like digging a well or placing thorns are tahjir; tilling, building a dam, or digging a canal are ihya.
5
Apply the proximity rule
Land immediately adjacent to settled areas (near al-‘amir) cannot be revived because it is needed for common purposes like pasture and dumping. This prohibition is based on the definitive or presumptive need of the community.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب إحياء الموات

أحكام إحياء الموات

ومن أحياه بإذن الإمام ملكه، وإن حجر لا، ولا يجوز إحياء ما قرب من العامر، أي الموات. يعني من أحيا الموات بإذن الإمام ملكه عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا يملكه من أحياه، ولا يشترط فيه إذن الإمام؛ لقوله عليه: "من أحيا أرضا ميتة فهي له" أخرجه أحمد والترمذي وصححه وبه قالت الثلاثة، إلا عند مالك: لو تشاح أهل العامر يعتبر الإذن، وإلا لا. وله قوله عليه: "ليس للمرء إلا ما طابت به نفس إمامه" والمراد به المباحات إلا أن الحطب والماء والحشيش خصت بالحديث، فبقي ما عداها على الأصل، والحديث محمول على أنه أذن لقوم بأعيانهم، أو المراد به إذا كان بإذن الإمام جمعا بين الحديثين. وبه يفتى، وهو ظاهر الرواية. وإن حجر لا: أي منع غيره منها لا يملكه بالتحجير، وهو إما من الحجر وهو المنع؛ لأنه يمنع غيره، وإما من وضع الحجر؛ لأنهم كانوا يضعون الأحجار حولها تعليما للحدود كي لا يشركهم فيها، لكنه هو أولى بها، فلا تؤخذ منه إلى ثلاث سنين، فإذا لم يعمرها أخذها الإمام منه ودفعها إلى غيره. وإنما قدر ثلاث سنين؛ لقول عمر ليس للمتحجر بعد ثلاث سنين حق. هذا من طريق الديانة، وأما في الحكم، فإذا أحياها غيره قبل مضيها ملكها؛ لتحقق سبب الملك منه دون الأول. وإن حفر لها بئرا فهو تحجير، وكذا جعل الشوك حولها. ولو كربها أو ضرب عليها المسناة أو شق لها نهرا فهو إحياء. ولا يجوز إحياء ما قرب من العامر: فيترك مرعى لأهل القرية ومطرحا لحصائدهم. لتحقق حاجتهم إليه تحقيقا عند محمد، أو تقديرا عند أبي يوسف على ما تقدم، فصار كالنهر والطريق. ولهذا قالوا: لا يملك الإمام أن يقطع ما لا غنى للمسلمين عنه كالملح والآبار يستسقي منها الناس.
Book of Reviving Dead Lands. Rulings of Reviving Dead Lands. Whoever revives it with the Imam's permission owns it, and if he encloses (tahjir) it, no. And it is not permissible to revive what is near settled land, i.e., dead land. Meaning: Whoever revives dead land with the Imam's permission owns it according to Abu Hanifa. And the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: whoever revives it owns it, and the Imam's permission is not required due to his (peace be upon him) statement: 'Whoever revives a dead land, it is his.' Ahmad and al-Tirmidhi narrated and authenticated it, and the three (schools) said with it, except according to Malik: if the people of the settled area dispute, permission is considered, otherwise not. And he (Abu Hanifa) has his (peace be upon him) statement: 'A person only has what his Imam approves of for him.' The intended meaning is permissible things, except that firewood, water, and grass are specified by the hadith, so what remains besides them remains on the original ruling. And the hadith is interpreted as that he gave permission to specific people, or the meaning is when it is with the Imam's permission, reconciling between the two hadiths. And with it fatwa is given, and it is the apparent transmitted (zahir al-riwayah). 'And if he encloses, no': meaning if he prevents others from it, he does not own it by tahjir. Tahjir is either from al-hajr meaning prevention because he prevents others, or from placing stones because they used to place stones around it to mark boundaries so that others do not share with him. But he has priority over it, so it is not taken from him for three years. If he does not cultivate it, the Imam takes it from him and gives it to another. It is only estimated at three years due to Umar's saying: 'The one who encloses has no right after three years.' This is from the way of religious duty (diyaana). As for legal ruling (hukm), if another revives it before that time passes, he owns it because the cause of ownership is realized from him, not the first. If he digs a well for it, it is tahjir, and likewise placing thorns around it. If he tills it, or builds a dam, or digs a canal for it, it is ihya (revival). 'And it is not permissible to revive what is near settled land': a pasture is left for the people of the village and a place to discard their harvests. This is due to the realization of their need for it definitively according to Muhammad, or presumptively according to Abu Yusuf as previously, so it becomes like a river and a road. For this reason they said: The Imam does not have the right to cut off what the Muslims are not self-sufficient from, such as salt and wells from which people draw water.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq deals with the revival of dead lands (ihya al-mawat). It begins by presenting a key intra-Hanafi dispute: According to Abu Hanifa, ownership of revived dead land requires the explicit permission of the Imam (ruler), whereas the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that permission is not necessary—the act of revival itself confers ownership. The companion's proof is the hadith "Whoever revives a dead land, it is his." Abu Hanifa's proof is the hadith "A person only has what his Imam approves of for him." The text reconciles these by interpreting the Imam's permission hadith as applying to general public lands, while the revival hadith applies to private permission. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the school is that of the companions, as it is the zahir al-riwayah and with it fatwa is given. Next, the text explains tahjir (enclosing or marking land): merely enclosing land without reviving it does not confer ownership; it only gives a priority right for three years. After three years, if the land remains unrevived, the Imam may give it to another. However, if someone else revives it before three years, that person owns it legally. Digging a well or placing thorns is tahjir, while tilling, building a dam, or digging a canal is considered actual revival. Finally, the text prohibits reviving land immediately adjacent to settled areas (what is near al-‘amir), as such areas are reserved for the common needs of the villagers, like pasture and dumping grounds. This is based on the principle of need (hajah), whether definitive or presumptive. Hence, the Imam cannot dispose of resources essential for the community, such as salt flats and public wells.

Key Terms
الموات (al-mawat): Dead land: land that is not owned by anyone and is not under cultivation or use, typically barren.
التحجير (al-tahjir): Enclosing or marking land with stones or similar to claim priority, without actual revival.
الإحياء (al-ihya): The act of reviving dead land through cultivation, construction, or other means that make it useful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi), what is the legal effect of tahjir (enclosing) on a piece of dead land?
A) It gives immediate ownership of the land.
B) It gives a right of priority for three years, but not ownership.
C) It is entirely ineffective and gives no rights.
D) It gives ownership only if the Imam approves.

Key Takeaway
Reviving dead land (ihya) confers ownership without Imam's permission; tahjir only gives a three-year priority, and land near settled areas cannot be revived.

Harim of Wells: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on well harim with hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the harim of wells (Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn) and apply the principle of preferring a general agreed-upon hadith over a specific disputed one to derive the ruling.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa rules a uniform 40-cubit harim for all wells; the two companions differentiate based on the method of drawing water.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the harim of wells dug in dead land. You will learn how the school's imams derive different measurements from the same hadith and how the principle of textual preference resolves the conflict. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to evaluate how general and specific texts are weighed in Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of harim
Harim is a protected zone around a water source or tree that belongs to its owner to ensure his benefit. For a well in dead land, the harim prevents others from digging nearby that could harm the well's water supply or access.
Harim is a property right, not a worship act.
2
Identify the well types
The text distinguishes two types: 'atan well (water drawn by hand) and nadi well (water drawn by a camel). This distinction is critical for the Sahibayn's ruling.
Arabic terms: العطن (al-'atan) and الناضح (al-nadi).
3
Compare the positions
Abu Hanifa: 40 cubits from every side for any well. Sahibayn: 40 cubits for 'atan, 60 cubits for nadi. The difference is based on the hadith and the greater need for space around a nadi well.
Cubit (ذراع) = about 45-50 cm.
4
Analyze the hadith evidence and principle
The hadith specifies: 'Harim of 'atan well: 40 cubits; harim of nadi well: 60 cubits.' Abu Hanifa uses a general narration without distinction and applies the principle that a general hadith accepted by all is preferred over a specific hadith that is disputed in acceptance.
This is a key usul principle: العام المتفق على قبوله مرجح على الخاص المختلف في قبوله.
5
Evaluate the practical consequence
For a nadi well, the harim is either 40 cubits (Abu Hanifa) or 60 cubits (Sahibayn). This affects the area where others cannot dig. The text does not explicitly give tarjih, but presents Abu Hanifa's argument as stronger.
The mufta bihi position is not stated; you should be aware of both views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن حفر بئرا في موات فله حريمها أربعون ذراعا من كل جانب، وحريم العين خمس مائة من كل، فمن حفر بئرا في حريمها منع منه، وللقناة حريم بقدر ما يصلحه، وما عدل عنه الفرات ولم يحتمل عوده إليه، فهو موات. أي حريم البئر أربعون ذراعا إلخ. ولا فرق في ذلك بين أن تكون البئر للعطن أو للناضح عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما: إن كانت للعطن فأربعون ذراعا، وإن كانت للناضح فحريمها ستون ذراعا؛ لقوله عليه: "حريم العين خمسمائة ذراع، وحريم بئر العطن أربعون ذراعا، وحريم بئر الناضح ستون ذراعا"، ولأن استحقاق الحريم باعتبار الحاجة، وحاجة بئر الناضح أكثر؛ لأنه يحتاج إلى موضع يسير فيه الناضح وهو البعير، وقد يطول الرشاء، وفي بئر العطن يستقى بيده، فلابد من التفاوت بينهما. وله ما روينا من غير فصل، ومن أصله أن العام المتفق على قبوله والعمل به مرجح على الخاص المختلف في قبوله والعمل به. وبئر العطن التي ينزح الماء منها باليد، وبئر الناضح التي ينزح الماء منها بالبعير. وحريم العين: لما روينا ولأن العين تستخرج للزراعة، فلابد من وطن يستقر فيه الماء، ومن موضع يجري فيه إلى الزراعة، وقدر الشارع بخمسمائة، ولا مدخل للرأي في المقادير، ثم قيل: الخمسمائة من الجوانب الأربعة، من كل جانب مائة وخمسة وعشرون ذراعا، والأصح أن خمسمائة ذراع من كل جانب، والذراع هو المكسرة، وهو ست قبضات، وكان ذراع الملك سبع قبضات فكسر منه قبضة. فمن حفر بئرا في حريمها منع منه لأنه صار ملكا لصاحب البئر ضرورة؛ لتمكنه من الانتفاع فكان الحافر متعديا بالحفر في ملك غيره، فإن حفر فللأول أن يؤاخذه بسد ما احتفره الثاني، وقيل: يضمنه النقصان، ثم يكبسه بنفسه، وليس له أن يكلفه الكبس بل يكبسه بنفسه، كما إذا هدم جدار غيره كان لصاحبه أن يؤاخذه بقيمته لا ببناء الجدار، وهذا هو الصحيح، فتقوم الأرض بلا حفر ومع الحفر، فيضمنه نقصان ما بينهما، وإن حفر الثاني بئرا بإذن الإمام في غير حريم الأولى، قريبة منه، وذهب ماء البئر الأولى إلى الثانية وغرقت الثانية، فلا شيء عليه، وللثاني الحريم من الجوانب الثلاث دون الجانب الأول. وللقناة إلخ: القناة مجرى الماء تحت الأرض. لم يقدر حريم بشيء يمكن ضبطه، وعن محمد: أنه بمنزلة البئر في استحقاق الحريم. وقيل: هذا قولهما، وعند الإمام: لا حريم له ما لم يظهر الماء على وجه الأرض؛ لأنه نهر في الحقيقة، فتعتبر بالنهر، قالوا: وعند ظهور الماء على الأرض هو بمنزلة عين فوارة، فيقدر حريمها بخمسمائة ذراع. وحريم شجر يغرس في الأرض الموات خمسة أذرع حتى لا يملك غيره أن يغرس شجرا في حريمه. فهو موات إلخ: لأنه ليس في ملك أحد، وجاز إحياؤه إذا لم يكن حريما لعامر.
And whoever digs a well in dead land, he has its harim (protected area) forty cubits from every side, and the harim of a spring (natural well) is five hundred cubits from every side. So whoever digs a well in its harim is prevented from it. And for a qanat (underground water channel), its harim is according to what suffices it. And whatever the Euphrates has shifted from and it is not likely to return to it, it is dead land. 'Harim of a well is forty cubits' etc. There is no difference in this between whether the well is for 'atan (watering livestock by hand) or for nadi (irrigation by camel) according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two companions: if it is for 'atan, then forty cubits; if for nadi, then its harim is sixty cubits, due to his (peace be upon him) statement: 'The harim of a spring is five hundred cubits, the harim of a 'atan well is forty cubits, and the harim of a nadi well is sixty cubits.' And because the entitlement of harim is based on need, and the need of a nadi well is greater because it needs a place for the nadi (camel) to walk and the rope may be long, while in a 'atan well one draws water by hand, so there must be a difference between them. And he (Abu Hanifa) has what we narrated without distinction, and his principle is that the general (hadith) that is agreed upon in acceptance and practice is preferred over the specific (hadith) that is disputed in acceptance and practice. A 'atan well is one from which water is drawn by hand, and a nadi well is one from which water is drawn by camel. And the harim of a spring: due to what we narrated, and because the spring is extracted for agriculture, so there must be a place for water to settle and a place for it to flow to agriculture, and the Lawgiver fixed it as five hundred, and there is no room for opinion in such measurements. Then it is said: the five hundred is from four sides, each side one hundred twenty-five cubits. The correct view is that it is five hundred cubits from each side. The cubit is the broken cubit (al-mukassara), which is six fists, and the royal cubit was seven fists, so one fist is deducted. So whoever digs a well in its harim is prevented because it has become the property of the owner of the well by necessity to enable his benefit, so the digger is transgressing by digging in another's property. If he digs, the first owner may hold him responsible for filling what the second dug. And it is said: he guarantees the decrease (in value), then he fills it himself; he is not obligated to have him fill it, rather he fills it himself, just as if someone demolishes another's wall, the owner can hold him for its value, not for rebuilding the wall. This is the correct view. So the land is appraised without the dig and with the dig, and he guarantees the difference. If the second digs a well with the Imam's permission outside the harim of the first but near it, and the water of the first well goes to the second and the second is flooded, then nothing is required of him, and the second has the harim from three sides except the first side. As for the qanat: the qanat is an underground water channel. Its harim is not determined by a precise measure. From Muhammad: it is like a well in entitlement of harim. It is said this is their (Sahibayn) view. According to the Imam (Abu Hanifa): it has no harim until water appears on the surface of the earth, because it is in reality a river, so it is considered like a river. They said: when water appears on the earth, it is like a gushing spring, so its harim is estimated as five hundred cubits. And the harim of a tree planted in dead land is five cubits, so that no one else may plant a tree in its harim. 'So it is dead land': because it is not in anyone's ownership, and its revival is permitted if it is not a harim of settled land.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains the rules of ḥarīm (protected area) for wells, springs, qanats, and trees in the context of reviving dead land. The ḥarīm is a perimeter around a water source or tree that belongs to its owner to ensure his use is not impaired. For a well dug in dead land, the ḥarīm is forty cubits from every side according to Abū Ḥanīfa, regardless of the well's purpose. However, the two companions (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad) differentiate: if the well is for ʻaṭan (watering animals by hand), the ḥarīm is forty cubits; if for al-nāḍiḥ (irrigation using a camel), it is sixty cubits. This is based on a hadith specifying each, and on the greater need for space in nāḍiḥ wells. Abū Ḥanīfa argues using a general hadith without distinction, and his principle is that a general hadith that is accepted and practiced is preferred over a specific hadith disputed in acceptance. The text does not clearly state which is mufta bihi, but the principle mentioned favours Abū Ḥanīfa's view. For a spring (ʿayn), the ḥarīm is five hundred cubits from each side, based on hadith and the need for water storage and flow channels. The correct view is that this measurement applies from each side. The cubit used is the 'broken cubit' of six fists, not the royal cubit of seven. If someone digs within another's ḥarīm, the owner can hold him responsible for filling the dig or paying compensation for the decrease in land value; the transgressor fills it himself. If a second well is dug outside the ḥarīm but causes the first well's water to drain, no liability arises, and the second well's ḥarīm is only on three sides (excluding the side adjacent to the first). For an underground qanat, there is a dispute: Abū Ḥanīfa says no ḥarīm until water surfaces, treating it as a river; the companions consider it like a well. Once water appears, it is like a spring with a 500-cubit ḥarīm. For trees planted in dead land, the ḥarīm is five cubits. Finally, land that was once irrigated but whose water source has permanently shifted becomes dead land again, eligible for revival as long as it is not the ḥarīm of settled land.

Key Terms
الحريم (al-harim): The protected area around a well, spring, or tree that belongs to its owner, preventing others from encroaching.
العطن (al-'atan): A well where water is drawn by hand for watering livestock.
الناضح (al-nadi): A well where water is drawn using a camel (or animal) for irrigation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two companions (Sahibayn), what is the harim of a nadi well (irrigation by camel)?
A) 40 cubits
B) 60 cubits
C) 500 cubits
D) No harim until water appears

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa holds a uniform 40-cubit harim for all wells; the Sahibayn differentiate (40 for hand-drawn, 60 for camel-drawn) based on a hadith and the principle of greater need.

Harim of Springs, Qanats, and Trees in Hanafi Law
Rulings on harim for springs, qanats, and trees, plus dead land revival

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings on harim for springs (500 cubits), qanats (dispute), and trees (5 cubits), and apply the rules of dead land reclamation.

Core Idea
The harim of a spring is 500 cubits from each side; for a qanat, it depends on water surfacing; for a tree, it is 5 cubits.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq continues to detail the harim for other water sources and trees. You will learn the fixed measurement for springs, the internal Hanafi dispute on qanats, and the uniform rule for trees. The passage also clarifies when land becomes dead again, opening it for revival.

Guided Path
1
Understand the harim of a spring (عين)
A spring has a harim of 500 cubits from each side. This is based on hadith and the need for water storage and flow for agriculture. The correct view is that 500 cubits applies to each side, not total.
The cubit used is the 'broken cubit' (6 fists).
2
Examine the dispute on qanat (قناة)
A qanat is an underground water channel. Abu Hanifa says no harim until water appears on the surface, treating it like a river. The two companions consider it like a well, thus a fixed harim. Once water surfaces, it becomes like a spring (500 cubits).
This is another intra-Hanafi dispute.
3
Learn the harim of trees
A tree planted in dead land has a harim of 5 cubits. No one else may plant a tree within that radius.
Smaller than water sources.
4
Understand the revival of dead land
Land that was once watered by a river (e.g., Euphrates) but has been permanently abandoned by the river becomes dead land again, eligible for revival (ihya) as long as it is not within the harim of settled land.
This is the final point of the passage.

Source Text

ومن حفر بئرا في موات فله حريمها أربعون ذراعا من كل جانب، وحريم العين خمس مائة من كل، فمن حفر بئرا في حريمها منع منه، وللقناة حريم بقدر ما يصلحه، وما عدل عنه الفرات ولم يحتمل عوده إليه، فهو موات. أي حريم البئر أربعون ذراعا إلخ. ولا فرق في ذلك بين أن تكون البئر للعطن أو للناضح عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما: إن كانت للعطن فأربعون ذراعا، وإن كانت للناضح فحريمها ستون ذراعا؛ لقوله عليه: "حريم العين خمسمائة ذراع، وحريم بئر العطن أربعون ذراعا، وحريم بئر الناضح ستون ذراعا"، ولأن استحقاق الحريم باعتبار الحاجة، وحاجة بئر الناضح أكثر؛ لأنه يحتاج إلى موضع يسير فيه الناضح وهو البعير، وقد يطول الرشاء، وفي بئر العطن يستقى بيده، فلابد من التفاوت بينهما. وله ما روينا من غير فصل، ومن أصله أن العام المتفق على قبوله والعمل به مرجح على الخاص المختلف في قبوله والعمل به. وبئر العطن التي ينزح الماء منها باليد، وبئر يكلفه الكبس بل يكبسه بنفسه، كما إذا هدم جدار غيره كان لصاحبه أن يؤاخذه بقيمته لا ببناء الجدار، وهذا هو الصحيح، فتقوم الأرض بلا حفر ومع الحفر، فيضمنه نقصان ما بينهما، وإن حفر الثاني بئرا بإذن الإمام في الشجر (al-shajar): Tree planted in dead land; its harim is 5 cubits.

When Land is Considered Mawat
Condition for land being mawat: no possibility of water return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for land to be considered mawat and identify whether a given piece of land qualifies.

Core Idea
Land is mawat if water has permanently left and it is neither owned nor harim.

Land ownership in Islam can be acquired by reviving dead land (ihya al-mawat). But what exactly counts as 'dead' land? This lesson unpacks a key condition from the Hanafi tradition: land becomes mawat when water has turned away from it and there is no possibility of return, as illustrated by the Euphrates river example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the example
The passage uses the Euphrates river as a case: water has turned away from its original course and cannot return. Identify what this scenario describes.
2
Extract the condition
The text states that such land is mawat because it is not owned and not within the harim of an inhabited area. Extract the two conditions: (1) no possibility of water return, (2) not owned and not harim.
The condition of no ownership is implied by 'not in anyone's ownership'.
3
Apply to new cases
Consider a river that has changed course permanently. The old riverbed is not owned and not adjacent to a village. Determine if it qualifies as mawat.
Apply the same principle: water cannot return and land is unowned.
4
Contrast with non-mawat land
If the water could return (e.g., seasonal flooding), or if the land is owned or within the harim, it is not mawat. Understand the boundary.
Harim is the buffer zone around a village; land there cannot be revived without permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما عدل عنه الفرات، ولم يحتمل عوده إليه، فهو موات، أي عود ماء الفرات. فهو موات إلخ: لأنه ليس في ملك أحد، وجاز إحياؤه إذا لم يكن حريما لعامر.
And whatever [water] has turned away from the Euphrates and its return to it is not possible, then it is mawat, meaning the return of the Euphrates water. So it is mawat etc. because it is not in anyone's ownership, and its revival is permissible if it is not the harim of an inhabited area.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage clarifies a condition for land to be considered mawat (dead land) that can be revived and owned. It uses the example of the Euphrates river: if water has turned away from its original course and there is no possibility of it returning, then the former riverbed or adjacent land becomes mawat. The reason is that such land is not in anyone's ownership, and it is not within the harim of any inhabited area. Therefore, it may be revived by anyone through the process of ihya al-mawat.

Key Terms
موات (mawat): Dead land: uncultivated, unowned land that may be revived by a person to claim ownership.
إحياء الموات (ihya al-mawat): Revival of dead land: the act of cultivating or developing mawat land, which grants ownership to the reviver.
حريم (harim): The buffer zone around an inhabited area or a well, which is reserved for the use of that area and cannot be revived without permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what makes land mawat?
A) Land that is far from any village and dry.
B) Land where water has turned away and cannot return, and it is not owned or harim.
C) Land that has never been cultivated.
D) Land that is not owned by anyone.

Key Takeaway
A piece of land is mawat only if water (if applicable) has permanently left and the land is unowned and not within a harim zone.

8 min
Dispute on River Easement (Hareem)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on river hareem

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and his two companions regarding the easement (hareem) of a river, distinguish between large and small rivers in this ruling, and identify the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa denies a fixed river easement; the two companions affirm it, and later consensus adopts their view with a measure dispute.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a river has a protected easement (hareem). The disagreement is not just on existence but also on its extent, and later scholars refined the ruling by distinguishing river sizes. You will explore the arguments and the operative position for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject matter
Determine what legal issue is being discussed: the existence and extent of a hareem (protected easement) for a river.
The term 'hareem' refers to the adjacent land reserved for access, maintenance, and disposal of mud.
2
State the positions
Recall Abu Hanifa's view: no hareem unless proven by evidence. Then the two companions' view: a hareem does exist for walking and dumping mud.
The two companions are Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
3
Examine the measure difference
Note that even among the companions, there is disagreement on the extent: Abu Yusuf says half the river's width on each side, Muhammad says the full width on each side.
The rationale: Abu Yusuf divides the width because mud is thrown on both sides; Muhammad considers the possibility of only one side being used.
4
Note the size distinction
According to Kashf al-Ghawamid, the dispute applies only to large rivers that do not need frequent dredging. For small rivers that require frequent dredging, there is consensus on a hareem.
This nuance was added by later commentators (Mulla Miskin, al-Fath).
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Later Hanafi scholars (al-Muhit, etc.) adopt the companions' view as the relied-upon position. For measure, there is no single authoritative choice, but Muhammad's view (full width) is often preferred for caution.
The tarjih is based on necessity and the preference for the more comprehensive protection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا حريم للنهر : أي من كان له نهر في أرض غير، فليس له حريم عند أبي حنيفة إلا أن يقيم البيئة على ذلك، وقالا : له مسناة النهر يمشي عليها، ويلقي عليها طينه، وكذا الخلاف لو حفر النهر في الأرض الموات والمسناة ما يبنى للسيل ليرد الماء عن الأرض كذا في "ملا مسكين" و "الفتح"، وقال العيني ولا حريم للنهر عند أبي حنيفة وقالا له حريم من الجانبين بقدر إلقاء الطين ونحوه، وبه قالت الثلاثة، وقيل: هذا بالاتفاق، وهو قول المحققين من أصحابنا، ذكره في "المحيط". [ رمز الحقائق: ٣٩٠/٢] ثم اعلم أن الصاحبين وإن اتفقا على أن النهر له حريما، لكن اختلفا في قدره، فأبو يوسف قدره بنصف عرض النهر من الجانبين؛ لأن طينه يلقي في جانبيه، فيقسم عرضه عليهما، وقدره محمد بكل عرضه من كل جانب؛ لأنه قد لا يمكنه إلقاء الطين من جانبيه جميعا، فيقدر بعرضه من كل جانب، وفي "كشف الغوامض": الاختلاف بين أبي حنيفة وصاحبيه في نهر كبير لا يحتاج فيه إلى كرى في كل حين وأما الأنهار الصغار التي يحتاج إلى كريها أي حفرها في كل وقت، فلها حريم بالاتفاق. (ملا مسكين، فتح)
And there is no easement for the river: meaning, whoever has a river in another's land, he has no easement according to Abu Hanifa unless he establishes evidence of that. The two companions said: he has the embankment of the river to walk on and to throw his mud upon. Similarly the difference exists if he dug the river in dead land. The 'musannah' is what is built for the flood to repel water from the land, as in 'Mulla Miskin' and 'al-Fath'. Al-Ayni said: there is no easement for the river according to Abu Hanifa, and the two companions said he has an easement on both sides to the extent of throwing mud and the like, and this is the view of the three imams. It is said this is by consensus, and it is the saying of the verifying scholars of our school, mentioned in 'al-Muhit'. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 390/2] Then know that although the two companions agreed that the river has an easement, they differed on its extent: Abu Yusuf estimated it as half the river's width on each side, because the mud is thrown on both sides, so the width is divided between them. Muhammad estimated it as the full width of the river on each side, because it may not be possible for him to throw mud from both sides together, so it is estimated as the width on each side. In 'Kashf al-Ghawamid': the difference between Abu Hanifa and his two companions is regarding a large river that does not require dredging every now and then. As for small rivers that need dredging (i.e., digging) at all times, they have an easement by consensus. (Mulla Miskin, Fath)
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the easement (hareem) of a river. The issue is whether a person who has a river flowing through another's land—or who digs a river in dead land—has a protected area around it for access and disposal of mud. Abu Hanifa's position is that there is no fixed easement unless the owner can prove a customary right. In contrast, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) affirm an easement, but they differ on its measure: Abu Yusuf says half the river's width on each side, while Muhammad says the full width on each side. The passage also notes a distinction: for large rivers that need infrequent dredging, the dispute applies; for small rivers requiring frequent dredging, there is consensus on an easement. Later Hanafi scholars (al-Muhit, Mulla Miskin) prefer the view of the companions, reconciling earlier disagreement.

Key Terms
حريم (hareem): The protected perimeter or easement around a river or well, reserved for access, maintenance, and disposal of debris.
مسناة (musannah): An embankment built along a river to walk on and to throw mud upon, serving as part of the claimed easement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is required for a river owner to have an easement (hareem)?
A) The river must be large and deep.
B) The owner must establish evidence (bayyinah) of the easement.
C) The river must be dug in dead land.
D) The owner must have permission from the landowner.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's operative position on river easement is that a river does have an easement, but its extent depends on the river's size and the specific reasoning of the two companions.

Definition and Ruling on Large Rivers
Defines shurb and states ruling on large rivers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Shariah definition of shurb and explain why large rivers are unowned and permissible for public use, citing the hadith.

Core Idea
Large rivers are not owned by anyone; water's force prevents private enclosure, so public use is permissible.

This passage introduces the Hanafi ruling on water rights in large rivers. After discussing reviving dead lands, the author turns to a fundamental need: water. Here we learn the precise definition of shurb and the legal principle that large rivers are unowned, based on the hadith of partnership in water. Understanding this distinction is crucial for property law and public entitlements.

Guided Path
1
Locate within the structure
The author places water rights after reviving dead lands because water is essential for land cultivation. Recognize that water rights logically precede reviving dead lands due to their originality and abundance of branches.
Commentators note that water rights could have been placed earlier but for the sake of structure they are here.
2
Define 'shurb' linguistically and technically
Lexically, 'shurb' means a share of water. In Shariah, it refers to the turn of benefiting from water for irrigation and animals. Note that 'shurb' is broader than 'shifa' (drinking for animals).
Some scholars criticize mentioning the lexical meaning in matn texts; it is more appropriate in commentaries.
3
Identify the legal status of large rivers
The text states large rivers are 'ghayr mamluuka' (not owned). The reasoning: the force of water prevents anyone from having exclusive possession (qahr al-ma' yamna'u qahr ghayrihi). Ownership requires enclosure (ihraz), which is impossible here.
Enclosure (ihraz) is the basis of ownership in Hanafi property law.
4
Derive the permissibility of uses
Since the rivers are unowned, every person may water his land, perform wudu, drink, set up a mill, and dig a canal to his land. This is a direct consequence of non-ownership.
The permission is general but subject to the condition of not harming others (implied).
5
Examine the hadith proof and its limitation
The hadith 'Muslims are partners in three: pasture, water, and fire' is cited as proof. But it applies only to unenclosed water. Once water is enclosed (e.g., in a container), it becomes owned like game after capture, and permission is required.
This distinction prevents misuse of the hadith to claim free use of private water.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الشرب إلخ: لما فرغ من ذكر إحياء الموات ذكر ما يتعلق به من مسائل الشرب؛ لأن إحياء الموات يحتاج إليه، وقدم فصل المياه على غيره؛ لأن المقصود هو الماء لا يقال: إذا كان الشرب مما يحتاج إليه الموات كان اللائق تقديم مسائل الشرب على مسائل إحياء الموات، قلنا لأصالته وكثرة فروعه يستحق التقديم على الشرب. [ تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٢٩/٨] هو نصيب الماء إلخ : هذا معنى الشرب لغة، وفي الشريعة: عبارة عن نوبة الانتفاع بالماء سقيا للمزارع والدواب، قال "الوافي : فيكون الشرب أعم . من الشفة؛ لأنها مخصوصة بالحيوان، ولك أن تقول: ذكر المعنى اللغوي كما فعله المصنف لا يليق في المتون. (فتح، تكملة) غير مملوكة إلخ لأن هذه الأنهار ليس لأحد فيها يد على الخصوص؛ لأن قهر الماء يمنع قهر غيره، فلا يكون محرزا، والملك بالإحراز. (فتح) ولكل أن يسقي إلخ يعني لما لم تكن هذه الأنهار مملوكة لأحد، فلكل واحد أن يسقي أرضه، ويتوضأ به، ويشربه، وينتصب الرحى عليه ويكري منها نهرا إلى أرضه؛ لقوله عليه: "المسلمون ركاء في ثلاثة الكلأ والماء والنار ، والمراد بالماء ما ليس بمحرز؛ لأنه بالإحراز يخرج من أن يكون مباحا كالصيد إذا أحرز، فلا يجوز لأحد أن ينتفع به إلا بإذنه.(فتح بتغيير) شر
Section on water entitlement etc.: When he finished mentioning reviving dead lands, he mentioned related issues of water entitlement because reviving dead lands requires it. He gave precedence to the section on waters over others because the objective is water. It is not said: if water entitlement is needed for dead lands, it would be more appropriate to present water entitlement issues before reviving dead lands; we say due to its originality and abundance of branches it deserves precedence over water entitlement. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Raiq: 429/8] 'It is the share of water' etc.: This is the lexical meaning of 'shurb'. In Shariah it is an expression for the turn of benefiting from water for irrigation of farms and animals. Al-Wafi said: so 'shurb' is more general than 'shifa' because the latter is specific to animals. You may say: mentioning the lexical meaning as the author did is not appropriate in matn texts. (Fath, Takmila) 'Not owned' etc. because these rivers no one has exclusive possession over them because the force of water prevents the force of others, so it is not enclosed, and ownership is by enclosure. (Fath) 'And everyone may water' etc. means since these rivers are not owned by anyone, everyone may water his land, perform ablution with it, drink it, set up a mill upon it, and dig a canal from it to his land, due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Muslims are partners in three: pasture, water, and fire.' And by water is meant what is not enclosed, because by enclosure it leaves being permissible like game when caught, so no one may benefit from it without permission. (Fath with modification)
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section titled 'Shurb' (water entitlement) following the discussion on reviving dead lands. The commentator explains why water issues are placed after reviving dead lands: because water is the fundamental need. The lexical meaning of 'shurb' is the share of water, while the Shar'i meaning is the turn of benefiting from water for irrigating farms and animals. The author then states the ruling: large rivers like the Tigris and Euphrates are not owned by anyone because water's force prevents exclusive possession, and ownership requires enclosure. Consequently, every person has the right to water his land, perform wudu, drink, set up a water mill, and dig a canal to his land from these rivers. The proof is the hadith: 'Muslims are partners in three things: pasture, water, and fire.' This hadith applies to unenclosed water; once water is enclosed (e.g., in a container), it becomes private property and cannot be used without permission.

Key Terms
شرب (shurb): In Shariah, the turn or entitlement to benefit from water for irrigation and animal watering.
محرز (muhraz): Enclosed or possessed property; for water, once it is collected in a container or well, it becomes owned and cannot be used without permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are large rivers not owned?
A) Because they are too large to be owned.
B) Because the force of water prevents enclosure.
C) Because the Prophet said so.
D) Because they belong to the government.

Key Takeaway
Large rivers are unowned and everyone may use them for irrigation, drinking, and milling, based on the hadith of partnership, provided the water is not enclosed.

Rulings on Owned Water Sources
Sets limits on using privately owned water: drinking and animal watering allowed, not irrigation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using privately owned water and identify the conditions and exceptions based on harm and necessity.

Core Idea
Using owned water for drinking and animal watering is allowed; irrigation is not, to protect the owner's interest.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq clarifies the limits of public access to privately owned water sources. While the hadith 'Muslims are partners in water' suggests a common right, the Hanafi school restricts it to immediate necessities like drinking and watering animals. Understanding these rules prevents property disputes and balances community needs with ownership rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Read the opening sentence: 'every person has the right to drink and water his animal, but not his land.' This is the central ruling. Note that it applies to owned rivers, wells, and cisterns.
Think about the difference between immediate consumption (drinking/animal) versus prolonged use (irrigation).
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The text cites the hadith 'Muslims are partners in three: pasture, water, and fire.' Understand how this hadith provides a basis for a common right in water.
Hanafis limit this partnership because of the owner's right through enclosure and the need to prevent harm (darar).
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah (legal link)
The hadith indicates a shared right, but the Hanafi school interprets this as only for essential uses. The passage explains that permitting irrigation would nullify the owner's benefit because there is no limit to water for land, whereas watering animals is limited.
Contrast the unlimited need for irrigation vs. limited need for drinking/animal watering.
4
Note the conditions and exceptions
The ruling changes if there is harm or if the water is enclosed. If the river banks might break due to many cattle, even animal watering can be prevented. Water in jugs or cisterns requires the owner's explicit permission.
Also note the discussion on theft: even if water is scarce and worth nisab, the hand is not cut due to shubhah from the partnership hadith.
5
Evaluate the cross-madhhab difference
Some other schools may allow irrigation if no harm results. The Hanafi position prioritizes the owner's exclusive right through enclosure, arguing that the hadith's generality is restricted by necessity and the principle of preventing harm.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that in a Hanafi context, you cannot take water for crops from a private well without permission, even if you are in need.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الأنهار المملوكة والآبار والحياض لكل شربه، وسقي دابته لا أرضه، وإن خيف تخريب النهر لكثرة البقور يمنع، والمحرز في الكوز والجب لا ينتفع به إلا أن يأذن صاحبه، لا وإن خيف تخريب النهر لكثرة البقور يمنع، والمحرز في الكوز والجب لا ينتفع به إلا أن يأذن صاحبه، لا ولو قليلا إن لم يضر بالعامة لأن الانتفاع المذكور لكل واحد إذا لم يضر بالعامة؛ لأن الانتفاع بالمباح لا يجوز إلا إذا كان يضر بأحد كالانتفاع بالشمس والقمر والهواء [ رمز الحقائق: ۳۹۱/۲] وإن أضربهم بأن يميل النهر العظيم إلى هذا الجانب إذا انكسر ضفته، فتغرق القرى والأراضي ونحوه لا يجوز. وقوله: "إن لم يضر بالعامة" متعلق بالرحى والكري. (عيني، ملا مسكين) لكل شربه وسقي دابته إلخ أي لا سقي أرضه، وإنما كان له حق الشرب وسقي الدواب؛ لما روينا من حديث "المسلمون شركاء في ثلاثة إلخ، ولأن الأنهار والآبار والحياض لم توضع للإحراز ، والمباح لا يملك إلا بالإحراز، ولكن المسافر لا يمكنه أن يأخذ ما يوصله إلى مقصده فيحتاج أن يأخذ مما يمر عليه مما ذكر ما يحتاج إليه لنفسه ودوابه وصاحبه، فلو منعه من ذلك لحقه ضرر عظيم، وهو مدفوع شرعا، بخلاف سقي الأراضي، حيث يمنع وإن لم يكن فيه ضرر؛ لأن في إباحة ذلك إبطال حق صاحب الأنهار؛ إذ لا نهاية لذلك، فتذهب منفعة صاحب الأنهار، فيلحقه بذلك ضرر، بخلاف سقي الدواب؛ لأن مثله لا يلحقه به ضرر ، حتى لو تحقق فيه الضرر يمنع تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٣٠/٨] لكثرة البقور: من البقر، وهو الشق، أراد أن صاحب النهر إذا خاف على النهر من سقي الدواب بأن تكسر ضفته، أو تنشق إلى موضع آخر، فحينئذ يمنع؛ لأن الحق له على الخصوص، وإنما أثبتنا حق الشرب لغيره للضرورة، فلا معنى لإثباته على وجه يتضرر به صاحبه؛ إذ به تبطل منفعته رمز الحقائق: ٣٩١/٢] الجب إلخ: ضبطه في "العيني" و"ملا" مسكين" بضم الحاء المهملة وتشديد الباء الموحدة، وهي الخابية، وقال في "الطائي" : هو بضم الجيم وهو الصريح، وفي بعض النسخ بالحاء المهملة، وهي الخابية ونحوها. على . إلا أن يأذن صاحبه لأنه ملكه بالإحراز ، فكان أحق به كالصيد إذا أخذه ولكن فيه شبهة الشركة لظاهر ما روينا، فيعمل فيما يسقط بالشبهة، ولو سرق الماء في موضع يعز فيه الماء، وهو يساوي نصابا لا يقطع. واعترض عليه بأنه هذا ينبغي أن لا يقطع في شيء من الأشياء كلها؛ لأن قوله تعالى: ﴿هُوَ الَّذِي خَلَقَ لَكُمْ مَا فِي الْأَرْضِ جَمِيعًا (البقرة: ٢٩) يورث الشبهة بهذا الطريق، وأجيب بأن العمل بالحديث يوافق قوله تعالى: ﴿هُوَ الَّذِي خَلَقَ لَكُمْ مَا فِي أَرْضِ جَمِيعا ولا يلزم من العمل به إبطال الكتاب، بخلاف قوله تعالى: ﴿هُوَ الَّذِي خَلَقَ لَكُمْ مَا فِي الْأَرْضِ جَمِيعًا فإن العمل به على الإطلاق يبطل العمل بقوله تعالى: ﴿الزَّانِيَةُ وَالزَّانِي ﴾ (النور : ٢) والسَّارِقُ وَالسَّارِقَةُ (المائدة: ۳۸)
And regarding owned rivers, wells, and cisterns: every person has the right to drink and water his animal, but not his land. And if the river is feared to be damaged due to many cattle, it is prevented. And (water) enclosed in a jug or cistern cannot be benefited from except with the owner's permission, even if a little, provided there is no harm to the public. Because the mentioned benefit for anyone is when there is no harm to the public; because benefiting from what is mubah is not permissible if it harms someone, like benefiting from the sun, moon, and air. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 391/2] And if it harms them, e.g., by diverting a large river to one side when its bank breaks, flooding villages and lands, etc., it is not permissible. And his statement 'if it does not harm the public' relates to the mill and digging. (Ayni, Mulla Miskin) 'Every person has the right to drink and water his animal' etc., i.e., not watering his land. He only has the right to drink and water animals due to the hadith we narrated, 'Muslims are partners in three', and because these rivers, wells, and cisterns were not set up for enclosure, and mubah is owned only by enclosure. But a traveler cannot take what will get him to his destination, so he needs to take from what he passes by what he needs for himself, his animals, and his companion. If he were prevented from that, great harm would befall him, and that is repelled by Shariah. Unlike watering lands, it is prevented even if there is no harm, because permitting that would nullify the owner's right, as it has no limit, so the owner's benefit would be lost, harming him. Unlike watering animals, because that does not harm him, so if harm is realized, it is prevented. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Raiq: 430/8] 'Due to many cattle' (البقور) from البقر (cattle) and it means the splitting. It means that if the owner of the river fears damage to the river from watering animals, such as breaking its bank or splitting it elsewhere, then it is prevented; because the right is specifically his, and we only established the right for others due to necessity, so there is no point in establishing it in a way that harms the owner, for then his benefit is nullified. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 391/2] 'The cistern' etc.: It is pronounced with damm on the ha and shadda on the ba, meaning the earthenware jar. Al-Tai said it is with jimm (jubb) which is clear. In some copies it is with ha (hubb), meaning the jar and the like. 'Except with the owner's permission' because he owns it by enclosure, so he has more right to it, like game when caught. But there is a semblance of partnership due to the apparent meaning of the hadith we narrated, so it is applied in cases of nullification due to semblance. If one steals water in a place where water is scarce, and it is worth a nisab, he is not amputated. An objection was raised: this should mean no hand is cut in any case because the verse 'He created for you all that is on earth' (2:29) creates a semblance. The answer is that acting on the hadith agrees with the verse, and acting on it does not necessitate nullifying the Book. Unlike acting on the verse absolutely, which would nullify the verses on cutting (for zina and theft).
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage addresses the rules for privately owned water sources (rivers, wells, cisterns). The basic ruling is that others have the right to drink and water their animals, but not to irrigate their land, because irrigating land consumes large quantities and would harm the owner's interests. The proof is the hadith of partnership in water, but it is limited by the owner's ownership through enclosure. However, if there is a risk of damage to the river due to many animals or banks breaking, even the animal watering can be prevented. For water enclosed in containers (jugs, cisterns), no one can use it without the owner's permission. There is a discussion about theft: if someone steals water worth the nisab in a water-scarce region, the hand is not cut because of a legal semblance (shubhah) from the verse that all things on earth are created for humans. An objection suggests this would preclude cutting for any theft, but the answer is that the hadith on water partnership provides a specific semblance here, whereas applying the verse absolutely would contradict explicit penal verses.

Key Terms
بقور (buqur): Splitting or breaking; here it refers to damage to the riverbanks caused by many cattle.
شبهة (shubhah): A legal semblance or doubt that prevents application of a penalty (hadd).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the right of a non-owner regarding water from an owned well?
A) He may take unlimited water for his land.
B) He may drink and water his animals, but not irrigate his land.
C) He may take water only with the owner's explicit permission.
D) He has no right at all.

Key Takeaway
Owned water is shared for basic survival needs, but ownership restricts excessive use like irrigation to prevent harm.

Rulings on Water Usage and Theft of Water
Ruling with proof on water usage and theft

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on permissible uses of water from public and owned sources and explain why the hadd for theft of water is not applied, citing the legal proofs.

Core Idea
Water from natural sources is mubah for basic needs; theft of water avoids hadd due to shubha of common ownership.

This lesson examines the Hanafi legal framework for water as a shared resource. We will see that water from natural sources is permissible for drinking and watering animals without ownership, but that irrigation of land is restricted. The lesson also addresses the unique ruling that theft of water, even reaching the nisab, does not incur the hadd penalty because of a doubt rooted in the general permissibility of water.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the text is ruling about: the permissible use of water from public and owned sources, and the penalty for theft of water.
The act of using water is mubah with conditions; the act of theft is haram but hadd is lifted.
2
Locate the dalil
The text cites the hadith 'Muslims are partners in three...' to establish common ownership of water, and the Quranic verse for the shubha.
The dalil is a hadith ahad, which is zanni al-thubut.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith indicates that water is common property, so it is permissible for basic needs. The shubha for theft comes from the verse 'He created for you all that is on the earth', creating doubt that water is owned.
The Hanafi school uses this shubha to drop the hadd, not to make theft permissible.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The text records an objection that this reasoning would apply to all theft. The Hanafi response limits the shubha to water.
This is an internal Hanafi critique and answer.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that theft of water does not incur hadd, as established in Zahir al-Riwayah.
This is based on istihsan and public benefit.

الْأَرْضِ
He may perform wudu with it, drink from it, set up a mill on it, and dig a canal from it to his land, provided it does not harm the public. Regarding owned rivers, wells, and cisterns, everyone has the right to drink and water his animal but not his land. If it is feared that the river will be ruined due to many cattle, it is prevented. Water stored in a pitcher or jar cannot be used without the owner's permission. No, if it does not harm the public, because the mentioned benefit is for everyone if it does not harm the public; because benefiting from the mubah is not permissible unless it harms someone, like benefiting from the sun, moon, and air. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 391/2] And if it harms them by diverting the great river to this side when its bank breaks, causing villages and lands to flood, etc., it is not permissible. His statement 'provided it does not harm the public' pertains to the mill and digging. (Ayni, Mila Miskin) 'For every drinking and watering of his animal, etc.' means not for watering his land. He only has the right to drink and water animals because we have narrated from the hadith: 'Muslims are partners in three, etc.' and because rivers, wells, and cisterns are not made for possession, and mubah is not owned except by possession. But a traveler cannot take what reaches his purpose, so he needs to take from what he passes of what is mentioned what he needs for himself, his animals, and his companion. If he is prevented from that, great harm would befall him, and that is prevented by Sharia, unlike watering lands, which is prevented even if there is no harm, because permitting that would nullify the right of the river owner since there is no limit to that, so the benefit of the river owner would go, and thus harm would befall him. Unlike watering animals, because its like does not cause him harm. Even if harm actually occurs, it is prevented. [Takmila al-Bahr al-Raiq: 430/8] 'Due to many cattle': from cattle, meaning the cracking. He meant that if the river owner fears for the river from watering animals because its bank might break or crack to another place, then he prevents it because the right is specifically his. We only established the right of drinking for others out of necessity, so there is no point in establishing it in a way that harms the owner, as it nullifies his benefit. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 391/2] 'Al-jubb, etc.': In al-Ayni and Mila Miskin, it is recorded with a damma on the unpointed ha and shadda on the pointed ba, meaning the jar. In al-Tai, it is with a damma on the jim, which is explicit. In some copies it is with an unpointed ha, meaning the jar and the like. 'Unless its owner permits' because he owns it by possession, so he has more right to it, like game when taken. But there is a semblance of partnership due to the apparent hadith we narrated, so it is acted upon where the doubt removes the cutting. And if water is stolen in a place where water is scarce and it reaches the nisab, the hand is not cut. It was objected that this implies that the hand should not be cut in any case because the verse 'He is the One who created for you all that is on the earth' (al-Baqara: 29) creates a doubt in this way. The answer was that acting upon the hadith agrees with the verse 'He is the One who created for you all that is on the earth' and acting upon it does not necessitate nullifying the Book, unlike the verse 'He is the One who created for you all that is on the earth' because acting upon it absolutely would nullify the verses 'The adulteress and the adulterer' (al-Nur: 2) and 'The thief and the she-thief' (al-Maida: 38).
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Reviving Dead Lands deals with two main rulings: (1) permissible uses of water from public and owned sources, and (2) the theft of water. The opening statement allows using non-owned water (mubah) for wudu, drinking, running a mill, and digging a canal as long as there is no harm to the public. For owned rivers, wells, and cisterns, the general public has the right to drink and water animals but not to irrigate land. However, if watering animals threatens the river's integrity (due to many cattle), the owner may prevent it. Water collected in containers (kuz, jabb) is owned and cannot be used without permission. The commentary then provides the legal proof: the well-known hadith 'Muslims are partners in three: water, pasture, and fire' (Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah) establishes common ownership of natural water sources. The reasoning is that rivers and wells are not meant for possession; mubah only becomes owned through active acquisition (ihraz). Since travelers need water for themselves and their animals, preventing them would cause severe hardship, which Sharia removes. In contrast, allowing irrigation of land would deplete the water and neutralize the owner's benefit. If theft of water occurs in a place where water is valuable (scarce) and the stolen amount reaches the nisab, the hadd of cutting the hand is not applied. This is because the general verse 'He created for you all that is on the earth' creates a doubt (shubha) that all things in the earth are mubah, so the hadd is dropped by doubt (shubha tatirru bi hudud). The objection that this would apply to all things is answered: the verse is not absolute; applying it absolutely would contradict the explicit verses and hadiths on cutting for robbery. Therefore, the cutting is only waived in the specific case of water, not all things. This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
مباح (mubah): Water from natural sources not owned by anyone
إحراز (ihraz): Physical possession or acquisition of mubah property
شبهة (shubha): A doubt or ambiguity that prevents application of hadd penalties

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the hadd of cutting the hand not applied when someone steals water that reaches the nisab according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because water is always mubah and cannot be owned.
B) Because of the doubt (shubha) raised by the verse 'He created for you all that is on the earth.'
C) Because the hadith 'Muslims are partners in three' proves water is common property.
D) Because water is not considered wealth.

Key Takeaway
Theft of water does not incur the hadd penalty in the Hanafi school due to the shubha arising from the general permissibility of water, even though the act itself is haram.

Fiscal Responsibility for Digging Rivers
Ruling on river digging costs and compulsion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on who bears the cost of digging a non-owned river versus an owned river, and explain when the imam can compel people to dig.

Core Idea
Public rivers from state; private rivers from owners; compulsion allowed to prevent harm.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a fundamental question in public works: who pays for digging rivers? The answer depends on ownership and public benefit. The Hanafi school distinguishes between non-owned (public) rivers, funded by the public treasury, and owned rivers, funded by the owners. If the treasury lacks funds, the imam may compel the people, but only the able-bodied dig, while the rich pay. This lesson unpacks these rules and the underlying principle of preventing harm (darar).

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of river
First, determine whether the river is non-owned (i.e., public watercourse) or owned (private or general). This classification is crucial because it determines the funding source.
Owned rivers are further categorised as private (partners <100) or public (≥100) based on preemption (shuf‘a) rights.
2
Determine the funding source
For a non-owned river, the cost is from the public treasury (bayt al-mal). For an owned river, the cost is upon the owners. If the owned river is private or public, the owners bear the cost, not the treasury.
The text clarifies: 'وكري ما هو مملوك على أهله' — digging what is owned is upon its owners.
3
Apply compulsion rules
If the treasury has no funds, the imam compels the people to dig the non-owned river, selecting those able to dig and levying the cost on the rich. For owned rivers, the imam compels the refuser absolutely to prevent harm, though there is a view that for private rivers, the case may be referred to a judge.
The effect of ‘Umar (رضي الله عنه) supports compulsion in life-threatening situations, but the text distinguishes between stored water and flowing water.
4
Understand the distinction between private and public owned rivers
The text uses entitlement to preemption (shuf‘a) as the criterion: if partners are less than a hundred, the river is private and preemption applies; if a hundred or more, it is public and preemption only for neighbors. This distinction affects compulsion and cost allocation.
Al-Zayla‘i suggests delegating the threshold to the mujtahids of each era.

من
The digging of a non-owned river is from the public treasury (bayt al-mal). If there is nothing in it, the people are compelled to dig it. The digging of what is owned is upon its owners, so the one who refuses is compelled to dig it. The cost of digging a shared river is upon them from its top. If it exceeds the land of a man... and other things, so it indicates that what is intended is other than what the specificities indicate, as in al-‘Inaya. [Takmila al-Bahr al-Raiq: 430/8] If someone withholds water while he fears thirst for himself or his animal, he may fight him with weapons due to the effect of ‘Umar. If it is stored in a container, he may not fight him with weapons, but he may fight him without weapons if there is surplus beyond the owner. In al-Kafi: it is said regarding a well and the like: it is better to fight without weapons. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 391/2] ‘Wa kari’: meaning digging, and it together with what is added to it is the subject. ‘From bayt al-mal, etc.’ because that is for the public interest, and the wealth of bayt al-mal is prepared for it, so the cost of digging is from it, and likewise the repair of its canals is from bayt al-mal. It is spent from the kharaj and jizya, not from ‘ushur and sadaqat, because the latter are for the poor, and the former for calamities. (Fath Allah al-Mu’in) ‘Umar... ‘He compels the people, etc.’: meaning if there is nothing in bayt al-mal, the imam compels the people to dig it because the imam is appointed as a supervisor, and leaving it causes great harm to the people, and the common people rarely agree on beneficial matters voluntarily. So he compels them, as it is narrated that he was compelled in such a matter, and they spoke to him, and he said: 'If you leave it, you would sell your children.' However, he selects for digging those who are able to dig among them, and places its cost upon the rich who cannot dig themselves. It is stated in al-Hidaya: If he wants to plaster the river out of fear of seepage, and there is great harm in it, he compels them to do so. [Takmila al-Bahr al-Raiq: 432/8] ‘Upon its owners’ because the benefit is specifically for them, so its cost is upon them, and because liability accompanies gain. Then the owned river may be private or public. The distinction between them is: what entitles its owner to preemption (shuf‘a) is private, and what does not entitle to preemption is public. If the partners in the river are less than a hundred, it is private and preemption is obligatory; if they are a hundred or more, the partnership is public and preemption is not obligatory for all, but only for the neighbor. Al-Itqani said this is the best what has been said about it, but al-Zayla‘i maintained that the more appropriate is to delegate the question of fewness or multiplicity to the opinion of the mujtahids in every age. And their digging, i.e., the private and public river, is upon their owners, not upon bayt al-mal. (Fath, Miskin) ‘So he compels the one who refuses, etc.’ absolutely, whether the owned river is private or public because of what we mentioned. And it is said: if the river is private, he is not compelled. The difference noted is: if it is public, there is repelling of general harm, so he compels the one who refuses, unlike the private, and in the private harm, it is possible to repel by raising the matter to the judge, who spends and then takes recourse from the refuser for his share. This is what Faqih Abu Ja‘far adopted, and it is like a crop between two partners where one refuses to spend, then the other may spend by order of the judge and take recourse for what he spent. So likewise this. (Miskin, Bahr) ‘From its top, etc.’ means the cost of digging a shared river is upon the partners from the top of the river. This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the digging is upon all of them from beginning to end according to the shares of drinking and land. Its example: ten people partnered in a river, and it needed - ...
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage from the same chapter discusses the financial responsibility for digging rivers. There are three scenarios. First, a non-owned river (i.e., public watercourse) is dug from the public treasury (bayt al-mal) because it benefits the general public. If the treasury lacks funds, the imam can compel the people to dig it, but only those able to dig; the cost is then levied on the wealthy who cannot dig themselves. An effect (athar) of ‘Umar is cited that if someone withholds water in a life-threatening situation, the needy may fight with weapons; but if the water is stored in a container and the owner has surplus, they may fight without weapons. This is based on the principle of necessity (darura). Second, an owned river (either private or public) is dug at the expense of its owners. The distinction between private and public owned rivers is based on entitlement to preemption (shuf‘a): if the partners are fewer than a hundred, it is private and preemption applies; if a hundred or more, it is public and preemption only for neighbors. There is a difference of opinion whether a private river owner can be compelled to dig: the relied-upon view is that he can be compelled absolutely to prevent harm, while another view says no, and the matter can be referred to a judge. Third, a shared river (between partners) is dug at their common expense. According to Abu Hanifa, the cost is from the top of the river downwards (i.e., the upstream part is dug by the shareholders, then the downstream). According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn), the cost is borne by all partners from beginning to end according to their respective shares of water and land. The text cuts off mid-example.

Key Terms
كري (kari): Digging of a river or canal
بيت المال (bayt al-mal): Public treasury of the Muslim state
شفعة (shuf‘a): Right of preemption in property

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if the public treasury has no funds to dig a non-owned river?
A) The river is left undug until funds become available.
B) The imam compels the people to dig, with the able-bodied digging and the rich paying.
C) The owners of adjacent land must dig at their own expense.
D) The imam must first sell public property to raise funds.

Key Takeaway
The responsibility for digging rivers is on the public treasury for non-owned rivers and on the owners for owned rivers, with compulsion allowed under certain conditions to prevent harm.
