Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Shared River Costs
Intra-Hanafi dispute on shared river digging costs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions on cost distribution for a shared river and identify the practical consequence of the ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: cost from top; Sahibayn: cost proportional to benefit from start to end.

When a river is shared among multiple partners, the cost of digging it must be allocated. The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement on how to distribute these costs. Imam Abu Hanifa ruled that the cost is borne from the top of the river downwards, while his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), held that the cost is divided among all partners proportionally from beginning to end based on their shares of water and land. This lesson analyses the dispute and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
We have a shared river (nahr mushtarak) between multiple partners. The river needs digging (cleaning or deepening).
The example given is ten partners in a river, but the text cuts off.
2
Present Abu Hanifa's view
Imam Abu Hanifa says the cost of digging is upon the partners from the top of the river (min a‘lahu). This implies that upstream partners bear the initial digging costs, and the cost is distributed as you go downstream.
The reason might be based on custom (urf) and ease of execution.
3
Present the Sahibayn's view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say the cost is upon all partners from the beginning to the end according to their shares of drinking water and land (bi hisas al-shurb wa al-aradi). This is proportional to benefit.
This is akin to a partnership where expenses are shared according to each partner's interest.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Abu Hanifa's view, a partner with land only downstream pays less for the initial digging but may pay more later if the digging extends to his section. Under the Sahibayn's view, everyone pays a share from the start, regardless of location.
The difference could affect how partners plan their finances.
5
Note the basis of each view
Abu Hanifa may rely on the custom that upstream sections are dug first and that benefit is realised gradually. The Sahibayn rely on general partnership principles where expenses follow benefit.
No explicit dalil is given in this passage; it is a qiyas-based ikhtilaf.

The digging of a non-owned river is from the public treasury (bayt al-mal). If there is nothing in it, the people are compelled to dig it. The digging of what is owned is upon its owners, so the one who refuses is compelled to dig it. The cost of digging a shared river is upon them from its top. If it exceeds the land of a man... and other things, so it indicates that what is intended is other than what the specificities indicate, as in al-‘Inaya. [Takmila al-Bahr al-Raiq: 430/8] If someone withholds water while he fears thirst for himself or his animal, he may fight him with weapons due to the effect of ‘Umar. If it is stored in a container, he may not fight him with weapons, but he may fight him without weapons if there is surplus beyond the owner. In al-Kafi: it is said regarding a well and the like: it is better to fight without weapons. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 391/2] ‘Wa kari’: meaning digging, and it together with what is added to it is the subject. ‘From bayt al-mal, etc.’ because that is for the public interest, and the wealth of bayt al-mal is prepared for it, so the cost of digging is from it, and likewise the repair of its canals is from bayt al-mal. It is spent from the kharaj and jizya, not from ‘ushur and sadaqat, because the latter are for the poor, and the former for calamities. (Fath Allah al-Mu’in) ‘Umar... ‘He compels the people, etc.’: meaning if there is nothing in bayt al-mal, the imam compels the people to dig it because the imam is appointed as a supervisor, and leaving it causes great harm to the people, and the common people rarely agree on beneficial matters voluntarily. So he compels them, as it is narrated that he was compelled in such a matter, and they spoke to him, and he said: 'If you leave it, you would sell your children.' However, he selects for digging those who are able to dig among them, and places its cost upon the rich who cannot dig themselves. It is stated in al-Hidaya: If he wants to plaster the river out of fear of seepage, and there is great harm in it, he compels them to do so. [Takmila al-Bahr al-Raiq: 432/8] ‘Upon its owners’ because the benefit is specifically for them, so its cost is upon them, and because liability accompanies gain. Then the owned river may be private or public. The distinction between them is: what entitles its owner to preemption (shuf‘a) is private, and what does not entitle to preemption is public. If the partners in the river are less than a hundred, it is private and preemption is obligatory; if they are a hundred or more, the partnership is public and preemption is not obligatory for all, but only for the neighbor. Al-Itqani said this is the best what has been said about it, but al-Zayla‘i maintained that the more appropriate is to delegate the question of fewness or multiplicity to the opinion of the mujtahids in every age. And their digging, i.e., the private and public river, is upon their owners, not upon bayt al-mal. (Fath, Miskin) ‘So he compels the one who refuses, etc.’ absolutely, whether the owned river is private or public because of what we mentioned. And it is said: if the river is private, he is not compelled. The difference noted is: if it is public, there is repelling of general harm, so he compels the one who refuses, unlike the private, and in the private harm, it is possible to repel by raising the matter to the judge, who spends and then takes recourse from the refuser for his share. This is what Faqih Abu Ja‘far adopted, and it is like a crop between two partners where one refuses to spend, then the other may spend by order of the judge and take recourse for what he spent. So likewise this. (Miskin, Bahr) ‘From its top, etc.’ means the cost of digging a shared river is upon the partners from the top of the river. This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the digging is upon all of them from beginning to end according to the shares of drinking and land. Its example: ten people partnered in a river, and it needed - ...

Commentary
This passage from the same chapter discusses the financial responsibility for digging rivers. There are three scenarios. First, a non-owned river (i.e., public watercourse) is dug from the public treasury (bayt al-mal) because it benefits the general public. If the treasury lacks funds, the imam can compel the people to dig it, but only those able to dig; the cost is then levied on the wealthy who cannot dig themselves. An effect (athar) of ‘Umar is cited that if someone withholds water in a life-threatening situation, the needy may fight with weapons; but if the water is stored in a container and the owner has surplus, they may fight without weapons. This is based on the principle of necessity (darura). Second, an owned river (either private or public) is dug at the expense of its owners. The distinction between private and public owned rivers is based on entitlement to preemption (shuf‘a): if the partners are fewer than a hundred, it is private and preemption applies; if a hundred or more, it is public and preemption only for neighbors. There is a difference of opinion whether a private river owner can be compelled to dig: the relied-upon view is that he can be compelled absolutely to prevent harm, while another view says no, and the matter can be referred to a judge. Third, a shared river (between partners) is dug at their common expense. According to Abu Hanifa, the cost is from the top of the river downwards (i.e., the upstream part is dug by the shareholders, then the downstream). According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn), the cost is borne by all partners from beginning to end according to their respective shares of water and land. The text cuts off mid-example.

Key Terms
النهر المشترك (al-nahr al-mushtarak): Shared river, owned in common by multiple partners.
حصة الشرب (hissat al-shurb): Share of water for drinking/irrigation.
أعلاه (a‘lahu): Its top; upstream part of the river.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, how is the cost of digging a shared river distributed among the partners?
A) Equally among all partners.
B) From the top of the river downwards.
C) Proportional to each partner's total landholding.
D) The imam pays from the public treasury.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has an internal difference on how to allocate the cost of digging a shared river: from the top (Abu Hanifa) or proportionally by shares (Sahibayn).

Allocation of Canal Maintenance Costs: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on apportioning canal cleaning costs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on allocating kari (canal cleaning costs) and explain the proofs and the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Cost is allocated by benefit: upstream pays least, downstream most—Imam's view; the Sahibayn split equally.

Shared infrastructure raises classic fiqh questions: who pays for maintenance? In a shared canal, all agree maintenance (karī) is wajib, but how to apportion costs? Imam Abu Hanifa proposes sequential allocation based on direct irrigation benefit, while his two eminent students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue for equal shares among all partners. This passage lays out both proofs and settles on the Imam's view as the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act and the dispute
The act is the maintenance (karī) of a shared canal. The dispute is not on obligation (wajib) but on how to allocate the cost among the partners who benefit.
Also note that Ahl al-Shiffah (those who only drink) are exempt from kari.
2
State Imam Abu Hanifa's position (sequential allocation)
Imam Abu Hanifa: cost is allocated from the top downward. The uppermost partner pays only for the portion paralleling his land, then is exempt. The next pays for his portion plus the remainder, and so on, so the lowest partner pays the most.
The first pays 1/10 of total, second 2/10, etc. (or fractions like 1/10, 1/9, 1/8… as per text).
3
State the Sahibayn's position (equal allocation)
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani: all partners share the maintenance equally, in tenths proportional to the number of partners, regardless of land position.
They argue that each partner benefits from the whole canal, including drainage.
4
Analyse the proofs for each view
Sahibayn: the upper needs the lower for drainage; if blocked, water overflows and damages crops, so all benefit from the entire canal. Imam: maintenance is only for those who directly irrigate; drainage benefit is incidental. He draws an analogy to roof water flowing onto a neighbour's property — the neighbour is not obligated to maintain that flow.
Imam's proof is a qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) based on the roof water case.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
The practical difference: under Imam's view, upstream farmers pay less; under Sahibayn, all pay equally. The mufta bihi is Imam Abu Hanifa's sequential allocation, as endorsed by later authorities (al-Kifayah, al-Khaniyah, al-Qahistani).
The author explicitly states 'al-fatwa 'ala qawl al-Imam'.

Source Text

He is free, and there is no maintenance cost on Ahl al-Shiffah. And the claim of water right without land is valid. A canal among people: that man from the kari? The canal up to the kari: according to both of them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the burden is placed on all partners equally in tenths. According to him (Abu Hanifa): he begins the kari from the top of the canal, and places on the first one-tenth of the maintenance of what parallels his land from the canal. When it passes his land, he is free from the kari. On the second, nine-tenths of the maintenance until it passes his land, and on the third an eighth, and so on. So the one at the bottom is the highest in expense, and the one before him is less, until the first is the least in expense. For them (the two): the owner of the upper part has a right in the lower part due to his need to let flow what remains of his water. If that is blocked, it overflows and damages his crop, so it becomes clear that all benefit from all. For this reason, they are equal in entitlement to pre-emption. For him (Abu Hanifa): the maintenance of the canal is on whoever benefits from the canal by irrigating land from it. When it passes his land, he has no benefit of irrigation, so no maintenance on him. His benefit from the lower part by letting the surplus water flow into it does not obligate him to maintenance. Do you not see that one who has the right to let the water of his roof flow onto his neighbor's roof is not obligated to anything for the maintenance of that place? And because he can prevent harm by blocking the mouth of the canal from above when he no longer needs it, so he does not need the kari from below. The fatwa is on the statement of the Imam as in al-Kifayah and others from al-Khaniyah and al-Qahistani. This is the summary of what is in the commentaries and others, and I have clarified the issue to make its understanding easy, so memorize it.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), regarding the allocation of maintenance costs (kari) for cleaning a shared canal. The author first states the ruling that there is no cost on Ahl al-Shiffah (those who only drink from the canal). The dispute is then detailed: according to the Sahibayn, all partners share the cost equally in tenths (i.e., proportionally by the number of partners, not by land size). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the cost is allocated sequentially from the top: the uppermost landowner pays only for the portion paralleling his land, then is exempt once the Kari passes his land. The next lower partner pays for his portion plus what remains, and so on, so the lowest partner pays the most. The proof for the Sahibayn is that each partner has a right in the entire canal because the upper needs the lower for drainage; thus all benefit equally. Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that maintenance is only for those who directly benefit from irrigation; once the canal passes one's land, he has no direct benefit, and the benefit of drainage does not obligate him. He supports this with the analogy of roof water flowing onto a neighbor's property—the neighbor is not obligated to maintain that flow. The fatwa is given on Imam Abu Hanifa's view, as stated in al-Kifayah, al-Khaniyah, and al-Qahistani.

Key Terms
الكَرِيّ (al-karī): The cost/expense of cleaning and digging a canal (commonly shared among beneficiaries).
أهل الشفة (Ahl al-Shiffah): People who only drink from the canal with their lips (i.e., use for personal drinking and livestock, not irrigation).
الشفعة (al-Shuf'ah): Pre-emption right: the right of a partner to purchase the share of another partner in a property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, when does the uppermost partner become free from the kari (maintenance cost)?
A) Immediately after the canal is dug.
B) When the cleaning reaches his land (i.e., the portion paralleling his land).
C) When the cleaning reaches the end of the canal.
D) Never; he always pays equally.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi is that canal maintenance costs are allocated sequentially based on direct benefit, not equally.

Maintenance of Canals: Private, Public, and Drainage
Commentary clarifying the dispute's scope and establishing drainage canal rule via qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cost sharing for canal maintenance, distinguish the three canal types, and apply the qiyas for drainage canals.

Core Idea
Cost sharing depends on benefit: drinking (lower pays more), drainage (upper pays more).

In this commentary passage, the author clarifies that the earlier dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn applies only to private canals. He then lays out the agreed rule for public canals and introduces a new scenario: drainage canals. Using qiyas, he reverses the cost allocation because the nature of benefit is opposite.

Guided Path
1
Classify the canal
Determine whether the canal is private (owned by partners), public (shared by villages), or drainage (for waste).
2
Apply private canal rule with dispute
For private canals, note the ikhtilaf: some say a partner is exempt once cleaning passes his land; the correct view (al-asahh) is that he must still pay.
This dispute only applies to private canals.
3
Apply public canal rule (agreed)
For public canals, when cleaning reaches the mouth of a village's canal, upstream villages are exempt by consensus.
4
Analyse the qiyas for drainage canals
The asl (drinking canal) has benefit decreasing downstream. The far' (drainage canal) has benefit increasing downstream because each property drains waste from its point to the end.
The illa is the direction of benefit.
5
Derive the drainage canal ruling
Since the uppermost property needs the entire canal for waste removal, it pays the most; the lowest pays the least.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برئ إلخ : قال في "الحقائق": الاختلاف بين الإمام وصاحبيه في النهر الخاص، وأما النهر العام الذي عليه قرى يشربون منه إذا اتفقوا على كريه فبلغوا فوهة نهر قرية يرفع عنهم مؤونة الكري اتفاقا، وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا احتاجوا إلى إصلاح حافتي النهر، وزعم بعض المشايخ أن الكري إذا انتهى إلى فوهة أرضه من النهر، فليس عليه شيء من المؤنة، والأصح أن عليه المؤونة، وأما نهر المساقط والأوساخ الذي يسقط فيه فائض الماء والكنيف الخارجة من الدور والأزقة كما في دمشق إذا احتاج إلى الكري، فهو على عكس نهر الشرب، فكلما وصلوا في الكري من أعلاه إلى دار رجل شارك من قبله؛ لأن حاجة كل واحد إلى تسييل أوساخه من داره إلى آخر النهر، فمن في الأعلى أكثرهم غرامة؛ لاحتياجه إلى جميع النهر، ودونه فيها من بعده إلى الآخر، فهو أقلهم غرامة. (ملا مسكين، فتح، رد المحتار)
The statement 'He is free, etc.' is commented on in al-Haqa'iq: The disagreement between the Imam and his two companions is concerning a private canal. As for a public canal by which villages drink, if they agree on its cleaning and reach the mouth of a village's canal, the maintenance cost is lifted from them by agreement. This disagreement applies if they need to repair the banks of the canal. Some mashayikh opined that when the cleaning reaches the mouth of his land from the canal, he has nothing of the maintenance. The most correct is that he must pay the maintenance. As for the canal of drains and filth in which surplus water and sewage from houses and alleys fall, as in Damascus, when it needs cleaning, it is the opposite of a drinking canal. Whenever they reach, in the cleaning from above, to a man's house, he shares with those before him, because each one needs to drain his filth from his house to the end of the canal. So the one at the top is the highest in expense, due to his need for the entire canal; the one after him is less, until the one at the bottom is the least in expense. (Mulla Miskin, Fath, Radd al-Muhtar).
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) that clarifies the scope of the previous dispute. It says that the disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn only applies to a private canal owned by specific partners. In a public canal used by many villages, when the cleaning reaches the mouth of a village's canal, the upstream villages are exempted by agreement. If repair of the banks is needed, the same dispute applies. Some scholars said a landowner is exempt once cleaning reaches his property line, but the correct view is that he still pays a share. The passage then discusses a drainage/sewage canal (nahr al-masaqit wa al-awsakh) as found in Damascus. Here, the rule is reversed: the highest house pays the most because they need the entire canal to drain their waste, while the lowest pays the least. This is because the benefit is continuous for the upper houses.

Key Terms
النهر الخاص (al-nahr al-khāṣṣ): A canal owned by specific individuals (partners), as opposed to a public canal.
النهر العام (al-nahr al-ʿāmm): A public canal shared by multiple villages or users.
نهر المساقط والأوساخ (nahr al-masāqiṭ wa al-awsākh): A drainage/sewage canal for waste water and filth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a drainage canal (nahr al-masaqit), who pays the most in maintenance costs?
A) The person at the top of the canal.
B) The person at the bottom of the canal.
C) All pay equally.
D) Only the municipality pays.

Key Takeaway
The allocation of canal maintenance costs depends on the type of canal and the benefit pattern; drainage canals invert the rule for drinking canals.

Exemption of Ahl al-Shiffah from Canal Maintenance Costs
Explanation of why Ahl al-Shiffah are exempt from kari

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal ruling for Ahl al-Shiffah regarding canal maintenance and explain the two reasons for their exemption.

Core Idea
Ahl al-Shiffah pay no maintenance costs because they are uncountable and secondary users.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq explains why those who only use a canal for drinking and personal needs are not required to contribute to its upkeep. The reasoning establishes a clear principle: costs fall on primary beneficiaries, not incidental users. Understanding this distinction prevents unfair imposition of costs and clarifies the limits of water rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State that Ahl al-Shiffah are exempt from kari (maintenance costs). The passage begins with 'wa la kari' (and no maintenance cost).
2
Understand the first reason
They are not countable (infinite potential users), so imposing a cost is impractical ('li-annahum la yuhsawn').
The text says 'all people of the world' could have this right.
3
Understand the second reason
They are followers (atba'), not principals; the primary purpose of canals is irrigation for landowners, so costs fall on the principals (usul).
The text: 'al-mu'nah tajibu 'ala al-usul duna al-atba'.
4
Note the consequence
Because they are followers, they also do not have pre-emption rights (shuf'ah) in the canal.
5
Define the term and rights
Ahl al-Shiffah have rights only to drink, water animals, and draw water with vessels; they cannot irrigate land.
The term 'al-shiffah' comes from 'shafah' (lip), indicating drinking from the lip.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا كري إلخ لأنهم لا يحصون؛ لأن أهل الدنيا كلهم لهم حق الشفة، ومؤونة الكري لا تجب على قوم لا يحصون؛ ولأن المراد من حفر الأنهار ونحوها سقي الأراضي وأهل الشفة أتباع والمؤونة تجب على الأصول دون الأتباع، ولهذا لا يستحقون به الشفعة والشفة واحد الشفاة وأصله شفهة سقطت الهاء تخفيفا، وأهل الشفة لهم حق الشرب بشفاههم وسقي دوابهم والاستقاء بالأواني دون سقي الأراضي.
And there is no maintenance cost, etc., because they are not countable; for all the people of the world have the right of drinking by lips, and the maintenance cost is not obligatory upon a group that is not countable. And because the purpose of digging canals and the like is to irrigate lands, and Ahl al-Shiffah are followers, and maintenance is obligatory upon principles, not followers. For this reason, they do not deserve pre-emption by it. And 'al-shaffah' is the singular of 'al-shifah', and its origin is 'shafah', the 'h' dropped for lightness. And Ahl al-Shiffah have the right to drink with their lips and to water their animals and to draw water with vessels, but not to irrigate lands.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage explains why Ahl al-Shiffah (those who only drink from the canal with their lips) are exempt from contributing to the maintenance (kari) of the canal. The reasons are: (1) They are an uncountable group; the whole world potentially has this right, so imposing a cost on them is impractical. (2) The primary purpose of canals is irrigation for agriculture; Ahl al-Shiffah are mere followers, and costs fall on the primary users (landowners), not followers. (3) By extension, they do not have the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) in the canal. The term 'al-shiffah' is defined: it is the singular of 'shifah', originally 'shafah' (lip), with the ha' dropped for ease. Their rights are limited to drinking, watering animals, and drawing water in vessels; they cannot use the water for irrigation.

Key Terms
أهل الشفة (Ahl al-Shiffah): People who have the right to drink from a canal with their lips (i.e., personal and livestock use, not irrigation).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Drinking from the canal with their lips.
B) Watering their animals.
C) Irrigating their agricultural land.
D) Drawing water with vessels.

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Shiffah are exempt from canal maintenance costs because they are uncountable and secondary users; only primary landowners bear the cost.

Validity of Claiming a Water Right Without Land
Ruling and proof that a claim of shurb is valid even without land ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on claiming a water right without land and explain why istihsan overrides qiyas.

Core Idea
A water right can be sued for independently of land because istihsan recognizes it as transferable property.

Can you sue to recover a water right if you don't own any land? Strict analogy (qiyas) says no, because the right is too ambiguous. But the Hanafi school, by juristic preference (istihsan), allows it. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind that preference and shows how it protects valuable rights that would otherwise be unenforceable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is bringing a lawsuit to claim a water right (shurb) without owning the underlying land. The Hanafi ruling: the claim is valid (sahih) by istihsan.
2
Contrast qiyas with istihsan
Qiyas would render the claim invalid because the water right is too unknown (jahalah) to inform the defendant and cannot be owned separately. But istihsan sets aside this analogy.
Understanding both sides is key to seeing why istihsan is needed.
3
Trace the basis of istihsan
The water right is desirable (marghub fihi) and can be owned independently through inheritance or bequest. It can even remain after the land is sold. This legal recognition makes it a proper subject of a claim.
The water right behaves like property even without land.
4
Apply the analogy with wine
The text compares it to wine: for Muslims, wine cannot be owned or claimed, but for dhimmis, it is property. Similarly, a water right is recognized property, so the claim is heard.
This shows that istihsan considers legal capacity.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The istihsan position is the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) in the madhhab, as confirmed by later authorities like al-Bahr al-Raiq.
Despite qiyas, the school chooses istihsan for practical justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويصح دعوى الشرب إلخ وهذا استحسان والقياس أن لا يصح؛ لأن شرط صحة الدعوى إعلام المدعى به هم الذين في الدعوى، والشهادة والشرب مجهول جهالة لا تقبل الإعلام، ولأنه يطلب من القاضي أن يقضي له بالمدعى به إذا ثبت دعواه بالبينة، والشرب لا يحتمل التمليك بدون الأرض، فلا يسمع القاضي فيه الدعوى والخصومة - = كالخمر في حق المسلمين، وجه الاستحسان أن الشرب مرغوب فيه، ويمكن أن يملكه بغير الأرض بالإرث والوصية، وقد تباع الأرض، ويبقى الشرب وحده، فإذا استولى عليه رجل ظلما كان له أن يرفع يده عنه بإثبات حقه بالبينة. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٣٣/٨]
And the claim of a water right is valid, etc. This is istihsan. The qiyas is that it is not valid, because a condition for the validity of a claim is to inform the defendant of what is claimed, and the witnessing and the water right are unknown with an ignorance that precludes information. And because he asks the judge to rule for him regarding what is claimed if he proves his claim with evidence, and the water right does not admit of ownership without land, so the judge does not hear the claim and litigation in it. Like wine in the right of Muslims. The basis of istihsan is that the water right is desirable, and it can be owned without land by inheritance and bequest. And land may be sold leaving the water right alone. So if a man usurps it unjustly, he (the owner) can remove him by establishing his right with evidence. Tkmilat al-Bahr al-Raiq 8/433.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of filing a lawsuit to claim a water right (shurb) without owning any land. According to strict analogy (qiyas), such a claim would not be heard because the water right is too ambiguous to be the subject of a claim. However, by istihsan (juristic preference), the claim is valid. The reason is that a water right is a valuable right that can be owned independently of land through inheritance or bequest. It can even persist after the land is sold. Therefore, if someone wrongfully seizes that right, the rightful owner can sue to recover it. The author compares it to wine: though wine is prohibited for Muslims, it can be owned by dhimmis, so a claim for it is heard. Istihsan overrides qiyas here because the water right is legally recognized as property.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference; abandoning qiyas for a more suitable ruling based on public interest or necessity.
الشرب (al-shurb): A water right, specifically the right to take water from a canal, stream, or well, often associated with land ownership but can be held separately.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the qiyas (analogy) ruling on claiming a water right without land?
A) It is valid by istihsan.
B) It is not valid because the water right is too ambiguous.
C) It is valid only if witnesses are present.
D) It is always invalid regardless of evidence.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a claim for a water right (shurb) without land is valid by istihsan, despite qiyas to the contrary.

Water Rights and Restrictions in a Shared Canal
Rulings on water apportionment and prohibitions on altering the canal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on how water is apportioned among partners in a shared canal, identify which alterations are prohibited, and explain the exception for non-harmful installations.

Core Idea
Water rights are proportional to land; altering the shared canal without consent is prohibited.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq, with commentary from later Hanafi masters, addresses a common rural dispute: partners in a shared water source arguing over their shares and modifications. The rulings establish clear principles to prevent harm and ensure fairness. You will learn the default division rule, the list of forbidden alterations, and the critical exception for harmless constructions on private land.

Guided Path
1
Apply the qiyas to road
Recognize that water rights differ from road rights because need varies by land size.
The asl: road ownership is equal; far': water shares proportional.
2
Identify prohibited alterations
List the actions that require consent: digging a canal, installing a waterwheel or daliya, building a bridge or qantarah, widening the mouth.
These are prohibited because they occupy shared property or take more water.
3
Recognize the exception for harmless installations
If a waterwheel does not harm the canal or decrease water, and is placed on the owner's private land, it is permitted.
The key is the absence of harm (darar).
4
Analyze the rules for damming (sakr)
One may dam the canal only to take one's share; prefer movable materials (plank) over mud to avoid choking.
If mutual agreement, different arrangements are possible.
5
Define the structures
Distinguish daliya (scoop on long beam), jisr (wooden bridge), qantarah (stone arch).
Understanding terminology avoids confusion in fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختصموا في الشرب، فهو بينهم على قدر أراضيهم وليس لأحدهم أن يشق منه نهرا، أو ينصب عليه رحى أو دالية أو جسرا أو يوسع فم النهر ، من النهر المشترك إلا برضاهم كالخمر في حق المسلمين، وجه الاستحسان أن الشرب مرغوب فيه، ويمكن أن يملكه بغير الأرض بالإرث والوصية، وقد تباع الأرض، ويبقى الشرب وحده، فإذا استولى عليه رجل ظلما كان له أن يرفع يده عنه بإثبات حقه بالبينة. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٣٣/٨] على قدر أراضيهم إلخ: لأن المقصود من الشرب سقي الأراضي والحاجة إلى ذلك تختلف بقلة الأراضي وكثرتها، والظاهر أن حق كل منهم من الشرب بقدر أرضه وبقدر حاجته، بخلاف الطريق إذا اختلف فيه الشركاء حيث يستوون في ملك رقبة الطريق، ولا يعتبر في ذلك سعة الدار وضيقها؛ لأن المقصود فيه الاستطراق، وهو لا يختلف باختلاف الدار، وليس لأحدهم أن يسكر النهر أي يسده على الأسفل، ولكنه يشرب بحصته، فإن تراضوا على أن الأعلى يسكره حتى يشرب بحصته، أو اصطلحوا على أن يسكر كل منهم في نوبته جاز، ولكن إن أمكنه أن يسكره بلوح أو باب فليس له أن يسكر بالطين والتراب؛ لأن النهر ينكبس به، وفيه إضرار بالشركاء إلا أن يتراضوا على ذلك، وإن لم يمكن باللوح فبالتراب وسكر النهر حبسه من حد دخل والسكر - بكسر السين - ما يسكر به الماء. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٩٢/٢] وليس لأحدهم أن يشق إلخ لأن في شق النهر ونصب الرحى كسر ضفة النهر المشترك، وشغل المشترك بالبناء بغير إذن الشركاء لا يجوز، إلا أن يكون الرحى لا تضر بالنهر ولا بالماء، ويكون موضعها في أرض صاحبها، فيجوز؛ لأن ما يحدثه من البناء في خالص ملكه، وبسبب الرحى لا ينقص الماء، والمانع من فعل ذلك الإضرار بالشركاء، و لم يوجد، وبالقنطرة والجسر إشغال الموضع المشترك بغير إذن الشركاء، فلا يجوز تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٣٤/٨] أو دالية إلخ : الدالية جذع طويل يركب تركيب مداق الأرز في رأسه مغرفة كبيرة ليسقي بها، وقيل: هو الدولاب والسانية للبعير يسقي عليها من البئر تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٣٤/٨] أو جسرا إلخ الجسر اسم لما يوضع ويرفع مما يكون متخذا من الألواح والخشب. والقنطرة: مما يتخذ من الآجر والحجر، ويكون موضوعا، ولا يرفع هكذا قال الشارح قلت: الجسر أعم من القنطرة؛ لأنه يكون من الخشب ومن التراب أيضا والقنطرة تكون من الحجر ونحوه، ولا يلزم الرفع في الجسر خصوصا إذا عمل من التراب. رمز الحقائق: ٣٩٣/٢] (تكملة) أو يوسع فم النهر: لأنه يكسر طرف النهر، ويزيد على مقدار حقه في أخذ الماء، وكذا إذا أراد أن يؤخر الكوة عن فم النهر بأن أراد أن يزيد في عرضه، ويؤخر الكوة عن فم النهر ، فجعلها أربعة أذرع عن فم النهر إلى أسفل، ليس له ذلك، وإن تصرف في خالص ملكه؛ لأنه يضر بأصحابه؛ لأنه يأخذ الماء أكثر من حظه. (فتح)
They disputed about the water right; it is among them according to the size of their lands. And none of them may dig a canal from it, or install a waterwheel or a 'daliyah' or a bridge, or widen the mouth of the canal from the shared canal, except with their consent. Like wine in the right of Muslims. The basis of istihsan is that the water right is desirable, and it can be owned without land by inheritance and bequest. And land may be sold leaving the water right alone. So if a man usurps it unjustly, he (the owner) can remove him by establishing his right with evidence. Tkmilat al-Bahr al-Raiq 8/433. 'According to the size of their lands etc.' because the purpose of the water right is to irrigate lands, and the need for that differs with the smallness or abundance of lands. And it is apparent that the right of each of them to the water is according to his land and according to his need. Unlike a road when partners differ, where they are equal in ownership of the road's land, and the width or narrowness of the house is not considered; because the purpose is passage, which does not differ with the difference of houses. And none of them may dam the canal, i.e., block it against the lower ones, but he may drink his share. If they mutually agree that the upper one may dam it so that he can drink his share, or they agree that each may dam in his turn, it is permissible. But if he can dam it with a plank or door, he may not dam with mud or earth, because the canal becomes choked, and that harms the partners, unless they agree to that. And if it is not possible with a plank, then with earth. And 'sakr al-nahr' is to block it from the point of inflow. And 'al-sakr' (with kasra) is what blocks the water. Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/392. And none of them may dig a canal etc., because in digging a canal or installing a waterwheel there is breaking the bank of the shared canal, and occupying the shared property with construction without permission of the partners is not permitted. Unless the waterwheel does not harm the canal or the water, and its place is in the owner's own land, then it is permitted; because what he constructs is in his pure ownership, and due to the waterwheel the water does not decrease, and the impediment to such action is harming the partners, and that is not present. As for the arch and bridge, they occupy the shared place without permission, so they are not permitted. Tkmilat al-Bahr al-Raiq 8/434. Or a 'daliyah' etc.: 'al-daliyah' is a long beam mounted like the pounding pole of rice, at its head a large scoop to draw water. It is said it is the waterwheel (dulab) and the 'saniyah' (well with a camel) used to draw water from the well. Tkmilat al-Bahr al-Raiq 8/434. Or a bridge etc.: 'al-jisr' is what is placed and raised, made of planks and wood. 'Al-qantarah' is made of brick and stone, is fixed, and not raised. Thus said the commentator. I say: 'al-jisr' is more general than 'al-qantarah' because it can be of wood or earth, while 'al-qantarah' is of stone etc., and raising is not necessary in a bridge (jisr), especially if made of earth. Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/393. Or widens the mouth of the canal: because he breaks the side of the canal and increases beyond his right in taking water. Similarly, if he wants to move the opening further from the mouth of the canal, i.e., he wants to increase its width and move the opening downstream, making it four cubits from the mouth, he does not have that, even if he acts in his pure ownership; because it harms his partners, since he takes more water than his share. (Fath).
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This long passage provides a comprehensive set of rulings on shared water rights (shurb). It begins with the fundamental principle: when partners dispute, the water is divided in proportion to their landholdings. Then it lists prohibited alterations to the shared canal: digging a new branch, installing a waterwheel (raha or daliyah), building a bridge or arch (qantarah), or widening the mouth. The reasoning is that these actions either break the canal bank, occupy common property, or allow one partner to take more than their share. However, if a waterwheel does not harm the canal or reduce water and is on the owner's private land, it is allowed. Damming (sakr) the canal is also restricted: one may only dam to take his share, but if possible, he should use a plank rather than mud to avoid choking. The passage also distinguishes water rights from road rights: in a road, partners own the land equally regardless of house size, but in water, shares are based on need (land size). The commentary at the end defines daliyah, jisr, and qantarah, and confirms that widening the mouth is disallowed even if on one's own land because it harms others by taking more water.

Key Terms
السكر (al-sakr): Damming or blocking a canal to prevent flow.
الدالية (al-dāliyah): A long beam with a scoop used to draw water (a type of waterwheel).
الجسر (al-jisr): A bridge, usually made of wood or earth, that is placed and can be raised.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the default rule for water distribution among partners in a shared canal?
A) Equal shares
B) Proportional to land size
C) First come first served
D) Determined by the eldest partner

Key Takeaway
Water rights in a shared canal are proportional to landholdings; any unilateral alteration that harms others is prohibited.

8 min
Water Rights in a Common Canal: Proportional Sharing and Prohibition of Unilateral Alteration
Ruling on water rights distribution and prohibition of unilateral alteration of common canal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on distribution of water rights in a common canal and identify the prohibitions on unilateral alteration, applying the principle of istihsan to justify the legal outcome.

Core Idea
Water rights are owned proportionally to land; no partner may alter the common canal without unanimous consent.

This lesson examines a ruling from Kanz al-Daqaiq regarding shared water rights (shurb) in a common canal. The passage establishes that water distribution is based on land size, reflecting the purpose of irrigation. It also prohibits any unilateral alteration of the canal, such as digging new branches or installing mills. The reasoning relies on istihsan (juristic preference), emphasizing that water rights are valuable separate property that can be reclaimed if wrongfully seized.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The passage states that water rights are divided among partners according to their landholdings. This proportional distribution is the key principle.
2
Recognize the prohibitions
No partner may dig a new canal, set up a mill, waterwheel, bridge, or widen the canal without the consent of all partners. List each prohibited act.
3
Understand the basis (istihsan)
The text justifies the prohibition via istihsan: water rights are desirable and can be owned separately from land (e.g., through inheritance). Thus, any wrongful seizure can be reclaimed by legal proof.
4
Distinguish from shared path
In a shared pathway, ownership is equal regardless of house size because the purpose (passage) does not vary with land. Water rights differ because irrigation need varies with land size.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, any unilateral alteration is haram. The affected partner can demand removal and establish his right by evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختصموا في الشرب، فهو بينهم على قدر أراضيهم وليس لأحدهم أن يشق منه نهرا، أو ينصب عليه رحى أو دالية أو جسرا أو يوسع فم النهر ، من النهر المشترك إلا برضاهم الأحد الشركاء في النهر كالخمر في حق المسلمين، وجه الاستحسان أن الشرب مرغوب فيه، ويمكن أن يملكه بغير الأرض بالإرث والوصية، وقد تباع الأرض، ويبقى الشرب وحده، فإذا استولى عليه رجل ظلما كان له أن يرفع يده عنه بإثبات حقه بالبينة. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٣٣/٨] على قدر أراضيهم إلخ: لأن المقصود من الشرب سقي الأراضي والحاجة إلى ذلك تختلف بقلة الأراضي وكثرتها، والظاهر أن حق كل منهم من الشرب بقدر أرضه وبقدر حاجته، بخلاف الطريق إذا اختلف فيه الشركاء حيث يستوون في ملك رقبة الطريق، ولا يعتبر في ذلك سعة الدار وضيقها؛ لأن المقصود فيه الاستطراق، وهو لا يختلف باختلاف الدار
They dispute over water rights, so it is among them according to the measure of their lands. And it is not for any one of them to dig from it a canal, or set up on it a mill, or a waterwheel, or a bridge, or widen the mouth of the river, from the common river, except with their consent. One of the partners in the river [is] like wine with respect to the rights of Muslims. The reason for istihsan is that water rights are desirable, and can be owned without the land by inheritance and bequest, and land may be sold while the water rights remain alone, so if a man unjustly seizes it, he can remove his hand by establishing his right with evidence. According to the measure of their lands etc.: because the purpose of water rights is irrigation of lands, and need for that differs with few lands and many, and the apparent is that the right of each of them from the water right is according to his land and according to his need, unlike the path when partners differ in it, where they are equal in ownership of the path's land, and spaciousness or narrowness of the house is not considered in that; because the purpose in it is passing through, and that does not differ with difference of the house.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with its proof (istihsan) regarding shared water rights (shurb) in a common canal. The basic principle is that water rights are distributed among partners in proportion to their landholdings. This is because the purpose is irrigation, and the need varies with land size, unlike a shared pathway where ownership is equal regardless of house size, since the purpose (passage) does not vary. The text then lists specific prohibitions: no partner may dig a new canal, install a mill, waterwheel, bridge, or widen the canal mouth without the consent of all partners. The reasoning (istihsan) is that water rights are valuable and can be owned separately from land (e.g., through inheritance or bequest). If someone wrongfully seizes another's water right, the owner can reclaim it through legal proof. The analogy with wine (khamr) may indicate that the water right in a common canal is a collective right that cannot be individually owned or transferred without consent, similar to the prohibition of wine for Muslims. The commentary further explains that the share is based on land size, not equal ownership like a public road.

Key Terms
الشرب (al-shurb): The right to use water for irrigation from a common source, typically a canal or river; a water share.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): A method of juristic preference, departing from qiyas for a stronger reason; here, used to justify the prohibition because water rights are valuable and can be reclaimed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for distributing water rights among partners in a common canal?
A) Equal shares for all partners
B) According to the size of their landholdings
C) According to the seniority of the partners
D) According to the need of each partner

Key Takeaway
Water rights in a common canal are distributed in proportion to land ownership, and no partner may unilaterally alter the canal without unanimous consent, as such acts are prohibited (haram) under Hanafi fiqh.

Prohibition of Damming the Common Canal Without Consent
Ruling on damming a common canal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on damming a common canal and identify the conditions that make it permissible or prohibited.

Core Idea
Damming a shared canal without consent is prohibited; only with agreement and using the least harmful method is it allowed.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, as explained in later Hanafi commentaries, addresses the right of partners in a common canal. While each partner may use water according to his share, unilaterally damming (sakr) the canal is prohibited because it harms others. The text then outlines exceptions based on consent and practical methods to minimize damage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
Recognize that damming (السكر) means blocking the river to prevent water from flowing downstream. The default ruling is that no individual partner may do this.
The term 'سكر' is used for damming, as in 'السكر ما يسكر به الماء'.
2
Recognize the exception of mutual consent
If all partners agree, or they agree to a schedule where each dams in his turn, it becomes permissible. The text says: 'فإن تراضوا... جاز'.
Consent overrides the prohibition because the right of each partner is waived.
3
Apply the principle of least harmful method
If damming is necessary, the least harmful material must be used. If a plank or door (لوح أو باب) suffices, clay and earth (طين وتراب) are prohibited because they compress the river and cause harm. If plank is impossible, earth is allowed.
The harm here is physical compression of the riverbed ('ينكبس' = compressed).
4
Note the overriding effect of consent
Even using clay and earth can become permissible if all partners consent to it. The text says: 'إلا أن يتراضوا على ذلك'.
Consent can permit even harmful methods, but the default is to avoid harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس لأحدهم أن يسكر النهر أي يسده على الأسفل، ولكنه يشرب بحصته، فإن تراضوا على أن الأعلى يسكره حتى يشرب بحصته، أو اصطلحوا على أن يسكر كل منهم في نوبته جاز، ولكن إن أمكنه أن يسكره بلوح أو باب فليس له أن يسكر بالطين والتراب؛ لأن النهر ينكبس به، وفيه إضرار بالشركاء إلا أن يتراضوا على ذلك، وإن لم يمكن باللوح فبالتراب وسكر النهر حبسه من حد دخل والسكر - بكسر السين - ما يسكر به الماء. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٩٢/٢] وليس لأحدهم أن يشق إلخ لأن في شق النهر ونصب الرحى كسر ضفة النهر المشترك، وشغل المشترك بالبناء بغير إذن الشركاء لا يجوز، إلا أن يكون الرحى لا تضر بالنهر ولا بالماء، ويكون موضعها في أرض صاحبها، فيجوز؛ لأن ما يحدثه من البناء في خالص ملكه، وبسبب الرحى لا ينقص الماء، والمانع من فعل ذلك الإضرار بالشركاء، و لم يوجد، وبالقنطرة والجسر إشغال الموضع المشترك بغير إذن الشركاء، فلا يجوز تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٣٤/٨] أو دالية إلخ : الدالية جذع طويل يركب تركيب مداق الأرز في رأسه مغرفة كبيرة ليسقي بها، وقيل: هو الدولاب والسانية للبعير يسقي عليها من البئر تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٣٤/٨] أو جسرا إلخ الجسر اسم لما يوضع ورفع مما يكون متخذا من الألواح والخشب. والقنطرة: مما يتخذ من الآجر والحجر، ويكون موضوعا، ولا يرفع هكذا قال الشارح قلت: الجسر أعم من القنطرة؛ لأنه يكون من الخشب ومن التراب أيضا والقنطرة تكون من الحجر ونحوه، ولا يلزم الرفع في الجسر خصوصا إذا عمل من التراب. رمز الحقائق: ٣٩٣/٢] (تكملة) أو يوسع فم النهر: لأنه يكسر طرف النهر، ويزيد على مقدار حقه في أخذ الماء، وكذا إذا أراد أن يؤخر الكوة عن فم النهر بأن أراد أن يزيد في عرضه، ويؤخر الكوة عن فم النهر ، فجعلها أربعة أذرع عن فم النهر إلى أسفل، ليس له ذلك، وإن تصرف في خالص ملكه؛ لأنه يضر بأصحابه؛ لأنه يأخذ الماء أكثر من حظه. (فتح)
And it is not for any one of them to dam the river, i.e. block it on the downstream, but he drinks according to his share. If they agree that the upstream one dams it so that he drinks his share, or they agree that each of them dams in his turn, it is permissible. But if he can dam it with a plank or door, he should not dam with clay and earth, because the river gets compressed, and there is harm to the partners unless they consent. If it is not possible with a plank, then with earth. And damming the river is confining it from where it enters. And as-sakr (with kasra on seen) is what dams water. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 2/392] And it is not for any one of them to dig a canal etc., because digging the canal and installing a mill breaks the bank of the common river, and occupying the common area with building without permission of the partners is not permissible, unless the mill does not harm the river and the water, and its place is in the owner's own land, then it is permissible; because what he builds is in his pure ownership, and due to the mill water does not decrease, and the prevention from such action is harming the partners, and that is not present. As for the arch and bridge, they occupy the common area without permission of the partners, so it is not permissible. [Tatimmah al-Bahr al-Raiq: 8/434] Or a waterwheel (daliyah) etc.: the daliyah is a long trunk assembled like a rice pounder with a large scoop at its top to water with; it is said it is the wheel and the bucket for the camel to draw water from the well. [Tatimmah al-Bahr al-Raiq: 8/434] Or a bridge (jisr) etc.: jisr is a name for what is placed and raised, made of planks and wood. And qantarah is made of brick and stone, and is placed and not raised. The commentator said: I say: jisr is more general than qantarah, because it can be made of wood and also earth, and qantarah is made of stone and the like, and removal is not necessary in jisr, especially if made of earth. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 2/393] (Additional) Or widening the mouth of the river: because it breaks the edge of the river, and increases beyond his measure in taking water. Similarly, if he wants to delay the opening (kuwa) from the mouth of the river, if he wants to increase its width and delay the opening from the mouth of the river, making it four cubits from the mouth downstream, he does not have that, even if he acts in his pure ownership, because it harms his partners, as he takes water more than his share. (Fath)
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage continues the rulings on shared water rights, specifically addressing various types of alterations and their conditions. First, damming the river (sakr al-nahr) is prohibited for an individual partner unless all consent or there is an agreement on rotation. Even with consent, if a plank or door can be used, clay and earth must be avoided because they compress the river and cause harm. Next, installing a mill (raha) is generally prohibited because it involves breaking the bank and occupying common space. However, an exception is made if the mill causes no harm to the river or water and is placed on the owner's own land, as it does not reduce water. Bridges and arches (qantara, jisr) are also prohibited because they occupy the common area without permission. The commentary then defines technical terms: daliyah is a waterwheel, jisr (bridge) is more general than qantarah (arch) and can be made of earth. Finally, widening the mouth of the river or delaying the opening (kuwa) is prohibited even if done on one's own land, because it allows the partner to take more than his rightful share, harming others.

Key Terms
السكر (al-sakr): Damming or blocking water; a structure that dams water.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the default Hanafi ruling on damming a common canal without the consent of all partners?
A) Makruh (disliked)
B) Mubah (permissible)
C) Haram (prohibited)
D) Wajib (obligatory) if needed for irrigation

Key Takeaway
Damming a common canal is prohibited unless all partners consent, and even then, the least harmful method must be used.

Prohibition of Other Canal Alterations: Mills, Bridges, and Widening
Rulings on mills, bridges, and widening a common canal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi rulings on installing mills, building bridges, and widening a common canal, and explain the conditions that allow them.

Core Idea
Alterations like mills and bridges are prohibited if they occupy common space or cause harm; only when harmless and on one's own land are they permitted.

The commentary continues with further prohibitions: digging a canal, installing a mill (raha), building an arch (qantarah) or bridge (jisr), and widening the canal mouth. Each is generally forbidden because it involves using common property without permission or causing harm to partners. However, the text provides a critical exception for mills if they cause no harm and are placed on the owner's land.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited acts
The text lists: digging a canal (شق نهر), installing a mill (نصب الرحى), building an arch (قنطرة) or bridge (جسر), and widening the mouth (توسيع فم النهر). Each is initially prohibited.
These are all acts that occupy or alter the common canal.
2
Understand the reasoning for prohibition
The reason is that these acts involve breaking the bank of the common canal (كسر ضفة النهر) or occupying common space with building without permission, which is not permissible.
The underlying principle: common property cannot be unilaterally altered.
3
Note the exception for a harmless mill on own land
A mill is allowed if two conditions are met: (1) it does not harm the river or the water, and (2) its location is on the owner's own land. In that case, it is permissible because the building is on private property, and water is not diminished.
The prohibition is linked to harm; when harm is absent, the act is allowed.
4
Apply the rule to bridges and widening
Bridges and arches occupy common space, so they are prohibited. Widening the canal mouth is prohibited even if done on one's own land because it allows taking more water than one's share, harming partners.
Widening is prohibited because it increases the right to water beyond the prescribed share.

Source Text

Key Terms
الدالية (al-daliyah): A waterwheel or long pole with a bucket for irrigation.
الرحى (al-raha): A mill, typically water-powered.
القنطرة (al-qantarah): An arch or vaulted structure, built of stone or brick.
الجسر (al-jisr): A bridge, more general than qantarah, can be of wood or earth.

The Legal Status of Water Rights (Shurb) in Hanafi Fiqh
Rulings on division, diversion, inheritance, bequest, and sale of water rights.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the sale, inheritance, and bequest of water rights (shurb) and evaluate the Balkh custom dispute.

Core Idea
Water rights can be inherited but not sold—they are not valuable property in the relied-upon Hanafi position.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a common agricultural issue: water distribution and transfer of water rights. The Hanafi school distinguishes sharply between rights that can be transferred by inheritance and those that can be sold. Understanding this distinction requires grasping the concepts of valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) and the role of custom. We will walk through the key rulings and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their rulings
List each transaction mentioned (inheritance, bequest, sale, gift, rental, charity) and note whether it is permitted or prohibited.
2
Locate the underlying principle
The text states water rights are not 'mal mutaqawwim' (valuable property) in the apparent narration. Examine what that means for each ruling.
Gharar and impossibility of possession are cited.
3
Distinguish between establishments of division
Note the difference between division by openings (kuwa) and division by days, and why the first cannot be changed after establishment.
4
Analyze the intra-madhhab dispute
Some Balkh scholars allowed sale based on custom. Understand the argument and the Hanafi response.
The mufta bihi (relied-upon position) is prohibition.
5
Apply to modern scenarios
Consider how these rules would apply today, e.g., water rights in irrigation districts.
The principles of share company and joint ownership remain relevant.

Or dividing by days, though division has already occurred by openings (kuwa), or diverting his water right to another land of his. That is, it is not for any one of them to... or to divert his water right to a land that has no water right in the river without their consent. And the water right is inherited, and bequest of usufruct is permitted for its entity, and it is not sold to the other land. That is, the water right. Or dividing by days etc.: meaning it is not for him to divide by days, while division has already occurred by openings; because the old is left as it is, due to the clarity of the right in it. And if each of them has a named opening in a private river, none of them can create an opening [؟], even if it does not harm the people, because the partnership is specific. Unlike if the openings are in the greater river, because each of them can dig a canal from it initially, so the opening is a fortiori. And if the upstream partner of two in a private river with openings between them wants to block some of them to prevent overflow onto his land, he does not have that, due to harming the other. (Fath Allah al-Mu'in) And 'it has occurred': the clause is a circumstantial clause, meaning while it has already occurred by openings etc. Al-kuwa (with kasra on kaf, and can be long or short) is the plural of kuwwa (with fatha), meaning a hole, and the kaf may be damma in singular and plural, and it is borrowed for water outlets to farms and streams, so it is said: the openings of the river. (Mulla Miskeen, Ayni) Or diverting his water right etc.: meaning it is not for anyone to divert his water right to another land that does not have a water right in the river, because if he does that, it is feared that he will claim a water right for it from this river as well, if time passes. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 2/393] And one deduces that from digging and running water into it. (Tatimmah) Without their consent etc.: related to all, meaning it is not for any of the partners these mentioned actions in the common river except with the consent of the partners, and they have the right to rescind after hiring, and their heirs after them, because this is a donation as verified by al-Zayla'i, and donations are not binding. (Fath with addition from the annotator) And the water right is inherited etc.: meaning inheritance applies to the water right, because it is a financial right, so inheritance applies to it. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 2/393] And because the heirs stand in place of the deceased, and they may stand in his place in what cannot be owned like compensations and donations like debt, retaliation, and wine, and likewise the water right. Tatimmah al-Bahr al-Raiq: 8/435] And bequest of usufruct is permitted etc.: because bequest is the sibling of inheritance, so it is like it. [Tatimmah al-Bahr al-Raiq: 8/435] And because ignorance of the bequeathed item does not prevent bequest, since bequest is one of the broadest contracts, as it is permitted for a non-existent and with a non-existent. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 2/393] And it is not sold nor gifted etc., similarly it is not rented nor given in charity, either due to gharar (uncertainty) or gross ignorance, or due to impossibility of possession, or due to lack of ownership at present, because it is not a valuable property in the apparent narration, and the fatwa is upon that. So if a person destroys another's water right by watering his land from another's water right, he is not liable according to the original narration. And some scholars of Balkh allowed its sale due to the practice of the people of Balkh, and analogy is abandoned for practice. We say: it is the practice of one town, and if a judgment is passed on the validity of its sale, it is enforced. Similarly, it is not valid as a dower in marriage, nor in =
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage covers multiple rulings regarding water rights (shurb) after a division has already been made. First, if the partners have already divided the water by openings (kuwa) in the canal, they cannot later change to a division by days, because the old arrangement is established and preserves the right. In a private creek, partners cannot create new openings even if harmless, because the partnership is specific; but in a public river, each can dig his own canal, so making an opening is even more permissible. Upstream partners cannot block openings to prevent overflow if it harms downstream partners. Second, a partner cannot divert his water share to another plot of land he owns that has no water right in the river, for fear that with time he might claim a prescriptive right. Third, inheritance of water rights is allowed because they are a financial right; heirs stand in the deceased's place. Fourth, bequest of usufruct of the water right is also allowed by analogy with inheritance. However, sale, gift, rental, or charity of water rights are not permitted in the relied-upon position (apparent narration) due to gharar, gross ignorance, impossibility of delivery, or lack of ownership. The water right is not considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim). Therefore, if someone destroys another's water right, he is not liable. Some Balkh scholars allowed sale based on custom, but that is limited to that locality.

Key Terms
الكوى (al-kuwa): Plural of kuwwa: openings or sluices in a canal that control water flow to individual fields; a method of distributing water shares.
المال المتقوم (al-mal al-mutaqawwim): Valuable property that is legally recognized as ownable and compensable; water rights are not considered such in the apparent narration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relied-upon ruling on selling water rights (shurb)?
A) Permissible based on custom
B) Not permissible due to gharar and lack of ownership
C) Permissible as a form of usufruct
D) Not permissible only if the water right is in a private canal

Key Takeaway
Water rights (shurb) can be inherited and bequeathed but not sold, gifted, rented, or given in charity in the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Liability for Damage from Customary Watering
Ruling on liability for irrigation damage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for liability in Hanafi tort law for indirect causes (mutasabbib) and apply it to scenarios of watering land.

Core Idea
No liability for damage from a lawful customary act; liability requires transgression (ta'addi).

When a person waters his land and water damages a neighbor's property, is he liable? Hanafi fiqh distinguishes between direct and indirect causation. This lesson examines the key condition of transgression and the role of customary practice in determining liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal category
Recognize that watering land is an indirect cause (mutasabbib), not a direct act (mubashir).
2
Distinguish mutasabbib from mubashir
A direct agent is liable regardless; an indirect cause is liable only if transgressive.
3
Identify the condition for liability: ta'addi
The passage states 'the condition of liability upon a cause is that it be transgressive.'
4
Apply the standard of customary practice (saqyan mu'tadan)
Customary watering (amount the land can bear) is not transgressive; excess watering is transgressive.
5
Note the nuance: watering within one's right (turn and share)
Shaykh Ismail al-Zahid adds that even customary watering done outside one's turn or with excess right is transgressive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم يضمن إلخ لأنه متسبب، وليس بمتعد، فلا يضمن؛ لأن شرط وجوب الضمان في السبب أن يكون متعديا، ألا ترى أن من حفر بئرا في أرضه لا يضمن ما عطب فيه، وإن حفر في الطريق يضمن، وإنما قلنا: إنه ليس بمتعد؛ لأن له أن يملأ أرضه ويسقيه قالوا: هذا إذا سقي أرضه سقيا معتادا بأن سقاها قدر ما تحتمله عادة، أما إذا سقاها سقيا لا تحتمله أرضه فيضمن وهو نظير ما قالوا لو أوقد نارا في داره فاحترق دار جاره، فإن كان أوقدها مثل العادة لم يضمن وإن كان بخلاف العادة يضمن وكان الشيخ إسماعيل الزاهد يقول: إنما لم يضمن بالسقي المعتاد إذا كان محقا فيه بأن سقى أرضه في نوبته مقدار حقه وأما إذا سقاها في غير نوبته زيادة على حقه، فيضمن لوجود التعدي تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٣٦/٨]
He is not liable – because he is a cause (mutasabbib) and not a transgressor (muta'addi), so he is not liable; for the condition of liability upon a cause is that it be transgressive. Do you not see that one who digs a well in his own land is not liable for what is damaged in it, but if he digs in the road, he is liable. We say: he is not a transgressor because he has the right to fill his land and water it. They said: This is if he waters his land with customary watering, giving it as much as it can normally bear. But if he waters it with watering that his land cannot bear, then he is liable. This is analogous to what they said: if one lights a fire in his house and it burns his neighbor's house, if he lit it as usual, he is not liable; if contrary to custom, he is liable. And Shaykh Ismail al-Zahid would say: He is not liable for customary watering only if he is rightful in it, by watering his land in his turn with the amount of his right. But if he waters it outside his turn, exceeding his right, he is liable due to transgression. (Completion of al-Bahr al-Raiq 8/436)
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with reasoning from the commentary of Kanz al-Daqaiq. It addresses the liability of a person who waters his land, and that water seeps into or floods a neighbor's land. The general principle is that a person is not liable for damage caused by a lawful act done in a customary manner. The act of watering is a cause (sabab), but liability attaches only if the cause is transgressive (muta'addi). Since he has the right to water his land, he is not a transgressor. However, this applies only to customary watering: giving the land as much water as it can normally absorb. If he overwater s, exceeding the land's capacity, he becomes a transgressor and is liable. An analogy is drawn with digging a well on one's own property versus a public road, and with lighting a fire. A further nuance from Shaykh Ismail al-Zahid: even customary watering does not excuse liability if he waters outside his turn or exceeds his water right, because that constitutes transgression.

Key Terms
متسبب (mutasabbib): One who is a cause of damage indirectly, as opposed to a direct agent (mubashir).
متعد (muta'addi): One who transgresses beyond his legal right or customary practice.
سقيا معتادا (saqyan mu'tadan): Customary watering, i.e., giving land the usual amount it can absorb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is the condition for liability when damage is caused by an indirect cause (mutasabbib) in Hanafi law?
A) The act caused damage.
B) The damage was foreseeable.
C) The act was transgressive.
D) The damage was substantial.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi tort law, a person is not liable for damage caused by an indirect act unless that act is transgressive (muta'addi); customary lawful use is not transgressive.

Liability for Water Damage: The Principle of Transgression
Explains when a landowner is liable for water seeping into neighbor's land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for liability of an indirect cause (mutasabbib) and apply the condition of transgression (ta'addi) to scenarios of water and fire damage.

Core Idea
An indirect cause is liable only if it transgresses – customary use incurs no liability.

You may think that if your actions damage a neighbor's property, you must always pay. But Hanafi fiqh distinguishes between direct action and indirect causation. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains when a landowner is liable for water seeping into a neighbor's land. The key is the concept of transgression (ta'addi). Let's explore the ruling and its analogies.

Guided Path
1
State the base ruling
Identify the basic ruling: a landowner who fills his land with water and it seeps or floods into a neighbor's land is not liable. Note the exceptions.
The ruling is 'la daman' (no liability) as a starting point.
2
Identify the legal principle
Extract the condition for liability on an indirect cause: the cause must be a transgressor (muta'addi). If there is no transgression, no liability.
The Arabic phrase 'shart wujub al-daman fi al-sabab an yakun muta'addiyan'.
3
Trace the analogy (qiyas)
Follow the qiyas: the text analogizes to digging a well in one's own land (no liability) vs. in the road (liability). Also the fire analogy. Understand the common illa: transgression.
The illa is 'adam al-ta'addi' (absence of transgression) in the original case.
4
Note the expansion by Shaykh Ismail al-Zahid
Shaykh Ismail adds that even customary watering must be within one's right (in his turn, to his rightful amount). If he waters outside his turn or beyond his right, he becomes liable.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; the mufta bihi follows the general rule without this condition.
5
Apply the ruling to modified scenarios
Practice applying: if a farmer irrigates normally, no liability. If he over-irrigates or waters at the wrong time, liability arises.
The key is to check for transgression (exceeding custom or right).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ملأ أرضه ماء، فنزت أرض جاره، أو غرقت لم يضمن لم يضمن إلخ لأنه متسبب، وليس بمتعد، فلا يضمن؛ لأن شرط وجوب الضمان في السبب أن يكون متعديا، ألا ترى أن من حفر بئرا في أرضه لا يضمن ما عطب فيه، وإن حفر في الطريق يضمن، وإنما قلنا: إنه ليس بمتعد؛ لأن له أن يملأ أرضه ويسقيه قالوا: هذا إذا سقي أرضه سقيا معتادا بأن سقاها قدر ما تحتمله عادة، أما إذا سقاها سقيا لا تحتمله أرضه فيضمن وهو نظير ما قالوا لو أوقد نارا في داره فاحترق دار جاره، فإن كان أوقدها مثل العادة لم يضمن وإن كان بخلاف العادة يضمن وكان الشيخ إسماعيل الزاهد يقول: إنما لم يضمن بالسقي المعتاد إذا كان محقا فيه بأن سقى أرضه في نوبته مقدار حقه وأما إذا سقاها في غير نوبته زيادة على حقه، فيضمن لوجود التعدي تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٣٦/٨
And if he fills his land with water, and it seeps into his neighbor's land, or it floods, he is not liable. He is not liable, etc., because he is a cause, not a transgressor, so he is not liable; for the condition of liability on a cause is that it be transgressing. Do you not see that if someone digs a well in his own land, he is not liable for what perishes in it, but if he digs in the road, he is liable. And we say that he is not a transgressor because he may fill his land and irrigate it. They said: This is when he irrigates his land in the customary manner, irrigating it to the extent it normally absorbs. But if he irrigates it to an extent it cannot normally absorb, then he is liable. And this is analogous to what they said: if someone lights a fire in his house and it burns his neighbor's house, if he lit it in the customary manner, he is not liable, but if contrary to custom, he is liable. And Shaykh Ismail al-Zahid used to say: He is not liable with customary irrigation only when he is rightful in it, by irrigating his land in his turn and to the extent of his right. But if he irrigates outside his turn and beyond his right, then he is liable due to the existence of transgression. (Completion of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/436)
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage discusses the liability of a landowner when water from his irrigation damages a neighbor's property. The ruling states that if he fills his land with water and it naturally seeps or floods into the neighbor's land, he is not liable. The reasoning is that he is merely a cause (mutasabbib) but not a transgressor (muta'addi). The principle is that liability for a cause requires transgression. Analogy: digging a well in one's own land does not create liability for accidents, but digging in a public road does. However, if the landowner irrigates excessively beyond the land's capacity, he becomes liable. Similarly, lighting a fire in the customary way in one's home does not make one liable for damage to a neighbor's house, but unusual fire does. Shaykh Ismail al-Zahid adds that even customary watering is only non-liable if done rightfully (in his turn and within his right); if he waters outside his turn or beyond his right, he becomes liable due to transgression. This is an expansion of the basic rule with detailed conditions.

Key Terms
متسبب (mutasabbib): One who causes something indirectly, without direct action or transgression.
متعمد (muta'ammid): Intentional transgressor; here used as muta'addi (transgressor) meaning one who exceeds bounds.
تعدي (ta'addi): Transgression: exceeding the legal limit or customary practice, resulting in liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is a landowner liable for water damage to a neighbor's land?
A) Whenever his water seeps into the neighbor's land
B) Only if he irrigates beyond the land's normal capacity or outside his rightful turn
C) Only if he intentionally floods the neighbor's land
D) He is never liable for water damage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi tort law, an indirect cause (mutasabbib) is liable only when there is transgression; customary and rightful actions do not create liability.
