Definition of Khamr and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Defines khamr and presents intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi definition of khamr from other intoxicants and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the condition of foam (qadhf bi al-zabad) for grape juice to become khamr.

Core Idea
Khamr is defined as fermented grape juice that throws foam; without foam, it is not yet khamr according to Abu Hanifa.

This lesson covers the opening of the Book of Beverages (Kitab al-Ashriba) from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author defines sharab as anything intoxicating and then focuses on khamr, the most severe intoxicant. A key intra-Hanafi debate arises: does grape juice become khamr as soon as it ferments and becomes strong, or only when it also throws foam? We will examine the proof arguments and practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and ruling
Read the definition of khamr given in the text: it is grape juice that ferments and throws foam (qadhf bi al-zabad). The ruling is that khamr is haram and carries a fixed punishment (hadd).
The text distinguishes khamr from other intoxicants which are also haram but may have different names.
2
Find the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa requires foam in addition to fermentation and strength. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) consider mere strength (ashidda) sufficient. Identify which position each imam holds and the evidence cited.
The three caliphs are said to support the Sahibayn's view.
3
Examine the proof for each position
For the Sahibayn: hadith that khamr is from dates and grapes, without mentioning foam, and the practice of the caliphs. For Abu Hanifa: the term khamr implies covering (mukhāmara), and foam is a clear sign; severe rulings require certainty.
The hadith 'every intoxicant is khamr' is interpreted metaphorically when applied to other drinks.
4
Determine the practical consequence and final tarjih
If a person drinks fermented grape juice without foam, Abu Hanifa does not apply the hadd punishment, while the Sahibayn do. The mufta bihi (relied-upon) position is that of the Sahibayn, as it is more cautious and supported by the caliphs.
The text indicates the Sahibayn's view is preferred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأشربة
تعريف الأشربة وأحكامها
كتاب الأشربة
الشراب ما يسكر والمحرم منها أربعة الخمر من الإسكار
الأشربة الأول جمع شراب ذلك
كتاب الأشربة إلخ ذكر الأشربة بعد الشرب؛ لأنهما شعبتا عرق واحد لفظا ومعنى، فاللفظي هو الشرب مصدر شرب، والعرق المعنوي هو معنى لفظ الشرب الذي هو مصدر شرب؛ فإن كلا منهما مشتق من المصدر، ولابد في الاشتقاق من التناسب بين المشتق والمشتق منه في اللفظ والمعنى، قال في "العناية": ومن محاسن ذكر الأشربة بيان حرمتها؛ إذ لا شبهة في حسن تحريم ما يزيل العقل الذي يحصل به معرفة شكر المنعم. [تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٣٧/٨] فإن قيل: لماذا حل للأمم السابقة مع احتياجهم إلى العقل؟ أجيب بأن السكر حرام في جميع الأديان، وحرم شرب القليل من الخمر علينا كرامة لنا من الله تعالى؛ لئلا يودي إلى المحظور بأن يدعو القليل إلى الكثير، ونحن مشهود لنا بالخيرية، فإن قيل: هلا حرمت علينا ابتداء، والداعي المذكور موجود؟ أجيب: إما بأن الشهادة بالخيرية لم تكن إذ ذاك، وإما لتدريج الضاري لئلا ينفر من الإسلام. الشراب إلخ الشراب في اللغة عبارة عن كل ما يشرب من المائعات حراما كان أو حلالا، وفي اصطلاح الفقهاء عما يسكر. أربعة إلخ: اعلم أن جميع ما يستخرج منه الأشربة أربعة: العنب والتمر والزبيب والحبوب كالحنطة والشعير والذرة، ثم للماء المستخرج منها حالتان نيء ومطبوخ والمطبوخ قد يطبخ حتى يبقى ثلثه، وقد يطبخ حتى يبقى ثلثاه، وقد يطبخ حتى يبقى نصفه، والحرام من الأشربة أيضا أربعة والحلال أيضا أربعة، كذا في "الدرر" ولكن تعقبه في "الشرنبلالية" بأن الحصر غير مسلم فإن الفواكه كالإجاص والشهد والألبان يتخذ منها الأشربة كما ذكره قاضي خان. (فتح) الخمر إلخ وهذا تعريف الخمر، وهو باتفاق أهل اللغة، وقال بعضهم: كل مسكر خمر؛ لما روي عن ابن عمر أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : كل مسكر خمر، وكل مسكر حرام ، رواه مسلم ،وآخرون، وعن النعمان بن بشير قال: قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: "إن من الحنطة خمرا، وإن من الشعير خمرا، ومن الزبيب خمرا، ومن التمر خمرا، ومن العسل خمرا"، رواه أبو داود والترمذي؛ ولأنها سميت خمرا لمخامرة العقل، وكل مسكر يخامر العقل، قلنا: الخمر حقيقة ما ذكرناه وغيره كل واحد له اسم مثل المثلث والباذق والطلا والمنصف والسكر ونحوها، وإطلاق الخمر عليها مجاز، وعليه يحمل الحديث ثم اشتراط القذف بالزبد عند أبي حنيفة وعندهما إذا اشتد صار خمرا، ولا يشترط فيه القذف بالزبد؛ لأنه سمي خمرا قبل القذف، وبه قالت الثلاثة، وله أن أحكام الخمر مقطوع بها، فلا تثبت بالشبهة، فإذا قذف بالزبد زالت شبهته والغليان والشدة شرط بالإجماع، وقيل: يؤخذ في حرمة الشرب بمجرد الاشتداد، وفي وجوب الحد على الشارب بقذف الزبد احتياطا رمز الحقائق: ٣٩٤/٢]
Book of Beverages. Definition of beverages and their rulings. Beverage (sharāb) is what intoxicates, and the prohibited ones are four: khamr from intoxication...
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, introducing the Book of Beverages. It first explains why beverages are discussed after fasting (because both are related to drinking), then defines sharāb linguistically and technically as anything intoxicating. The author lists four base ingredients for intoxicating drinks: grapes, dates, raisins, and grains. He then discusses the definition of khamr, citing a hadith that every intoxicant is khamr and prohibited, but argues that the term khamr is specifically applied to grape juice that has fermented and thrown foam (qadhf bi al-zabad). He presents the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn: Abu Hanifa requires the foam for the drink to become khamr, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider mere fermentation sufficient. The text notes that the Sahibayn's view is supported by the practice of the three caliphs, but Abu Hanifa argues that the severe rulings on khamr require certainty, which is only achieved with foam.

Key Terms
الخمر (al-khamr): In this text, khamr is defined as the juice of grapes that ferments and throws foam (qadhf bi al-zabad).
القذف بالزبد (al-qadhf bi al-zabad): The stage when fermented grape juice throws up foam; according to Abu Hanifa, this is a condition for the drink to become khamr.
المسكر (al-muskir): That which intoxicates; any substance that covers the intellect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the condition for grape juice to become khamr according to Abu Hanifa?
A) It must be cooked
B) It must ferment and throw foam (qadhf bi al-zabad)
C) It must be aged
D) It must be mixed with dates

Key Takeaway
Khamr is defined by fermentation and, according to Abu Hanifa, foam; the Sahibayn hold fermentation sufficient, and their view is more widely adopted—but all intoxicants are haram.

The Prohibition of Raw Fermented Grape Juice by Consensus
States the prohibition of raw fermented grape juice with foam, citing ijma and discussing hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on raw fermented grape juice that produces foam and explain why even a small amount is haram based on consensus (ijma).

Core Idea
Consensus (ijma) proves that fermented grape juice is haram in any quantity.

This passage presents the core ruling on raw grape juice that has fermented. The matn is clear: once it foams, both small and large amounts are forbidden. The author emphasizes that this ruling is not open to debate because it is established by the consensus of the scholars. We will also explore a unique implication: the hadd penalty is incurred for drinking even a small amount, because it leads to intoxication. The linguistic clarification of the word 'nei' is also touched upon.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the specific case? Raw (uncooked) grape juice that has fermented, become strong, and thrown foam. The ruling: both its small and large amounts are haram (prohibited).
2
Locate the proof
The author states 'This is by consensus' (وهذا بالإجماع). Consensus (ijma) is a primary source of law in Hanafi usul, and it gives definitive certainty (qat'i) to the ruling.
Ijma is used here as the proof, indicating no valid disagreement exists.
3
Understand the consequence for hadd
The text notes that the hadd (fixed punishment for drinking wine) is incurred even by drinking a small amount, unlike other beverages. The reason: a small amount of this wine leads to consuming a large amount (because of its intoxicating nature). This shows the severity of the prohibition.
This distinguishes it from other non-intoxicating beverages.
4
Note the linguistic clarification
The author discusses the correct pronunciation of 'al-nayyi' (النيء), noting that it is pronounced with a hamzah (like the word for raw meat). This is a minor point but helps avoid confusion.
The commentary cites 'Ayni and others on this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النيء من ماء العنب إذا غلا واشتد وقذف بالزبد، وحرم قليلها وكثيرها، رمی بالزبد وهو الرغوة أي الني من ماء العنب بفتحتين التي إلخ التي بكسر النون وتشديد الياء، قاله العيني، وتبعه في "الشرنبلالية": قال شيخنا: هو على غير المشهور كما في "المصباح"، ثم نقل عن عرمي زاده: أن النيء مهموز وزان حمل كل شيء يعالج بطبخ أو شي ولم ينضج. (فتح) إذا غلا من غلى يغلي غليا وغليانا بأن صار أسفله أعلاه وحرم إلخ: وهذا بالإجماع حتى أن وجوب الحد يتعلق بنفس الشرب؛ لأن قليله يدعو إلى كثيره ، بخلاف سائر المشروبات واختلفوا في إباحة إتلافها، فقيل: يباح، وقيل: لا إلا لغرض صحيح بأن كانت عند شريب خيف عليه الشرب، وأما إذا كانت عند صالح، فلا يباح؛ لأنه يخللها، والظاهر أن الاختلاف في إباحة إتلافها مفرع على الاختلاف في سقوط ماليتها، فمن قال: إنها مال، وهو الأصح؛ لأن الطباع تميل إليها، وتضن بها، قال: لا يباح إتلافها إلا لغرض صحيح كما سبق، ومن الناس من أنكر حرمة عينها، وزعم أن السكر حرام، وهذا باطل وكفر منه. (عيني، فتح، ملا مسكين)
The raw (uncooked) grape juice when it ferments, becomes strong, and throws foam – its small and large amounts are prohibited. 'Throws foam' means the froth. He said: 'The raw grape juice with two fatḥas...' This is by consensus, so much so that the hadd punishment is incurred by the very act of drinking, because a small amount leads to a large amount, unlike other beverages. They differed on the permissibility of destroying it: some say it is permissible, others say not except for a valid reason, such as if it is with a person who is likely to drink it... The apparent view is that the difference on destroying it is derived from the difference on whether it retains value. The one who says it has value (which is the more correct view because people desire it and are stingy with it) says it cannot be destroyed except for a valid reason as mentioned. And some people deny the prohibition of its essence, claiming that only intoxication is prohibited – this is false and disbelief.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn ruling on raw fermented grape juice (nei). The author first states the matn: when raw grape juice ferments, becomes strong, and throws foam, both its small and large amounts are haram. He then explains the linguistic point regarding the word 'nei'. The key point is that this ruling is by consensus (ijma) of the scholars, and even the hadd punishment for drinking is incurred by the act itself, unlike other intoxicants where a small amount might be excused. The reason given is that a small amount of such wine leads to consumption of a large amount. The author then discusses a secondary issue: whether this wine can be destroyed (e.g., poured out) without necessity. Some say it is always permissible to destroy it, while others say it is only permissible if there is a valid reason, such as fear that someone might drink it. The more correct view is that it is still considered property, so destroying it without reason is not allowed. The passage ends by refuting those who deny the inherent prohibition of wine, claiming that only intoxication is prohibited – calling such a view false and disbelief.

Key Terms
النيء (al-nayyi'): Raw, uncooked grape juice; in this context, it refers to juice that has not been heated.
القذف بالزبد (al-qadhf bi al-zabad): The stage when fermented grape juice throws up foam; indicates full fermentation.
الإجماع (al-ijma'): Consensus of the scholars that raw fermented grape juice with foam is haram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the consensus mentioned in the text, what is the ruling on drinking a small amount of fermented raw grape juice that has thrown foam?
A) Permissible if it is a small amount
B) Haram regardless of quantity
C) Makruh
D) Halal if cooked

Key Takeaway
Fermented raw grape juice that foams is haram in any amount by consensus of the scholars.

Destroying Khamr: Property Rights and Valid Reasons
Discusses the permissibility of destroying fermented grape juice and refutes a deviant opinion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ikhtilaf on whether fermented grape juice retains property value and determine the mufta bihi position on when it may be destroyed.

Core Idea
Fermented grape juice retains property value; destruction requires a valid reason.

This passage delves into a secondary but important issue: once grape juice has become haram due to fermentation, does it still have value as property? Can it be poured out? The author presents differing views within the Hanafi school and explains that the disagreement stems from whether the wine retains its malia (property value). The more correct view is that it does, so destroying it without a valid reason is not permitted. The passage concludes with a strong refutation of a deviant opinion that denies the inherent prohibition of wine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the secondary issue
After stating the prohibition, the author discusses the ruling on destroying (itlāf) the fermented juice. This is a practical issue: is it permissible to pour it out?
2
State the two positions
Some say it is always permissible to destroy it. Others say it is only permissible for a valid reason (gharad sahiḥ), such as fear that someone might drink it.
The second position is more qualified.
3
Understand the basis of the ikhtilaf
The difference hinges on whether the fermented juice is still considered property (mal) that has value. Those who say it retains value (which is the more correct view) argue that people desire it and are stingy with it, so destroying it without reason is not allowed.
This shows that ownership rights still apply to khamr in the Hanafi school.
4
Analyze the refutation of the deviant view
The author refutes those who claim that only intoxication is prohibited, not the substance itself. Such a view is labeled as false and disbelief, because it contradicts the consensus on the prohibition of khamr.
This emphasizes that the ruling is about the substance, not just the state of intoxication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النيء من ماء العنب إذا غلا واشتد وقذف بالزبد، وحرم قليلها وكثيرها، رمی بالزبد وهو الرغوة أي الني من ماء العنب بفتحتين التي إلخ التي بكسر النون وتشديد الياء، قاله العيني، وتبعه في "الشرنبلالية": قال شيخنا: هو على غير المشهور كما في "المصباح"، ثم نقل عن عرمي زاده: أن النيء مهموز وزان حمل كل شيء يعالج بطبخ أو شي ولم ينضج. Ruling on Tila' (Cooked Wine) and Sukar (Date Wine)
Defines tila' and sukar and states their rulings with disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two interpretations of tila', state the ruling for each, and apply the Hanafi ruling on sukar based on the condition of foaming.

Core Idea
Cooked wine (tila') is only halal when two-thirds have boiled off; date wine (sukar) is haram only when it foams.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq unpacks the rulings on two types of intoxicating beverages: tila' (grape juice cooked until some evaporation) and sukar (date wine). The author clarifies that tila' has two definitions, each leading to a different ruling, and discusses a dispute with Shuraik over sukar. By mastering these distinctions, you will be able to correctly classify modern fruit-based beverages according to Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Define tila' and identify its two interpretations
Locate the two definitions given in the text: (1) grape juice cooked until less than two-thirds is gone, (2) grape juice cooked until two-thirds is gone (al-muthallath). Note which definition is preferred by the author of al-Muhit.
2
State the Hanafi ruling for each interpretation
For definition (1), the ruling is haram if it intoxicates, based on the principle that anything intoxicating in large quantity is haram in small quantity. For definition (2), it is halal, as practiced by senior Companions.
The mufta bihi is the second interpretation.
3
Follow the dispute with Al-Awza'i
Al-Awza'i permits tila' because it is a pleasant drink and not khamr. The Hanafi response is that it resembles khamr in its intoxicating and addictive nature, thus it is prohibited to prevent corruption.
4
Define sukar and its ruling
Sukar is date wine. It is haram only when it throws foam (i.e., becomes intoxicating). Before foaming, it is halal. This is based on hadith and consensus.
5
Analyze the counter-argument of Shuraik
Shuraik argues that sukar is halal even when foaming, citing Quran 16:67 as a favor. The Hanafi response is that the verse refers to the pre-prohibition period; later texts abrogate it.
The Hanafi position is supported by stronger evidence (hadith and ijma).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والطلاء إلخ: أي النوع الثاني من الأنواع الأربعة الطلاء، وهو اسم لعصير العنب إن طبخ حتى ذهب أقل من ثلثيه، وقيل: إذا ذهب ثلثه فهو الطلاء، وإذا ذهب نصفه فهو المنصف، وإن طبخ أدنى طبخة ويسمى فهو الباذق، والكل حرام إذا غلا واشتد وقذف بالزبد على الاختلاف السابق في القذف بالزبد، وجه الحرمة: أنه رقيق مطرب يجتمع عليه الفساق، فيحرم شربه دفعا للفساد الناشي من شربه، وقال الأوزاعي: إنه مباح؛ لأنه مشروب طيب، وليس بخمر، وبه قال بعض المعتزلة ولنا أنه كالخمر ؛ لأنه رقيق ملذ مطوب يدعو قليله إلى كثيره، والتقييد بالطبخ باعتبار الغالب؛ لأنه لا فرق بين أن يذهب ما ذهب بحر الشمس أو حر النار. واعلم أن الطلاء بهذا التفسير هو الطلاء المذكور في "الجامع الصغير"، وقد بين حكمه أنه حرام، وقال صاحب "المحيط": الطلاء اسم للمثلث وهو ما إذا طبخ من ماء العنب حتى ذهب ثلثاه وبقي ثلثه وصار مسكرا، وتفسير الطلاء بهذا التفسير هو الصواب، وحكم هذا الطلاء الحل؛ لما روي أن كبار الصحابة كانوا يشربون من ما ذهب ثلثاه، وبقي ثلثه على ما يجيء عن قريب وإنما سمي طلاء ؛ لقول عمر الله ما أشبه هذا بطلاء البعير، وهو القطران الذي يطلى به البعير إذا كان به حرب فالحاصل أن الطلاء يفسر بتفسيرين، وحكمه على أحد التفسيرين الحرمة، وعلى الآخر الحل.
الطلاء
والسكر إلخ: أي النوع الثالث من الأشربة المحرمة السكر، وهو بفتحتين مشتق من سكرت الريح إذا سكنت، وإنما يحرم إذا قذف بالزبد، وقبله حلال، وقال شريك بن عبد الله: هو مباح وإن قذف بالزبد؛ لقوله تعالى: تَتَّخِذُونَ مِنْهُ سَكَرا وَرِزْقًا حَسَنام (النحل: ٦٧) امتن علينا به، والامتنان لا يتحقق بالمحرم، ولنا قوله : "الخمر من هاتين الشجرتين" الحديث، ولإجماع الصحابة والآية محمولة على الابتداء حين كانت الأشربة مباحة؛ =
And 'al-tila’’ etc.: meaning the second of the four types: tila’. It is the name for grape juice if it is cooked until less than two-thirds is gone. It is said: if one-third is gone, it is tila’; if half is gone, it is al-munsif; if it is cooked the slightest bit, it is called al-badhiq. All are prohibited if they ferment, become strong, and throw foam, according to the previous difference on throwing foam. The reason for prohibition: it is thin, exciting, and wicked people gather around it, so drinking it is prohibited to prevent the corruption arising from it. Al-Awza’i said: it is permissible because it is a pleasant drink and not khamr, and some Mu’tazila agreed. Our evidence: it is like khamr because it is thin, pleasurable, and its small amount leads to a large amount. The restriction to cooking is based on the usual case; there is no difference whether the evaporation is by sun heat or fire heat. Know that tila’ according to this interpretation is the tila’ mentioned in al-Jami’ al-Saghir, and its ruling is prohibition. The author of al-Muhit said: tila’ is the name for al-muthallath, which is when grape juice is cooked until two-thirds is gone and one-third remains, and it becomes intoxicating. This interpretation is the correct one, and the ruling of this tila’ is permissible, because it is narrated that senior Companions used to drink what had two-thirds gone and one-third remained. It is called tila’ because of ‘Umar’s saying: 'How similar this is to the tila’ of a camel,' which is the tar used for camels if they have mange. So the conclusion is that tila’ is interpreted in two ways: according to one interpretation it is prohibited, according to the other it is permissible. And 'al-sukr' etc.: meaning the third of the prohibited beverages: sukr (date wine). It is derived from 'sakarat al-rih' (the wind calmed). It is only prohibited if it throws foam; before that it is permissible. Shuraik ibn ‘Abdullah said: it is permissible even if it throws foam, because of the verse: 'You take from it intoxicating drink and goodly provision' (al-Nahl 16:67). He argued that a favor (imtinan) cannot involve something prohibited. Our evidence: the Prophet’s hadith 'Khamr is from these two trees,' and the consensus of the Companions. The verse is applied to the early period when beverages were permissible.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, specifically on the terms 'tila’' and 'sukr'. The author first explains that tila’ is the second type of prohibited beverage. He gives multiple interpretations: according to one view, tila’ is grape juice cooked until less than two-thirds is gone; according to another, it is when one-third is gone (al-muthallath). The first interpretation renders it haram if intoxicating, while the second interpretation (the correct one per al-Muhit) renders it halal, as senior Companions drank it. The author then discusses sukr (date wine), stating that it is haram only when it throws foam. He includes a dispute with Shuraik, who considered it halal based on Quran 16:67, but the Hanafi response is that the verse refers to a time before prohibition, and the hadith and consensus confirm its prohibition.

Key Terms
الطلاء (al-tila'): In this text, tila' is grape juice cooked until some part evaporates. Two interpretations: (1) haram if less than two-thirds gone; (2) halal (muthallath) if two-thirds gone.
السكر (al-sukr): Date wine (or similar) that intoxicates; it is haram when it throws foam.
المثلث (al-muthallath): Tila' that has been cooked until one-third remains; according to the correct view, it is halal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is sukar (date wine) prohibited?
A) As soon as it is prepared from dates.
B) Only when it throws foam (becomes intoxicating).
C) Only when consumed in large amounts.
D) Only when mixed with grape juice.

Key Takeaway
The ruling on tila' and sukar hinges on the presence of intoxication: tila' is halal only as muthallath, and sukar is haram only when foaming.

The Ruling on Muthallath: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi ruling dispute with evidence and tarjih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three positions of the Hanafi imams on muthallath, identify the mufta bihi, and explain the evidence and tarjih.

Core Idea
Muthallath is haram by fatwa, though Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf deem it halal; the later school follows Imam Muhammad's hadith-based prohibition.

Grape juice boiled to one-third (muthallath) generated a significant intra-Hanafi dispute. While the early masters Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf considered it permissible based on Companion practice, Imam Muhammad declared it absolutely haram. This lesson unpacks the evidence on both sides and reveals why the school ultimately settled on Muhammad's view as the operative ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the drink
Define muthallath as grape juice cooked until two-thirds evaporate. This is the fourth type of drink mentioned.
See the Arabic term 'المثلث العنبي'.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
They deem it halal even if it becomes strong, citing narrations from Companions like Abu Musa, Abu Darda, Umar, etc., and Ahmad's opinion.
They rely on specific reports that it does not intoxicate.
3
State the position of Muhammad
Muhammad declares it absolutely haram, using the general hadiths: 'Every intoxicant is khamr and every intoxicant is haram' and 'What intoxicates in large amounts, a small amount is haram'.
He applies the general rule without exception.
4
Identify the tarjih and mufta bihi
The text explicitly says 'al-fatwa 'ala qawl Muhammad'. Thus, the operative ruling is haram.
This is the weight of the later school.
5
Note the condition of intention
The dispute applies only when drinking for sustenance (qasada bihi al-taquwa), not for amusement. If drunk for fun, it is haram by consensus.
The author notes this condition should have been mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمثلث العنبي إلخ: أي النوع الرابع: المثلث العنبي، وهو ما طبخ من ماء العنب حتى يذهب ثلثاه، ويبقى الثلث، وهو حلال وإن غلى واشتد؛ لما روي عن أبي موسى الأشعري رضي الله عنه أنه كان يشرب من الطلاء ما ذهب ثلثاه وبقي الثلث رواه النسائي، وله مثله عن أبي الدرداء، وقال البخاري: رأى عمر وأبو عبيدة ومعاذ من شرب الطلاء على الثلث وشرب البراء، وأبو جحيفة على النصف، وقال أبو داود سألت أحمد عن شرب الطلاء إذا ذهب ثلثاه، وبقي ثلثه فقال: لا بأس به، قلت: إنهم يقولون: إنه يسكر، فقال: لا يسكر، ولو كان يسكر لما أحله. انتهى وهذا كله قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف، وقال محمد والثلاثة الأشربة الأربعة المذكورة حرام جميعها مطلقا قليلها وكثيرها؛ لقوله عليه السلام: كل مسكر خمر وكل مسكر حرام، رواه مسلم من رواية ابن عمر، وعن ابن عمر أنه عليه السلام قال: ما أسكر كثيره فقليله حرام، رواه أحمد وابن ماجه والدارقطني وصححه، والفتوى على قول محمد. وهذا الاختلاف فيما إذا قصد به التقوى دون التلهي وإن قصد به التلهي فهو حرام بالإجماع، ولهذا كان ينبغي للمصنف أن يذكر التقييد بقوله: بلا لهو وطرب بعد الرابع، ليكون قيدا للكل، وإذا كان مباحا عندهما، فلا يحد شاربه، وإن سكر منه، ولا يقع طلاق السكران منه بمنزلة النائم وذاهب العقل بالبنج ولبن الرماك، وعن محمد يحد إذا سكر منه، ويقع طلاقه إذا طلق امرأته وهو سكران منه كما في الأشربة المحرمة.
And the muthallath from grapes etc.: i.e., the fourth type: the muthallath from grapes, which is what is cooked from grape juice until two-thirds go away and one-third remains, and it is lawful even if it boils and becomes strong; because of what was narrated from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari that he used to drink talla' (boiled grape juice) from which two-thirds had gone and one-third remained, narrated by al-Nasa'i, and similar from Abu al-Darda', and al-Bukhari said: 'Umar, Abu 'Ubayda, and Mu'adh saw drinking talla' at one-third, and al-Bara' and Abu Juhaifa at half, and Abu Dawud said: I asked Ahmad about drinking talla' when two-thirds go and one-third remains, and he said: there is no problem with it. I said: They say it intoxicates. He said: It does not intoxicate, and if it intoxicated, he would not have made it lawful. End. And all this is the saying of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said: the three drinks (and the four mentioned) are all absolutely unlawful, their small amount and large amount; because of his saying (peace be upon him): Every intoxicant is khamr and every intoxicant is unlawful, narrated by Muslim from Ibn 'Umar, and from Ibn 'Umar that he (peace be upon him) said: That of which a large amount intoxicates, a small amount is unlawful, narrated by Ahmad, Ibn Majah, al-Daraqutni and he authenticated it. And the fatwa is on Muhammad's saying. And this difference is when the intention is piety without amusement; if the intention is amusement, it is unlawful by consensus. Hence it was incumbent on the author to mention the restriction with his saying: 'without amusement and frivolity' after the fourth, so that it is a restriction for all. And when it is permissible according to them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), then its drinker is not subject to the hadd punishment, even if he becomes intoxicated from it, and the divorce of one intoxicated from it does not occur, like a sleeping person and one who loses his mind due to banj (henbane) and mare's milk. And from Muhammad: he is subject to the hadd if he becomes intoxicated from it, and his divorce occurs if he divorces his wife while intoxicated from it, as in unlawful drinks.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling on muthallath (grape juice boiled to one-third). The author explains that the fourth type of drink is muthallath, which is halal according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, based on practices of the Companions and early scholars. However, Imam Muhammad considers it haram, relying on generalized hadiths that equate any intoxicant with khamr and declare small amounts of intoxicants unlawful. The fatwa is on Muhammad's view, making muthallath haram. The text then clarifies that this difference applies only when the drink is taken for sustenance, not for amusement; if for amusement, it is haram by consensus. Consequently, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, one who drinks muthallath does not receive the hadd punishment even if intoxicated, and his divorce is invalid, likened to a sleeping person. According to Muhammad, hadd applies if intoxicated, and divorce is valid. The commentary emphasizes that the operative ruling (mufta bihi) is Muhammad's position, thus muthallath is treated like other prohibited drinks in matters of hadd and divorce.

Key Terms
المثلث العنبي (al-muthallath al-‘inabi): Grape juice cooked until two-thirds evaporate, leaving one-third.
الطلاء (al-tila’): Boiled grape juice; often used synonymously with muthallath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the relied-upon position on muthallath in the Hanafi school?
A) Halal
B) Haram
C) Makruh
D) Mubah

Key Takeaway
Muthallath is considered haram in the Hanafi school for fatwa, even though early imams differed.

Consequences of Drinking Muthallath: Hadd and Divorce
Practical consequences of the intra-Hanafi dispute on muthallath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the differing consequences on hadd punishment and divorce validity when muthallath is consumed, according to the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf versus Muhammad.

Core Idea
According to the mufta bihi, drinking muthallath triggers hadd and valid divorce; according to the minority view, neither applies—a critical difference in legal practice.

The dispute over muthallath's permissibility has direct consequences on criminal and family law. If the drink is considered halal, its consumption does not incur the hadd punishment for intoxication, and any divorce uttered while intoxicated is invalid. But if it is deemed haram (as per the fatwa), the drinker is liable for hadd and his divorce becomes effective. This lesson explores these practical effects.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two rulings
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say halal; Muhammad says haram. The fatwa is on Muhammad.
Refer to Lesson 1 if needed.
2
Determine the consequence on hadd
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, since the drink is permissible, the drinker is not subject to hadd even if intoxicated. Muhammad holds that if intoxicated, hadd applies.
This is a direct application of the ruling.
3
Determine the consequence on divorce
Similarly, Muhammad states that if a man divorces his wife while intoxicated from muthallath, the divorce is valid. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf consider it invalid, like the divorce of a sleeping person.
The analogy: intoxication from a haram drink nullifies sanity, but from a halal drink it does not.
4
Note the consensus exception
If the drink is taken for amusement (talahhi), it is haram by consensus, so the stricter consequences would apply according to all.
The author criticizes the author for not mentioning this restriction explicitly.
5
Summarize the practical difference
The thamarah al-khilaf is clear: one view leads to criminal liability and marital consequences, the other exempts.
This highlights the importance of the ruling on the drink itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمثلث العنبي إلخ: أي النوع الرابع: المثلث العنبي، وهو ما طبخ من ماء العنب حتى يذهب ثلثاه، ويبقى الثلث، وهو حلال وإن غلى واشتد؛ لما روي عن أبي موسى الأشعري رضي الله عنه أنه كان يشرب من الطلاء ما ذهب ثلثاه وبقي الثلث رواه النسائي، وله مثله عن أبي الدرداء، وقال البخاري: رأى عمر وأبو عبيدة ومعاذ من شرب الطلاء على الثلث وشرب البراء، وأبو جحيفة على النصف، وقال أبو داود سألت أحمد عن شرب الطلاء إذا ذهب ثلثاه، وبقي ثلثه فقال: لا بأس به، قلت: إنهم يقولون: إنه يسكر، فقال: لا يسكر، ولو كان يسكر لما أحله. انتهى وهذا كله قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف، وقال محمد والثلاثة الأشربة الأربعة المذكورة حرام جميعها مطلقا قليلها وكثيرها؛ لقوله عليه السلام: كل مسكر خمر وكل مسكر حرام، رواه مسلم من رواية ابن عمر، وعن ابن عمر أنه عليه السلام قال: ما أسكر كثيره فقليله حرام، رواه أحمد وابن ماجه والدارقطني وصححه، والفتوى على قول محمد. وهذا الاختلاف فيما إذا قصد به التقوى دون التلهي وإن قصد به التلهي فهو حرام بالإجماع، ولهذا كان ينبغي للمصنف أن يذكر التقييد بقوله: بلا لهو وطرب بعد الرابع، ليكون قيدا للكل، وإذا كان مباحا عندهما، فلا يحد شاربه، وإن سكر منه، ولا يقع طلاق السكران منه بمنزلة النائم وذاهب العقل بالبنج ولبن الرماك، وعن محمد يحد إذا سكر منه، ويقع طلاقه إذا طلق امرأته وهو سكران منه كما في الأشربة المحرمة.

Ruling on vinegar of wine (khal al-khamr)
States that vinegar of wine is halal whether made actively or spontaneously

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on vinegar of wine and explain why it differs from the Shafi'i school, using the evidence of transformation (istihala) and general Quranic permission.

Core Idea
Vinegar from wine is halal because the substance has transformed into a good thing, and the general permission of good things applies.

This lesson examines a well-known dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding vinegar made from wine. While the Shafi'i school restricts its permissibility based on a hadith, the Hanafi school declares it absolutely halal, relying on the principle of istihala (transformation) and the Quranic verse permitting good things. Understanding this dispute helps clarify how the Hanafi school applies the concept of substantial change to purify impure substances.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling: vinegar of wine is halal whether it is made by adding a substance or turns spontaneously.
The Hanafi position is absolute: no distinction between intentional and natural conversion.
2
Identify the opponent's view
The Shafi'i school holds that if wine is intentionally made into vinegar, it remains haram; if it turns naturally, there are two opinions.
The Shafi'i view is more restrictive, differentiating based on human intervention.
3
Examine the Shafi'i proof
The Shafi'i rely on the hadith narrated by Anas: the Prophet said 'No' when asked about wine being made into vinegar.
This hadith is recorded in Muslim.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis interpret the prohibition as referring to using wine as a condiment (like vinegar), not to the process of making vinegar. They cite the Quranic verse (5:4) and the hadith 'Excellent is vinegar as a condiment' which is general.
The Hanafi response reconciles the two hadiths by specifying the scope of the prohibition.
5
Determine the practical difference
Hanafis may consume any vinegar from wine; Shafi'is may avoid intentionally made vinegar and differ on spontaneous vinegar.
This dispute has real-world implications for food consumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخل الخمر إلخ: أي حل خل الخمر سواء خللت بإلقاء شيء فيها كالملح ونحوه، أو تخللت بنفسها من غير علاج، وقال الشافعي: إن خللت لا تحل قولا واحدا، وإن تخللت بالنقل من الظل إلى الشمس أو بالعكس، أو بإيقاد النار بالقرب منها، ففيه قولان لما روي عن أنس رضي الله عنه أنه عليه السلام سئل عن الخمر يتخذ خلا، فقال: لا، رواه مسلم، ولنا: قوله تعالى: ﴿أُحِلَّ لَكُمُ الطَّيِّبَاتُ﴾ (المائدة: 4) وقد تغيرت العين والخل طيب بالطبع فيحل، وفي التخليل إزالة الوصف المفسد وإثبات صفة الصلاح، وقوله عليه السلام: نعم الإدام الخل، رواه مسلم، وهو مطلق، فيتناول جميع صورها والمراد بالنهي أن يستعمل الخمر استعمال الخل بأن ينتفع بها انتفاعه كالائتدام وغيره.
And vinegar of wine etc.: i.e., the permissibility of vinegar of wine, whether it is made vinegar by throwing something into it like salt or the like, or it becomes vinegar by itself without treatment. And al-Shafi'i said: If it is made vinegar, it is not permissible with one opinion, and if it becomes vinegar by transfer from shade to sun or vice versa, or by lighting fire near it, then there are two opinions; because of what was narrated from Anas that the Prophet was asked about wine being made into vinegar, and he said: No, narrated by Muslim. And our evidence: the saying of Allah: 'Lawful for you are good things' (al-Ma'idah: 4), and the substance has changed, and vinegar is naturally good, so it becomes permissible. And in making vinegar there is removal of the corrupting quality and establishment of the quality of soundness. And his saying (peace be upon him): 'Excellent is vinegar as a condiment', narrated by Muslim, and it is absolute, so it includes all forms. And the intended meaning of the prohibition is that wine is used in the manner of vinegar, that one benefits from it as one benefits from vinegar, such as using it as a condiment and other uses.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding vinegar of wine (khal al-khamr). The Hanafi position is that it is absolutely halal, whether the vinegar is produced by adding a substance like salt, or it turns spontaneously. The Shafi'i school holds that if it is intentionally made into vinegar, it is not permissible (according to one opinion), and if it turns on its own due to environmental factors like sun or fire, there are two opinions (some consider it permissible, others not). The Hanafi proof includes the Quranic verse about good things, the change of the substance from wine to vinegar (which is a transformation from a corrupt to a sound state), and the hadith praising vinegar as a condiment (which is general and covers all types of vinegar). The apparent prohibition in the hadith of Anas is interpreted by Hanafis to mean using wine as a condiment (as if it were vinegar), not the process of making vinegar itself. The commentary follows the author's order: first the ruling, then the Shafi'i view, then the Hanafi proofs, and lastly the response to the opposing evidence.

Key Terms
خل الخمر (khal al-khamr): Vinegar that results from wine, whether through intentional conversion or natural process.
التخليل (al-takhlil): The process of turning wine into vinegar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is vinegar made from wine (khal al-khamr) permissible if the wine is intentionally turned into vinegar by adding salt?
A) No, it is haram because the wine was initially impure.
B) Yes, it is halal because the substance has changed and become a good thing.
C) It is permissible only if the wine turns to vinegar on its own without intervention.
D) It is makruh.

Key Takeaway
Vinegar from wine is halal in the Hanafi school due to istihala and the general permission of good things.

6 min
Dregs of Wine: Prohibition and Hadd
Explains the ruling on dregs of wine as makruh meaning haram, and lack of hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on dregs of wine, explain why it is haram (makruh tahrimi), and distinguish when the hadd punishment applies.

Core Idea
Dregs of wine are haram, but hadd requires intoxication—people detest dregs so deterrence is not served.

This lesson examines the ruling on dirdi al-khamr (dregs of wine). The text clarifies that although dregs are prohibited (haram), the hadd punishment is not applied unless the drinker becomes intoxicated. This distinction arises from the purpose of hadd: deterrence against what natures incline to. We will explore the legal reasoning behind this nuanced position, including the inter-madhhab difference with the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its level
The text says 'kariha' but clarifies it means haram (makruh tahrimi) because dregs contain parts of wine. Note the use of 'karahah' due to lack of qat'i evidence.
Prohibition is established but not with the strongest evidence category.
2
Examine the applications of prohibition
List the prohibited uses: treating wounds, giving to dhimmis/children/animals. Combing hair is specified because it beautifies, except for medical necessity with no permissible alternative.
This shows the strictness of prohibition extends to all forms of benefit.
3
Analyze the hadd dispute
Hanafi: no hadd unless intoxication. Shafi'i: hadd even without intoxication. Identify each school's dalil from the text.
The dispute centers on whether hadd attaches to the substance or the act of intoxicating.
4
Understand the Hanafi talil
Hadd is for deterrence (zajar); people detest dregs so it resembles non-wine drinks. Also, sediment is predominant, making it akin to eating rather than drinking, so the wine is considered mustahlak (consumed).
This reasoning restricts hadd to cases where the purpose is fulfilled.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: a Hanafi judge requires proof of intoxication for hadd; a Shafi'i judge applies hadd upon mere consumption of dregs.
The ruling affects both judicial practice and personal fiqh understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و كره دردي الخمر: لأن فيه أجزاء الخمر، فكان حراما نجسا والانتفاع بمثله حرام، ولهذا لا يجوز أن يداوي به جرحا، ولا أن يسقي ذميا ولا صبيا والوبال على من سقاه، وكذا لا يسقيه الدواب. وأراد بالكراهة الحرمة، وعبر بــ'كره'؛ لعدم القاطع. والامتشاط به خص الامتشاط؛ لأن له تأثيرا في تحسين الشعر، إلا إذا كان على وجه التداوي، فإنه يجوز حيث لم يوجد من المباح ما يقوم مقامه. ولا يحد شاربه إلخ أي شرب الدردي مكروه وحرام، لكن لا يحد شاربه إلا إذا سكر، وقال الشافعي يحد بلا سكر؛ لأن الحد يجب بشرب قطرة من الخمر، وفي الدردي قطرات منها، ولنا أن وجوب الحد للزجر، والزجر يشرع فيما تميل إليه الطباع والطباع لا تميل إلى شرب الدردي، بل تعافه، فأشبه غير الخمر من الأشربة، ولأن الثفل غالب، فأشبه غالب الماء، فيصير الخمر كالمستهلك به، وإذا كانت الغلبة للثفل كان هذا أكلا لا شربا، فلا يرد عليه ما قيل: إن حرمة قليل الخمر قطعية.
And he disliked (makruh) dregs of wine: because it contains parts of wine, so it is unlawful and impure, and benefiting from it is unlawful, and therefore it is not permissible to treat a wound with it, nor to give it to a dhimmi nor a child, and the sin is on the one who gives it, and likewise one should not give it to animals. And he intended by 'karahah' (dislike) the meaning of prohibition (hurma), and he expressed it as 'kariha' due to lack of decisive evidence. And combing (the hair) with it is specifically mentioned because it has an effect in beautifying hair, except if it is for the purpose of treatment, then it is permissible when there is no permissible alternative available. And its drinker is not subjected to the hadd punishment, etc., i.e., drinking dregs is disliked and prohibited, but its drinker is not subjected to hadd unless he becomes intoxicated. And al-Shafi'i said: He is subjected to hadd even without intoxication, because the hadd is obligatory for drinking a drop of wine, and in dregs there are drops of it. And our evidence: the obligation of hadd is for deterrence, and deterrence is legislated for what natures incline to, and natures do not incline to drinking dregs; rather they detest it, so it resembles non-wine drinks. And because the sediment is predominant, it resembles predominant water, so the wine becomes like consumed (mustahlak) within it, and when the sediment predominates, this is eating, not drinking, so the objection that 'the prohibition of a small amount of wine is definitive' does not apply.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on dirdi al-khamr (dregs of wine). The author states it is makruh, but clarifies that the term 'makruh' here means haram (prohibited), not merely disliked; the author uses 'kariha' because the evidence is not categorical (qat'i). The prohibition is due to the wine content. Consequently, it cannot be used for medical treatment, nor given to non-Muslims, children, or animals. However, combing hair with it is specifically mentioned as prohibited because it beautifies, but if used for medicinal purpose and no permissible alternative exists, it is allowed. Regarding the hadd punishment: drinking dregs is haram, but the drinker is not subjected to the hadd punishment unless he becomes intoxicated. This is because the hadd is for deterrence, and people's nature does not incline to drinking dregs; rather they detest it, so it is treated like other non-wine drinks. Additionally, since the sediment (thifl) is predominant, the wine becomes 'consumed' in it, making it more like eating than drinking. The Shafi'i opinion is also mentioned: they impose hadd even without intoxication because dregs contain drops of wine, and a drop of wine triggers hadd. The Hanafi response is given as above. The commentary follows the author's order: first the ruling, then the explanation of prohibition and its applications, then the issue of hadd and the inter-madhhab difference.

Key Terms
دردي الخمر (dirdi al-khamr): The dregs or sediment at the bottom of wine.
الكراهة (al-karahah): In this context, it means prohibition (hurma), not mere dislike.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is a person liable for hadd punishment if they drink dregs of wine (dirdi al-khamr) without becoming intoxicated?
A) Yes, because dregs contain wine.
B) No, because hadd requires intoxication; dregs are not consumed with inclination.
C) Yes, because any amount of wine calls for hadd.
D) No, because dregs are not considered wine at all.

Key Takeaway
Dregs of wine are haram, but the hadd punishment only applies if the drinker becomes intoxicated, because the deterrence purpose is not served by a substance people naturally detest.

Dregs of Wine: Prohibition and Hadd
Expansive ruling on dregs of wine with inter-madhhab dispute on hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on dregs of wine and explain why the drinker is not flogged without intoxication.

Core Idea
Dregs of wine are impure and prohibited, but the hadd penalty only applies if intoxication results.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on the dregs (sediment) of wine. Although the text says 'disliked', the commentator clarifies it is actually prohibited due to the presence of wine parts. We will also explore the important distinction in punishment: the fixed hadd flogging is only applied when the drinker becomes intoxicated, contrasting with the Shafi'i position that any drinking of wine triggers the hadd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is consuming the dregs of wine (dardi al-khamr). The ruling is expressed as 'karih' but means prohibition (haram) due to impurity.
The term 'karih' here is makruh tahrimi, indicating prohibition without qat'i evidence.
2
Understand the nature of dregs
Dregs are the sediment at the bottom of wine, containing parts of wine. Hence they are impure (najis) and any beneficial use is prohibited, including medicinal application or giving to animals.
Combing hair is specifically mentioned because it has a beautifying effect; it is prohibited unless needed for medicinal treatment when no permissible alternative exists.
3
Analyze the hadd condition
The Hanafi school holds that the drinker of dregs is not flogged (hadd) unless intoxication occurs. The hadd is for deterrence from what natures desire; natures detest dregs. Additionally, the solid content predominates, making it akin to eating rather than drinking.
This is a key point of ikhtilaf with the Shafi'i school.
4
Compare with Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that any drop of wine triggers the hadd because prohibition of a small amount is certain. The Hanafi response distinguishes dregs due to repulsive nature and solid dominance.
The practical consequence (thamarah): under Hanafi law, one who drinks dregs without intoxication faces ta'zir, not hadd.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is the Hanafi view: dregs are impure and prohibited, but hadd requires intoxication.
This is supported by later Hanafi authorities like Fath Allah al-Mu'in.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحكام الأشربة
كتاب الأشربة خللت أو تخللت وكره دردي الخمر والامتشاط به، ولا يحد شاربه بلا سكر.
هو ما يبقى في أسفلها
أي بدردي الخمر الدردي
و كره دردي الخمر: لأن فيه أجزاء الخمر، فكان حراما نجسا والانتفاع بمثله حرام، ولهذا لا يجوز أن يداوي به جرحا، ولا أن يسقي ذميا ولا صبيا والوبال على من سقاه، وكذا لا يسقيه الدواب. [ تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٤٣/٨] وأراد بالكراهة الحرمة، وعبر بــ" كره"؛ لعدم القاطع. (فتح) والامتشاط به خص الامتشاط؛ لأن له تأثيرا في تحسين الشعر، إلا إذا كان على وجه التداوي، فإنه يجوز حيث لم يوجد من المباح ما يقوم مقامه. (فتح) ولا يحد شاربه إلخ أي شرب الدردي مكروه وحرام، لكن لا يحد شاربه إلا إذا سكر، وقال الشافعي يحد بلا سكر؛ لأن الحد يجب بشرب قطرة من الخمر، وفي الدردي قطرات منها، ولنا أن وجوب الحد للزجر، والزجر يشرع فيما تميل إليه الطباع والطباع لا تميل إلى شرب الدردي، بل تعافه، فأشبه غير الخمر من الأشربة، ولأن الثفل غالب، فأشبه غالب الماء، فيصير الخمر كالمستهلك به، وإذا كانت الغلبة للثفل كان هذا أكلا لا شربا ، فلا يرد عليه ما قيل: إن حرمة قليل الخمر قطعية. (فتح الله المعين)
Rulings of Drinks. The Book of Drinks: ...or it turned into vinegar. And the dregs of wine are disliked, and combing the hair with them, and its drinker is not flogged without intoxication. It is what remains at its bottom, meaning by dregs of wine the sediment. And the dregs of wine are disliked because they contain parts of wine, so they are prohibited and impure, and using something like that is prohibited. Therefore, it is not permissible to treat a wound with it, nor to give it to a dhimmī or a child, and the burden of sin is on the one who gives it to drink, and likewise he should not give it to beasts. And he intended by 'disliked' prohibition, and he expressed with 'disliked' due to the lack of a decisive proof. And combing the hair with it: he specified combing because it has an effect in beautifying the hair, except if it is for medicinal purposes, then it is permissible where there is no permissible substitute. And 'its drinker is not flogged' etc., meaning drinking the dregs is disliked and prohibited, but its drinker is not flogged unless he becomes intoxicated. Al-Shafi'i said: he is flogged without intoxication because the hadd (punishment) is obligatory for drinking a drop of wine, and in the dregs there are drops of it. Our proof: the obligation of hadd is for deterrence, and deterrence is legislated for that which natures incline towards, and natures do not incline to drinking dregs, but rather detest it, so it resembles non-wine drinks. And because the solid content is predominant, so it resembles the majority of water, thus the wine becomes as if consumed by it. And when the solid content is predominant, this is eating not drinking, so the objection that the prohibition of a small amount of wine is certain does not apply.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage is an expansive commentary on the ruling of dregs of wine (dardi al-khamr). The author explains that dregs are the sediment remaining at the bottom of wine after fermentation. Although the text says 'disliked' (karih), the commentator clarifies that this is actually prohibition (haram), but it is termed 'disliked' because the evidence is not absolutely decisive (qati). The dregs are considered impure (najis) since they contain parts of wine, and thus any use of them is haram—it cannot be used medicinally, nor given to a dhimmi, child, or animal; the sin falls on the giver. Combing hair with dregs is specifically mentioned because it has a beautifying effect, but it remains prohibited unless needed for medicinal treatment when no permissible alternative exists. As for the hadd punishment for drinking dregs, the Hanafi position is that the drinker is only flogged if intoxication occurs, because the dregs are not something people naturally desire; they detest them. The hadd is for deterrence from what is tempting, not from what is repulsive. Additionally, the solid part (thifl) is predominant, making it closer to eating than drinking. In contrast, Imam al-Shafi'i holds that any drop of wine triggers the hadd, regardless of intoxication, because the prohibition of a small amount is certain. The Hanafi response is that the ruling differs here due to the nature of dregs and the predominance of solids. This disagreement is a practical thamarah: under Hanafi law, one who drinks dregs and does not get drunk faces ta'zir, not hadd.

Key Terms
دردي (dardi): The dregs or sediment that settles at the bottom of wine.
الكراهة (al-karahah): Here it means prohibition (hurmah) but expressed as disliked due to lack of qati' evidence.
الحد (al-hadd): The fixed corporal punishment (flogging) for specific crimes like drinking wine.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the drinker of dregs of wine not flogged (hadd) unless intoxicated?
A) Because dregs are not wine at all.
B) Because the solid content is predominant, making it eating, and natures detest it, so hadd for deterrence is not applicable.
C) Because the Prophet ﷺ said 'There is no hadd except with intoxication.'
D) Because dregs are halal to drink.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between the absolute impurity of wine derivatives and the penal consequence of drinking, which requires intoxication.

Definition and Ruling of Hunting (Sayd) in the Hanafi School
Opening of Kitab al-Sayd: definition, ruling, and proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of sayd (hunting) and distinguish between the act of hunting (ownership) and the permissibility of eating the game (dependent on tadhkiyah).

Core Idea
Hunting establishes ownership, not permissibility; permissibility depends on the hunter being eligible for tadhkiyah.

This lesson opens the Book of Hunting by defining the term 'sayd' and establishing its basic ruling. The commentator clarifies that the act of hunting (istiyad) is permissible with trained predators, but a critical distinction is made: the hunt itself grants ownership, while the permissibility to eat the game depends on the hunter being eligible for slaughter. We will explore the linguistic and legal definitions and the primary proof from the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being defined? Sayd is defined as 'istiyad' (the act of hunting). The ruling is 'yuhallu' (it is permissible) with trained jawarih.
The literal translation of 'yuhallu' is 'it is made permissible'.
2
Distinguish milk from hillah
Note that the text says 'the ruling of hunting is ownership, not permissibility'. Why? Because permissibility comes from tadhkiyah (Islamic slaughter). The hunt itself gives ownership of the game, but the meat is only halal if the hunter is ahl al-tadhkiyah.
This is a key Hanafi subtlety; many laypeople conflate ownership with permissibility.
3
Locate the Quranic proof
Two verses are cited: (5:2) 'And when you are free, then hunt' and (5:4) 'Permitted to you are good things and what you have taught of hunting animals (al-jawarih).' These prove the permissibility of the act.
The imperative in 5:2 indicates permissibility (ibahah) after ihram is lifted.
4
Understand the legal definition
The commentator gives a legal definition: sending a trained animal with its conditions to take permissible wild animals. The conditions include that the hunter is ahl al-tadhkiyah, the game is not owned, and the animal is trained.
This definition excludes hunting by a muhrim or inside the Haram.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصيد
هو الاصطياد، ويحل بالكلب المعلم والفهد والبازي وسائر الجوارح المعلمة .
أي
الاصطياد
كتاب الصيد إلخ: قال في "التكملة" عن "العناية": إن مناسبة كتاب الصيد بكتاب الأشربة من حيث إن كل واحد من الأشربة والصيد مما يورث السرور ، إلا أنه قدم الأشربة لحرمتها اعتناء بالاحتراز عنها. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٤٥/٨] وقال في "ملا" مسكين" و "الفتح": المناسبة بين الكتابين أن كلا منهما من أسباب اللهو والطرب، وهما يوجبان الغفلة، قال النبي : " من اتبع الصيد فقد غفل"، إلا أن الطرب في الأشربة أقوى؛ لأنه بأمر يدخل في الباطن، والطرب في الصيد بأمر خارجي، فكان الأول أقوى، وصار بالتقديم أحرى، قلت: وكلا الوجهين يصلح أن يكون سببا لتقديم كتاب الأشربة على كتاب الصيد.
مع
أنه
تفسير
هو الاصطياد إلخ : أي الصيد في اللغة الاصطياد، هذا تفسير الصيد لغة، وإنما فسر الصيد بالاصطياد بالأخفى؛ لكون الاصطياد فرع الصيد، ومعرفة الفرع موقوف على معرفة الأصل؛ لأن الصيد مصدر، في " تكملة حاشية رد المحتار": يطلق تارة على الفعل أي أخذ الصيد، وتارة على ما يصاد، ويجمع على صيود. [٤٤٥/٨] فأشار بهذا التفسير أن المراد به ههنا الفعل، قال في "التكملة": ولا يخفى أن هذا أي ذكر المعنى اللغوي لا يناسب أن يذكر في المتن وأما تفسيره شرعا فهو على ما في "التكملة : الإرسال بشروطه لأخذ ما هو مباح من الحيوان المتوحش الممتنع عن الآدمي بأصل خلقته. واعلم أن حكم الاصطياد ثبوت الملك لا الحل؛ لأنه حكم الذكاة، وشرط ثبوت الملك كون الصيد غير مملوك، وشرط الحل أن يكون الصائد من أهل الذكاة، وسبب ثبوت الملك الأخذ، ثم الصيد مباح لغير المحرم في غير الحرم، ودليل إباحته قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِذَا حَلَلْتُمْ فَاصْطَادُوا (المائدة: ۲) وسائر الجوارح إلخ [جمع جارحة التي تجرح، وقيل: الكاسبة يعني يحل الاصطياد بهذه الأشياء المذكورة وبغيرها من الجوارح كالشاهين والباشق والعقاب والصقر، وفي الجامع الصغير : وكل شيء علمته من ذي ناب من السباع،
وذي مخلب من الطير فتعلم، فلا بأس بصيده، ولا خير فيما سوى ذلك تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٤٥/٨] والأصل فيه قوله تعالى: أُحِلَّ لَكُمُ الطَّيِّبَاتُ وَمَا عَلَّمْتُمْ مِنَ الْحَوَارِحِ ﴾ (المائدة: ٤) في معنى الجوارح قولان: أحدهما: أن يكون جارحا حقيقة بنابه أو مخلبه فيكون من الجرح بمعنى الجراحة، والثاني: الكواسب كقوله تعالى: وَيَعْلَمُ مَا جَرَحْتُمْ بِالنَّهَارِ ﴾ (الأنعام: ٦٠) أي كسبتم، ويمكن حملهما على معنى واحد؛ لأن الجارح هو الذي يكتسب، فيشترط أن يكون من الكواسب التي تجرح ليعمل بالجرح. وقوله "وسائر الجوارح" إلخ، استثنى أبو يوسف ذلك الأسد والذئب؛ لأنهما لا يعملان لغيرهما، الأسد؛ لعلو همته، والذئب؛ لخساسته، وذكر بعضهم الدب مكان الذئب، ومنهم من ذكر الثلاثة كصاحب "الاختيار"، =
The Book of Hunting. It is hunting (al-istiyad), and it is permissible with the trained dog, cheetah, falcon, and all other trained predators (al-jawarih). Meaning: hunting. The Book of Hunting etc.: He said in al-Takmilah from al-Inayah: The suitability of the Book of Hunting being placed after the Book of Drinks is that both drinks and hunting bring joy, but he gave precedence to drinks due to their prohibition, emphasizing avoidance from them. And in 'Mulla Miskin' and 'al-Fath': The connection between the two books is that both are causes of amusement and pleasure, and they cause heedlessness; the Prophet said: 'Whoever follows hunting has become heedless.' However, the pleasure in drinks is stronger because it enters the inner being, while the pleasure in hunting is external, so the former is stronger and more deserving of precedence. I say: both aspects are valid reasons for placing the Book of Drinks before the Book of Hunting. Explanation: 'It is hunting' means hunting in the language is al-istiyad. This is the linguistic definition. He defined sayd with the more obscure istiyad because istiyad is derived from sayd, and knowledge of the derivative depends on knowledge of the root. In Takmilah Hashiyah Radd al-Muhtar: It is sometimes applied to the act (taking game) and sometimes to the game caught, plural suyud. So this interpretation indicates that the intended meaning here is the act. He said in al-Takmilah: It is not suitable to mention the linguistic meaning in the matn. The legal definition according to al-Takmilah is: sending (an animal) with its conditions to take permissible wild animals that resist humans by their nature. Know that the ruling of hunting is the establishment of ownership, not permissibility; because it is the ruling of slaughter (tadhkiyah). The condition for ownership is that the game is not owned; the condition for permissibility is that the hunter is eligible for slaughter; the cause of ownership is taking. Then hunting is permissible for a non-muhrim outside the Haram. The proof of its permissibility is the verse: 'And when you are free, then hunt' (5:2). And 'all other trained predators' etc.: collection of jarhah which wound; it is said: earning. It means hunting is permissible with these mentioned things and other predators like the shahin, bashaq, eagle, and saker falcon. In al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'Any predator with fangs or any bird with claws that you train, there is no harm in its hunt, but there is no good in what is other than that.' The basis for this is the verse: 'Permitted to you are good things and what you have taught of hunting animals (al-jawarih).' (5:4) Concerning the meaning of jawarih, there are two opinions: One: that it is literally a wounder with fangs or claws, derived from 'jarh' meaning wound. The second: earners, as in the verse: 'and He knows what you have earned (jarahtum) by day' (6:60), i.e., you have earned. Both can be reconciled into one meaning because the jarh is the one that earns, so it is required that it be of the earners that wound, so that it works by wounding. And his statement 'and all other jawarih' etc.: Abu Yusuf excluded the lion and the wolf because they do not work for anyone else: the lion due to his high spirit, and the wolf due to his base character. Some mentioned the bear instead of the wolf, and some mentioned all three, like the author of al-Ikhtiyar. =
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Hunting (Kitab al-Sayd) with an explanation of the ruling and definition. The author defines sayd as the act of hunting (istiyad) and states that it is permissible (yuhallu) using certain trained animals: the dog, cheetah, falcon, and all other trained jawarih. Jawarih are predators that wound and earn for their owner. The commentator first addresses the placement of this book after the Book of Drinks, giving two justifications: both bring joy but drinks are stronger externally and internally, and their prohibition is more severe. Then he defines the term 'sayd' linguistically and legally. Legally, hunting is defined as sending a trained animal with its conditions to take permissible wild animals. The commentator clarifies an important subtlety: the ruling of hunting is the establishment of ownership (thubut al-milk), not permissibility (hillah); permissibility is a result of proper slaughter. The condition for ownership is that the game is not already owned; the condition for permissibility is that the hunter be eligible for tadhkiyah (i.e., Muslim or Kitabi). The proof for the permissibility of hunting is the Quranic verses 5:2 and 5:4. The discussion on jawarih includes two opinions: literal wounders or earners, both reconciled. Finally, Abu Yusuf's exception is mentioned: lions and wolves are not lawful for hunting because they do not work for humans (the lion is too proud, the wolf too base). Some substitute bear for wolf. This exception is intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
الصيد (al-sayd): The act of hunting or the game caught; here specifically the act, as clarified by the commentator.
الجوارح (al-jawarih): Trained hunting animals that wound and earn (catch game) for their owner; includes dogs, cheetahs, falcons, etc.
التذكية (al-tadhkiyah): The Islamic method of slaughtering an animal to make it permissible to eat; for hunting, the trained animal's catch serves as tadhkiyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the condition for the permissibility (hillah) of eating game caught by a trained dog?
A) The dog must be trained and the hunter must be Muslim or Kitabi.
B) The dog must be muzzled.
C) The game must be cooked immediately.
D) The hunter must be in a state of ihram.

Key Takeaway
Hunting (istiyad) with trained jawarih is mubah, but the game is only halal if the hunter is eligible for tadhkiyah and the animal is properly trained.

The Exception of Lions and Wolves in Hunting with Trained Animals
Intra-Hanafi exception to the permissibility of hunting with trained jawarih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi difference regarding hunting with lions and wolves and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf excludes lions and wolves from permissible hunting animals because they do not work for others.

In the previous lesson, we learned that hunting is permissible with all trained jawarih. However, the commentator notes an intra-Hanafi exception: Abu Yusuf excludes the lion and the wolf, and some also exclude the bear. This lesson examines the reasoning behind this exception and its practical implication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
The general ruling from the Quran and hadith is that all trained jawarih are permissible for hunting. This includes dogs, cheetahs, falcons, and other predators with fangs or claws.
The text quotes al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'Any predator with fangs or any bird with claws that you train, there is no harm in its hunt.'
2
Locate the exception
Abu Yusuf excluded the lion and the wolf from this general ruling. Some scholars also exclude the bear.
The exception is based on the nature of these animals.
3
Understand the rationale
The lion is excluded because of its high pride ('uluww himmatih) — it does not work for anyone else. The wolf is excluded because of its base character (khissatih) — it is untrustworthy and does not bring the catch to the hunter.
This rationale is based on empirical observation, not a direct text.
4
Note the alternative lists
Some scholars substitute the bear for the wolf, and some include all three (lion, wolf, bear). The author of al-Ikhtiyar mentions all three.
This shows a difference within the exception itself.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text does not explicitly state the relied-upon position, but later Hanafi works often adopt Abu Yusuf's exception. Students should be aware of both views.
In fatwa, the stricter view (excluding these animals) is often taken to be cautious.

Source Text

The Book of Hunting. It is hunting (al-istiyad), and it is permissible with the trained dog, cheetah, falcon, and all other trained predators (al-jawarih). Meaning: hunting. The Book of Hunting etc.: He said in al-Takmilah from al-Inayah: The suitability of the Book of Hunting being placed after the Book of Drinks is that both drinks and hunting bring joy, but he gave precedence to drinks due to their prohibition, emphasizing avoidance from them. And in 'Mulla Miskin' and 'al-Fath': The connection between the two books is that both are causes of amusement and pleasure, and they cause heedlessness; the Prophet said: 'Whoever follows hunting has become heedless.' However, the pleasure in drinks is stronger because it enters the inner being, while the pleasure in hunting is external, so the former is stronger and more deserving of precedence. I say: both aspects are valid reasons for placing the Book of Drinks before the Book of Hunting. Explanation: 'It is hunting' means hunting in the language is al-istiyad. This is the linguistic definition. He defined sayd with the more obscure istiyad because istiyad is derived from sayd, and knowledge of the derivative depends on knowledge of the root. In Takmilah Hashiyah Radd al-Muhtar: It is sometimes applied to the act (taking game) and sometimes to the game caught, plural suyud. So this interpretation indicates that the intended meaning here is the act. He said in al-Takmilah: It is not suitable to mention the linguistic meaning in the matn. The legal definition according to al-Takmilah is: sending (an animal) with its conditions to take permissible wild animals that resist humans by their nature. Know that the ruling of hunting is the establishment of ownership, not permissibility; because it is the ruling of slaughter (tadhkiyah). The condition for ownership is that the game is not owned; the condition for permissibility is that the hunter is eligible for slaughter; the cause of ownership is taking. Then hunting is permissible for a non-muhrim outside the Haram. The proof of its permissibility is the verse: 'And when you are free, then hunt' (5:2). And 'all other trained predators' etc.: collection of jarhah which wound; it is said: earning. It means hunting is permissible with these mentioned things and other predators like the shahin, bashaq, eagle, and saker falcon. In al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'Any predator with fangs or any bird with claws that you train, there is no harm in its hunt, but there is no good in what is other than that.' The basis for this is the verse: 'Permitted to you are good things and what you have taught of hunting animals (al-jawarih).' (5:4) Concerning the meaning of jawarih, there are two opinions: One: that it is literally a wounder with fangs or claws, derived from 'jarh' meaning wound. The second: earners, as in the verse: 'and He knows what you have earned (jarahtum) by day' (6:60), i.e., you have earned. Both can be reconciled into one meaning because the jarh is the one that earns, so it is required that it be of the earners that wound, so that it works by wounding. And his statement 'and all other jawarih' etc.: Abu Yusuf excluded the lion and the wolf because they do not work for anyone else: the lion due to his high spirit, and the wolf due to his base character. Some mentioned the bear instead of the wolf, and some mentioned all three, like the author of al-Ikhtiyar. =
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Hunting (Kitab al-Sayd) with an explanation of the ruling and definition. The author defines sayd as the act of hunting (istiyad) and states that it is permissible (yuhallu) using certain trained animals: the dog, cheetah, falcon, and all other trained jawarih. Jawarih are predators that wound and earn for their owner. The commentator first addresses the placement of this book after the Book of Drinks, giving two justifications: both bring joy but drinks are stronger externally and internally, and their prohibition is more severe. Then he defines the term 'sayd' linguistically and legally. Legally, hunting is defined as sending a trained animal with its conditions to take permissible wild animals. The commentator clarifies an important subtlety: the ruling of hunting is the establishment of ownership (thubut al-milk), not permissibility (hillah); permissibility is a result of proper slaughter. The condition for ownership is that the game is not already owned; the condition for permissibility is that the hunter be eligible for tadhkiyah (i.e., Muslim or Kitabi). The proof for the permissibility of hunting is the Quranic verses 5:2 and 5:4. The discussion on jawarih includes two opinions: literal wounders or earners, both reconciled. Finally, Abu Yusuf's exception is mentioned: lions and wolves are not lawful for hunting because they do not work for humans (the lion is too proud, the wolf too base). Some substitute bear for wolf. This exception is intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
الجوارح (al-jawarih): Trained hunting animals that wound and earn (catch game) for their owner; the dispute is whether lions and wolves qualify as such.
الأسد (al-asad): Lion; excluded by Abu Yusuf from permissible hunting animals.
الذئب (al-dhi'b): Wolf; excluded by Abu Yusuf from permissible hunting animals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Lion and wolf only.
B) Lion, wolf, and bear.
C) Only the lion.
D) All big cats.

Key Takeaway
Abu Yusuf's exception that lions and wolves are not permissible for hunting is an intra-Hanafi difference based on the animals' nature not to work for humans.

Definition and Ruling of Hunting in Hanafi Fiqh
Defines hunting linguistically and legally, states permissibility with trained predators.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hunting and identify the conditions for its validity and permissibility of the meat.

Core Idea
Hunting is permissible for the non-muhrim outside the Haram, but ownership and permissibility are distinct conditions.

This lesson clarifies the Hanafi legal definition of hunting (sayd) and its ruling. You will learn that hunting is fundamentally permissible (mubah) for those not in ihram and outside the sacred precincts, but the permissibility of consuming what is hunted depends on additional conditions related to the hunter and the method. The passage also discusses the types of trained predators (jawarih) that may be used and an intra-madhhab difference regarding lions and wolves.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish linguistic and legal definitions
Identify that the passage first gives the linguistic meaning of sayd as the act of hunting (istiyad), then provides the legal definition: releasing a trained animal under conditions to take permissible wild game that naturally eludes humans.
The matn clarifies that the intended meaning here is the action, not the quarry.
2
Identify the ruling and its evidence
State the hukm: hunting is mubah for a non-muhrim outside the Haram. Cite the Quranic evidence: 5:2 'When you become lawful, then hunt' and 5:4 'Lawful to you are good things and what you have taught of hunting animals.'
The imperative 'fastadu' indicates permission (ibaha) in context.
3
Understand ownership vs. permissibility
Note that the ruling of hunting is ownership (milk), not permissibility (hilla). Permissibility of the meat depends on the hunter being qualified for dhabh (Muslim or Kitabi) and the animal being wounded (jarh).
Ownership is established by taking (akhdh); permissibility is a separate condition.
4
Recognize the conditions for using trained predators
List the acceptable predators: trained dog, leopard, falcon, and all other trained birds and beasts of prey (jawarih mu'allamah). The training (ta'leem) is a condition.
Al-Jami' al-Saghir states that any canine-toothed predator or clawed bird can be used if trained.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on excluded predators
Abu Yusuf excludes the lion and wolf because they do not work for others: the lion is too proud, the wolf too base. Some also exclude the bear. The mufta bihi position is that all trained predators are permissible unless excluded by tradition.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
