The Book of Hunting: Hunting is the act of hunting. It is permissible with the trained dog, leopard, falcon, and all other trained birds and beasts of prey. With the explanation: 'Hunting linguistically means the act of hunting.' This is the linguistic definition. It was explained by the less known because the act of hunting is a branch of hunting, and knowledge of the branch depends on knowledge of the root. Hunting is a verbal noun. In Takmilat Hashiyat Radd al-Muhtar: it is sometimes used for the action of taking game, and sometimes for what is hunted; plural is suyud. This indicates that the intended meaning here is the action. In al-Takmila: This linguistic meaning is inappropriate for the matn; its legal definition is: releasing with its conditions to take what is permissible from wild animals that naturally elude humans. Know that the ruling of hunting is ownership, not permissibility, because permissibility is the ruling of dhabh. The condition for ownership is that the game is not owned; the condition for permissibility is that the hunter is eligible for dhabh. The cause of ownership is taking. Then hunting is permissible for the non-muhrim outside the Haram. The evidence for its permissibility is His saying: 'And when you become lawful, then hunt' (5:2). 'And all other trained birds and beasts of prey': the plural of jariha, which wounds, or as is said, earns. It means hunting is permissible with these mentioned items and other predators like the shahin, bashaq, eagle, and falcon. In al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'Whatever you have taught of the canine-toothed predators and the clawed birds, teach them, then there is no harm in their hunting; but there is no good in what is beyond that.' The basis for this is His saying: 'Lawful to you are good things and what you have taught of hunting animals' (5:4). Regarding the meaning of jawarih, there are two opinions: one is that they are truly predators with fangs or claws, from jaraha meaning wounding; the second is that they are earners, as in His saying 'and He knows what you have earned by day' (6:60) meaning you earned. Both can be reconciled because the predator is the one that earns; so it is required that they be from the earners that wound to act by wounding. His saying 'and all other predators' etc. Abu Yusuf excepted the lion and wolf because they do not work for others: the lion due to high pride, the wolf due to lowliness. Some mentioned the bear instead of the wolf, and some mentioned all three, like the author of al-Ikhtiyar.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Book of Hunting of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author first gives the linguistic definition of sayd as 'the act of hunting' and notes that the matn clarifies that the intended meaning here is the action, not the quarry. He then provides the legal (shar'i) definition: the release (of a trained animal) under conditions to take permissible game that by nature eludes humans. The ruling for hunting is that it causes ownership of the game, not permissibility (hilla) — permissibility is the effect of proper dhabh (ritual slaughter). Ownership requires that the game be unowned, and permissibility requires that the hunter be qualified to slaughter (i.e., Muslim or Kitabi). The cause of ownership is the act of taking (al-akhdh). Hunting is permissible for a non-muhrim outside the Haram. The proof is Quran 5:2 'and when you become lawful, then hunt.' The passage then confirms that hunting is permissible with the mentioned animals (dog, leopard, falcon) and all other trained birds and beasts of prey such as the shahin, bashaq, eagle, and falcon. It cites al-Jami' al-Saghir for the principle that any canine-toothed predator or clawed bird can be used if trained. The primary proof is Quran 5:4 'Lawful to you are good things and what you have taught of hunting animals.' The author discusses two meanings of jawarih: (1) those that wound with fangs or claws, and (2) those that earn/capture. Both are reconciled because the animal must earn by wounding. Finally, Abu Yusuf excludes the lion and wolf because lions are too proud to work for humans and wolves too base; some add the bear. The passage ends with a page break.

Key Terms
الصيد (al-sayd): Here used for the act of hunting (al-istiyad), not the quarry. In Arabic, it can be used for both.
الجوارح (al-jawarih): Birds and beasts of prey that hunt by wounding (jarh) or earning (kasb). Plural of jariha.
المعلم (al-mu'allam): Trained to hunt and respond to commands. The training is a condition for the permissibility of using the animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The hunter must say Bismillah when releasing the animal.
B) The game must be unowned (non-mamluk).
C) The hunter must be in ihram.
D) The predator must be a dog.

Key Takeaway
Hunting is permissible for the non-muhrim outside the Haram, but the meat becomes halal only if the hunter is eligible for dhabh and the animal is wounded.

Conditions of Training the Hunting Animal
Ruling with proof on training conditions for hunting animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for training a hunting animal, identify the signs of training for dogs and birds of prey, and explain the Hanafi evidence and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding the number of times.

Core Idea
Training the hunting animal is wajib; the dog is trained by leaving food three times, the falcon by returning when called.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on the training (ta'leem) of hunting animals. The school requires training as a condition for the permissibility of consuming game caught by such animals. The signs of training differ between dogs and birds of prey based on their physical nature. We will explore the textual proofs, the reasoning behind the distinct signs, and the differing opinions among the Hanafi imams on the required number of repetitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition of ta'leem
State that training (ta'leem) is a necessary condition (wajib) for the hunting animal's catch to be permissible without manual slaughter. Find the explicit statement in the passage.
The phrase 'Wa la budda min al-ta'leem' establishes this.
2
Locate the dalil
Identify the primary proofs: Quran 5:4 and the hadith of Abu Tha'laba. Note how each proof is used.
The Quran mentions 'mukallibin' (trainers); the hadith distinguishes trained from untrained dogs.
3
Distinguish the signs for dogs and birds
Explain why dogs are trained by leaving food (tark al-akl) and birds by returning (ruju'), and the qiyas based on body tolerance.
The illa is the animal's ability to withstand beating; both signs indicate the animal has learned to refrain/return.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on number
Describe that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) fix the repetitions at three, while Abu Hanifa (in one narration) delegates it to the teacher's discretion.
This shows flexibility; the mufta bihi is that three is sufficient but not rigid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولابد من التعليم، وذا بترك الأكل ثلاثا في الكلب، وبالرجوع إذا دعوته في أي ثلاث مرات أي برجوع الجارح البازي، ومن التسمية عند الإرسال ومن الجرح في أي موضع كان .. ولابد من التسمية وليس عدم جواز الصيد بها لمعنى يعود إلى عينها بل لفقد التعليم؛ لأن من عادتهم إمساك الصيد، وعدم أكله في الحال، فلا يستدل بترك الأكل على كونها معلمة حتى لو تصور التعليم جاز، وألحق بعضهم الحدأة بها؛ لخساستها، وأما الخنزير فمستثنى بلا خلاف؛ لأنه نجس العين، فلا يجوز الانتفاع به (عيني، فتح، ملا مسكين) ولابد من التعليم إلخ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا عَلَّمْتُمْ مِنَ الْجَوَارِحِ مُكَلِّبِينَ تُعَلَّمُونَهُنَّ (المائدة: ٤) ومعنى مكلبين: معلمين الاصطياد، ولقوله عليها لأبي ثعلبة: "ما صدت بكلبك المعلم فذكرت اسم الله عليه فكل، وما صدت بكلبك غير المعلم، فأدركت ذكاته فكل ، رواه البخاري ومسلم .وأحمد تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٤٧/٨] وذا بترك الأكل إلخ أي التعليم في الكلب يكون بترك الأكل ثلاث مرات، وفي البازي بالرجوع إذا دعوته، روي ذلك عن ابن عباس اما؛ ولأن بدن الكلب يحتمل الضرب، فيمكن ضربه حتى يترك الأكل، وبدن البازي لا يحتمل الضرب فلا يمكن تحقق هذا الشرط فيه فاكتفى بغيره ما يدل على التعليم؛ ولأن آية التعليم ترك ما هو مألوفه عادة، وعادة البازي التوحش والاستنفار وعادة الكلب الانتهاب والاستلاب؛ لائتلافه بالناس، فإذا ترك كل واحد منهما مألوفه دل على تعليمه وانتهاء علمه وهذا الفرق لا يتأتى إلا في الكلب خاصة؛ لأنه هو الألوف دون غيره من ذوات الأنياب فإنها ليست بألوفة والفرق الأول يتأتى في الكل؛ لأن بدن كل ذي ناب يحتمل الضرب، فأمكن تعليمه بالضرب إلى أن يترك الأكل تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٤٧/٨] ثلاثا إلخ: عند أبي يوسف ومحمد رحمها؛ لأن الثلاث مدة ضربت لذلك كمدة الخيار ونحوها، وهي رواية عن أبي حنيفة، أنه وعنه : مفوض إلى رأي المعلم؛ لأنه يختلف باختلاف الأشخاص والأحوال، فيفوض إليه، وعند الشافعي تعليم الكلب بالعرف وعند مالك بالانزجار [ رمز الحقائق: ۳۹۸/۲]
And training is necessary, and that [is established] for the dog by leaving eating for three [times], and for the falcon by returning when called, in any three times, i.e., by the return of the predator falcon. And [it is necessary] to mention the name [of Allah] at the time of release, and for wounding in any place. And it is necessary to mention the name. And the impermissibility of hunting with it is not due to a meaning returning to its essence but due to lack of training. Because their habit is to hold the game and not eat it immediately, so leaving eating is not taken as evidence of being trained, even if training is imagined, it is permissible. Some added the kite to it due to its lowliness. As for the pig, it is exempted without disagreement because it is intrinsically impure, so it is not permissible to benefit from it. And training is necessary due to His saying: 'And what you have taught of the hunting animals, training them as hunting dogs' (5:4), meaning teachers of hunting. And due to his saying عليه السلام to Abu Tha'laba: 'Whatever you hunt with your trained dog, and you mention the name of Allah over it, then eat. And whatever you hunt with your untrained dog, and you reach its slaughter, then eat,' narrated by Bukhari, Muslim, and Ahmad. And that [training] for the dog is by leaving eating three times, and for the falcon by returning when called, narrated from Ibn Abbas (رضي الله عنه). Because the dog's body can withstand beating, so it can be beaten until it leaves eating, while the falcon's body cannot withstand beating, so this condition cannot be realized in it; so another sign indicating training is sufficient. Because the sign of training is leaving what is customary. The falcon's habit is wildness and flight, and the dog's habit is greed and plunder due to its familiarity with people. So when each leaves what is familiar, it indicates its training and completion of instruction. This distinction is only realized specifically for the dog because it is the familiar one, unlike other canines which are not familiar. The first distinction applies to all because the body of every canine can withstand beating, so it can be taught by beating until it leaves eating. [The number] three is according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) because three is a period set for that, like the period of option and similar. This is a narration from Abu Hanifa, but from him it is also narrated that it is delegated to the opinion of the teacher because it differs according to persons and situations, so it is delegated to him. According to al-Shafi'i, training of the dog is by custom; according to Malik, by deterrence.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage deals with the condition of ta'leem (training) for the hunting animal, with proofs from Quran and Sunnah, and includes intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. It begins by stating that training is necessary (lā budda min al-ta'līm). For the dog, training is established by it leaving eating for three consecutive times (tark al-akl thalāthan). For the falcon/bird of prey, it is established by the bird returning when called (al-rujū‘ idhā da‘awtah). The passage then explains the wisdom behind these different signs: the dog's body can withstand beating, so it can be physically trained to refrain from eating its catch; the falcon's body is delicate, so a different sign must be used—returning when called indicates it is trained. This is based on a narration from Ibn Abbas (RA). The text also mentions that some scholars include the kite (ḥidāh) in the category of lowly birds that are not acceptable. The pig is unanimously excluded because it is najis al-‘ayn. The proof for the necessity of training is Quran 5:4 and the hadith of Abu Tha'laba. The passage then discusses the number of times (three). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that three is the fixed number, like the period of option in contracts. Abu Hanifa has two narrations: one agreeing with three, and another that delegates it to the discretion of the teacher because animals differ. The passage concludes by noting that al-Shafi'i determines training by custom ('urf) and Malik by the animal being deterred (inzijār).

Key Terms
التعليم (al-ta'leem): Training the hunting animal to respond to commands and refrain from eating the catch until given permission.
المكلب (al-mukallib): Trainer of hunting dogs; from kalb (dog). In Quran 5:4, mukallibīn means those who teach the dogs to hunt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is training established for a falcon (bazi)?
A) By beating it until it leaves eating.
B) By it returning when called three times.
C) By it not eating the prey for three days.
D) By custom and usage in the region.

Key Takeaway
Training the hunting animal is a wajib condition; its sign differs by species (dog: leaving food three times; bird: returning when called), and the number three is a guideline, not a rigid limit according to Abu Hanifa.

The Obligation of Tasmiyah at Release and the Exception for Forgetfulness
States the obligation of tasmiyah with evidence and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tasmiyah when releasing a hunting animal, identify the Quranic proof, and explain when forgetfulness is excused.

Core Idea
Tasmiyah at release is wajib; forgetting it does not make the game haram.

The obligation to mention Allah's name at the moment of releasing a trained hunting animal is a key condition for the permissibility of the game. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq establishes the ruling, provides the Quranic evidence, and clarifies that forgetfulness is excused. It also addresses edge cases like the length of the pause and whether the same rule applies to fishing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is releasing a trained hunting animal (like a dog or bird of prey) to catch game. The ruling is that the hunter must say Bismillah at the moment of release for the catch to be permissible.
This is a condition (shart) for the catch to be halal.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is Quran 6:121: 'Do not eat of that over which Allah's name has not been mentioned.' The passage also references the hadith of Abu Tha'laba, which states: 'When you send your dog, mention the name of Allah.'
The verse is general; the hadith specifies the context of hunting.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Quranic prohibition includes meat from an animal killed without mentioning Allah's name. In hunting, the trained animal acts as the hunter's agent; thus the hunter must say Bismillah at release to fulfill the requirement.
If tasmiyah is omitted, the catch is considered as if the animal was killed without Allah's name, making it haram unless forgotten.
4
Note the exception for forgetfulness
If the hunter forgets to say Bismillah at release, the catch is still halal. This is based on another hadith: 'And if you forget, there is no sin.' The obligation remains, but forgetfulness is excused.
This exception applies only to unintentional forgetfulness, not deliberate omission.
5
Consider the scope and edge cases
The passage also discusses that the pause between release and catch should not be too long, unless the predator uses a hunting strategy (like ambush). Additionally, the commentator notes that the rule applies only to land game, not fish, so stating 'game of the land' would be more precise.
The absolute wording of the author includes fish, but the commentator corrects this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن التسمية إلخ: عطف على قوله: "من "التعليم" أي ولابد من التسمية بعد الإرسال؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تَأْكُلُوا مِمَّا لَمْ يُذْكَرِ اسْمُ اللَّهِ عَلَيْهِ ﴾ (الأنعام: (۱۲۱) ولما مر من حديث أبي ثعلبة، وكذا لابد من عدم طول وقفته بعد الإرسال؛ فإنها إن طالت بعده لم يكن الاصطياد مضافا إلى الإرسال إلا إذا كمن الفهد، فإنه حيلته في الاصطياد، فيكون مضافا إلى الإرسال وكذا إذا اعتاد الكلب الاختفاء؛ لما بينا في الفهد واعلم أن المصنف أطلق في قوله: "ولابد من التسمية" فشمل ما إذا كان المرمي سمكا أو غيره من صيد البر مع أن الرمي إلى السمك لا يحتاج إلى التسمية، فلو قال: "في صيد البر" لكان أولى ، وإذا نسي التسمية عند الإرسال، فلا بأس به، وقد بيناه. (عيني، فتح، تكملة)
And from the tasmiyah etc.: It is connected to his saying 'from teaching', i.e., it is necessary to mention the name [of Allah] after the release, due to His saying: 'And do not eat of that over which Allah's name has not been mentioned' (6:121), and due to the hadith of Abu Tha'laba that has passed. Similarly, it is necessary that the pause [between release and catch] is not long; for if it becomes long after release, the hunting is not attributed to the release, unless the leopard lies in ambush, for that is its strategy in hunting, so it is attributed to the release. Similarly, if the dog has become accustomed to hiding, as we explained in the case of the leopard. And know that the author (al-Musannif) gave an absolute statement in his saying: 'And it is necessary to mention the name' which includes whether the target is fish or other land game, even though casting toward fish does not require tasmiyah. So if he had said 'in land hunting' it would have been better. And if one forgets the tasmiyah at the time of release, there is no harm in it, and we have explained that.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage discusses the obligation of tasmiyah (saying Bismillah) when releasing the hunting animal. It is connected to the earlier condition of training, implying that just as training is necessary, so is mentioning Allah's name at the moment of release. The proof is Quran 6:121 'Do not eat of that over which Allah's name has not been mentioned' and the hadith of Abu Tha'laba (which states: 'if you mention the name of Allah, then eat'). The passage also adds a related condition: the time between release and capture should not be excessively long, otherwise the catch is not attributed to the release. However, if the predator lurks (like a leopard that ambushes) or if the dog customarily hides, then the longer pause is acceptable because it is part of the animal's hunting strategy. The author (al-Musannif, i.e., the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq) made an absolute statement requiring tasmiyah for any hunting, but the commentator notes that this includes fishing, which does not require tasmiyah. It would have been more precise to restrict it to land hunting. Finally, if one forgets to say Bismillah at release, the catch is still permissible; there is no harm (lā ba'sa bihi). This exception is based on other Hanafi principles (e.g., the hadith of Abu Tha'laba also indicates that forgetfulness does not harm).

Key Terms
التسمية (al-tasmiyah): Mentioning the name of Allah (saying Bismillah) at the release of the hunting animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a hunter deliberately omits tasmiyah when releasing his trained dog?
A) The game is halal if the dog is properly trained.
B) The game is haram because tasmiyah is obligatory.
C) The game is halal because the dog acts on its own.
D) The game is makruh but permissible.

Key Takeaway
Tasmiyah at release is wajib; forgetfulness is excused, but deliberate omission renders the game haram.

The Condition of Wounding in Hunting: Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the necessity of jarh in hunting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Zahir al-Riwayah position that jarh is required, explain the opposing view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and identify the Quranic proof (5:4) used by both sides.

Core Idea
Zahir al-Riwayah requires jarh; Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf do not — the Quranic text is absolute.

This passage addresses a key condition in hunting: must the predator wound the game or is any kill sufficient? The purpose of hunting, like slaughter, is to let the flowing blood escape. According to the apparent narrations (Zahir al-Riwayah), a wound is necessary to achieve this. However, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf disagree, arguing that the Quranic permission in 5:4 is unconditional.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition under dispute
The passage discusses 'al-jarh' (wounding) as a condition for the hunted game to be permissible. Ask: what exactly is being required?
The condition applies only to hunting with trained predators, not to slaughter.
2
State the Zahir al-Riwayah position
Zahir al-Riwayah holds that jarh is required (wajib). The reasoning: the goal is to let out the flowing blood, which normally requires a wound. Without it, the game is not halal.
This is the 'apparent narration' from Imam Muhammad's books.
3
State the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that jarh is not a condition. Their view is supported by al-Sha'bi and one opinion of al-Shafi'i. They cite the absolute wording of Quran 5:4.
Imam Muhammad's position appears to align with Zahir al-Riwayah.
4
Analyze the dalil
The verse 'So eat of what they have caught for you' is mutlaq (unrestricted). Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf take it at face value: no wounding required. The opposing side argues that the verse implies the predator's normal method, which includes wounding.
This is a classic usul issue: mutlaq vs. implied characteristic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الجرح إلخ عطف أيضا على قوله من "التعليم" أي ولابد من الجرح في أي موضع كان من الصيد؛ لأن المقصود إخراج الدم المسفوح، وهو يخرج بالجرح عادة وهذا ظاهر الرواية، وعن أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف: أنه لا يشترط، وهو قول الشعبي، وقول للشافعي؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَكُلُوا مِمَّا أَمْسَكْنَ عَلَيْكُمْ ﴾ (المائدة: ٤ ) . من غير قيد للجرح، = مطلقا
And from the wounding etc.: It is also connected to his saying 'from teaching', i.e., it is necessary to wound [the game] in any place it is, because the purpose is to let out the flowing blood, and that comes out typically from a wound. This is the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah). And from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: it is not a condition. This is the saying of al-Sha'bi and a saying of al-Shafi'i, due to His saying: 'So eat of what they have caught for you' (5:4) without any restriction of wounding, absolutely.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage addresses the condition of jarh (wounding) in hunting. It is connected to the earlier conditions, implying that wounding is also necessary. According to Zahir al-Riwayah (the apparent transmission), the game must be wounded by the predator; the purpose is to let the blood flow, which typically occurs by a wound. However, there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf do not require wounding as a condition. This is also the view of al-Sha'bi and one opinion of al-Shafi'i. Their proof is Quran 5:4 'eat of what they have caught for you' – which is absolute and does not stipulate wounding. The passage ends here, indicating that the discussion continues on the next page (which is not included in this window). The ruling: According to Zahir al-Riwayah, wounding is required for the game to be halal; but the alternative view (attributed to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) considers the catch permissible even without a wound, as long as the animal is killed by the predator's attack, even if it dies from shock or strangulation. This difference has practical implications: if a trained dog catches a deer and kills it by breaking its neck without drawing blood, some Hanafi scholars would permit it according to Abu Hanifa's view, while others would require a wound.

Key Terms
الجرح (al-jarh): Wounding the game by the predator's claws or fangs, causing blood to flow.
الدم المسفوح (al-dam al-masfuh): Flowing blood that is required to be let out in slaughter; in hunting, the wound serves this purpose.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) on jarh in the Hanafi school?
A) Wounding is not required; the game is halal in any case.
B) Wounding is required for the game to be halal.
C) Wounding is required only for birds, not land animals.
D) Wounding is recommended but not obligatory.

Key Takeaway
In hunting, the Hanafi madhhab's relied-upon position requires wounding (jarh) to let out the flowing blood, but Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf consider it unnecessary due to the absolute Quranic permission.

Condition of Training (Ta'lim) for Hunting Animals
Explains the condition of ta'lim for dogs and birds of prey

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi requirement for training hunting animals, distinguish between the proof of training for dogs vs birds, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the number three.

Core Idea
Training a hunting animal is wajib; for dogs, proven by leaving prey three times; for birds, by returning when called.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi condition of ta'lim (training) for hunting animals. Without proper training, the prey caught by an animal is not permissible to eat. The text establishes distinct methods to verify training for dogs and birds of prey, supported by Quranic and hadith proofs, and notes an important difference of opinion within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its conditions
The passage states that training (ta'lim) is necessary (wajib) for a hunting animal to be lawful. Without it, the prey is haram.
This is a condition, not a pillar.
2
Distinguish the signs of training for dogs vs birds
For dogs, training is established by the animal leaving eating the prey three times. For birds of prey (baz), it is established by the bird returning when called three times.
The difference arises because dogs can be beaten to enforce training, while birds cannot.
3
Analyze the proof from Quran and hadith
The primary dalil is Quran 5:4, which commands training hunting animals (mukallibin). The hadith of Abu Tha'laba explicitly distinguishes between trained and untrained dogs.
The Quranic text is qat'i al-thubut but zanni al-dalala regarding the specific number.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad fix the number at three. A narration from Abu Hanifa delegates the number to the trainer's judgment. The mufta bihi is the fixed number of three.
This dispute is within the school; cross-madhhab views (Shafi'i, Maliki) also differ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولابد من التعليم، وذا بترك الأكل ثلاثا في الكلب، وبالرجوع إذا دعوته في أي ثلاث مرات أي برجوع الجارح البازي، ومن التسمية عند الإرسال ومن الجرح في أي موضع كان .. ولابد من التسمية وليس عدم جواز الصيد بها لمعنى يعود إلى عينها بل لفقد التعليم؛ لأن من عادتهم إمساك الصيد، وعدم أكله في الحال، فلا يستدل بترك الأكل على كونها معلمة حتى لو تصور التعليم جاز، وألحق بعضهم الحدأة بها؛ لخساستها، وأما الخنزير فمستثنى بلا خلاف؛ لأنه نجس العين، فلا يجوز الانتفاع به (عيني، فتح، ملا مسكين) ولابد من التعليم إلخ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا عَلَّمْتُمْ مِنَ الْجَوَارِحِ مُكَلِّبِينَ تُعَلَّمُونَهُنَّ (المائدة: ٤) ومعنى مكلبين: معلمين الاصطياد، ولقوله عليها لأبي ثعلبة: "ما صدت بكلبك المعلم فذكرت اسم الله عليه فكل، وما صدت بكلبك غير المعلم، فأدركت ذكاته فكل"، رواه البخاري ومسلم .وأحمد تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٤٧/٨] وذا بترك الأكل إلخ أي التعليم في الكلب يكون بترك الأكل ثلاث مرات، وفي البازي بالرجوع إذا دعوته، روي ذلك عن ابن عباس اما؛ ولأن بدن الكلب يحتمل الضرب، فيمكن ضربه حتى يترك الأكل، وبدن البازي لا يحتمل الضرب فلا يمكن تحقق هذا الشرط فيه فاكتفى بغيره ما يدل على التعليم؛ ولأن آية التعليم ترك ما هو مألوفه عادة، وعادة البازي التوحش والاستنفار وعادة الكلب الانتهاب والاستلاب؛ لائتلافه بالناس، فإذا ترك كل واحد منهما مألوفه دل على تعليمه وانتهاء علمه وهذا الفرق لا يتأتى إلا في الكلب خاصة؛ لأنه هو الألوف دون غيره من ذوات الأنياب فإنها ليست بألوفة والفرق الأول يتأتى في الكل؛ لأن بدن كل ذي ناب يحتمل الضرب، فأمكن تعليمه بالضرب إلى أن يترك الأكل تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٤٧/٨] ثلاثا إلخ: عند أبي يوسف ومحمد رحمها؛ لأن الثلاث مدة ضربت لذلك كمدة الخيار ونحوها، وهي رواية عن أبي حنيفة، أنه وعنه : مفوض إلى رأي المعلم؛ لأنه يختلف باختلاف الأشخاص والأحوال، فيفوض إليه، وعند الشافعي تعليم الكلب بالعرف وعند مالك بالانزجار [ رمز الحقائق: ۳۹۸/۲]
And training is necessary, and that is by leaving eating for three times in the dog, and by returning when you call it in any three times—i.e., by the return of the predator the baz. And from tasmiyah at the time of release and from wounding in any place. And tasmiyah is necessary. And the impermissibility of hunting with it is not due to a meaning pertaining to its essence but due to lack of training; because their habit is to catch the prey and not eat it immediately, so not eating does not indicate being trained. Even if training is imagined, it is permissible. Some included the kite with it due to its baseness. As for the pig, it is exempted without disagreement because it is najis al-ayn, so it is not permissible to benefit from it. (Ayni, Fath, Miskin). And training etc. is necessary due to His saying: 'And what you have taught of hunting animals, training them' (Ma'ida 4), and the meaning of mukallibin is trainers for hunting. And due to his saying to Abu Tha'laba: 'What your trained dog catches, and you mention Allah's name upon it, then eat; and what your untrained dog catches, then you find its slaughter, then eat'—narrated by Bukhari, Muslim, and Ahmad. Tkmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8:447. And this by leaving eating etc.: i.e., training in the dog is by leaving eating three times, and in the baz by returning when called. This is narrated from Ibn Abbas. And because the dog's body can withstand beating, so it can be beaten until it leaves eating; the baz's body cannot withstand beating, so this condition cannot be realized in it, so another sign indicating training is sufficient. And because the sign of training is leaving what is customary, and the custom of the baz is wildness and flight, and the custom of the dog is plunder and seizure due to its familiarity with people. So if each leaves its custom, it indicates its training and completion of its learning. This distinction applies only to the dog specifically because it is the familiar one, unlike other fanged animals which are not familiar. The first distinction applies to all because the body of every fanged animal can withstand beating, so it can be taught by beating until it leaves eating. Tkmila 8:447. 'Three times etc.': According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, because three is a period set for that like the period of option and the like. And it is a narration from Abu Hanifa, and from him: it is delegated to the opinion of the trainer because it varies with persons and circumstances, so it is delegated to him. According to al-Shafi'i, training of the dog is by custom; according to Malik, by deterrence. Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:398.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof concerning the condition of ta'lim (training) for hunting animals. The text establishes that for a hunting animal to be lawful, it must be trained. The method of determining training differs between dogs (canines) and birds of prey (baz). For dogs, training is proven by the animal leaving eating the prey three times; for birds, by returning when called. The reason for the difference is physical: dogs can withstand beating to enforce training, while birds cannot. The proof is from the Quran (al-Ma'ida:4) and the hadith of Abu Tha'laba. The passage includes an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the number three: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider it fixed, while Abu Hanifa has a narration delegating it to the trainer's judgment. It also notes that other schools differ: al-Shafi'i uses custom, Malik uses deterrence.

Key Terms
التعليم (al-ta'lim): The training of a hunting animal so that it responds to commands and does not eat the prey.
الجوارح (al-jawarih): Hunting animals (dogs, birds of prey) that catch game.
مكلبين (mukallibin): Those who train dogs (or hunting animals) for hunting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how is training (ta'lim) established for a hunting dog?
A) By the dog returning when called three times
B) By the dog leaving eating the prey three times
C) By the dog's custom of hunting
D) By the dog being deterred from eating

Key Takeaway
Training (ta'lim) is a necessary condition for lawful hunting; for dogs, it is proven by leaving the prey three times, and for birds, by returning when called three times, with the relied-upon Hanafi position fixing the number at three.

Hunting Animal Eating from Prey: Ruling on Baz vs Dog
Matn ruling and commentary on baz vs dog eating from prey

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi ruling on the eating of a hunting bird (baz) versus a hunting dog from the prey, and state the respective proofs from hadith.

Core Idea
A dog eating from the prey renders the remainder haram; a bird of prey eating does not, because training standards differ.

Hunting with trained animals is permissible in Islam, but what happens when the animal eats from the prey? The Hanafi school draws a clear line between birds of prey and dogs. This lesson examines the ruling, the reasoning, and the evidence, including a comparison with the Shafi'i view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Note the two distinct cases: the baz (bird of prey) eats from the prey, and the dog (or cheetah) eats from the prey. Each has a different ruling according to the matn.
Baz refers to falcons and similar birds; dog includes trained hunting dogs and cheetahs.
2
State the Hanafi ruling for each
For the baz: the remainder of the prey is halal (eaten). For the dog: the remainder is haram (not eaten) absolutely, whether the eating is rare or habitual.
3
Understand the underlying reasoning
The reasoning hinges on what constitutes proper training. For a bird of prey, refraining from eating is not a condition; its nature is to eat immediately. For a dog, a trained dog must not eat from the prey; eating indicates lack of training.
The commentator mentions that the analogy (qiyas) would treat both alike, but the exception for the baz is based on istihsan.
4
Examine the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof is the well-known hadith from Bukhari and Muslim: 'Except if the dog eats, then do not eat.' The prohibition is taken as absolute.
The hadith is ahad but has clear dalala (qati' signification of prohibition).
5
Compare with the Shafi'i position and the rebuttal
The Shafi'i school allows the remainder if the dog's eating is rare, based on a variant hadith. The Hanafi response: that variant is gharib (uncommon) and cannot outweigh the established hadith; even if authentic, the prohibition is precautionary.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is that Hanafis forbid absolutely while Shafi'is permit if rare.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أكل منه البازي أكل، وإن أكل الكلب أو الفهد لا. فإن أكل منه البازي إلخ : أي إن أكل البازي من الصيد أكل الباقي؛ لأن ترك الأكل غير معتبر في تعليم البازي، ومثل البازي غيره مما يجوز الصيد به من سباع الطيور، قال في "ملا" مسكين: وكان القياس أن يحرم الباقي، وهو أحد قولي الشافعي قال في "الفتح": ينظر وجه القياس. وإن أكل الكلب إلخ : أي لا يوكل الباقي عندنا مطلقا، سواء كان أكله نادرا أو معتادا، وللشافعي قولان فيما أكل نادرا في قول يحرم وفي قول يحل وبه قال مالك له قوله عليها لأبي ثعلبة حين قال: إن لي كلابا مكلبة، فأفتني في صيدها كل" مما أمسكن عليك، فقال: وإن أكل منه، قال: "وإن أكل منه". ولنا: ما في "البخاري" و "مسلم" من قوله عليه: "إلا أن يأكل الكلب فلا تأكل" ومرويهما غريب، فلا يعارض الصحيح المشهور، ولئن صح، فالمحرم أولى، ولو شرب الكلب من دم الصيد أكل؛ لأن شرب الدم وترك اللحم غاية حذاقة الكلب وعلمه؛ فإنه أكل ما لا يحتاج إليه المالك، وترك ما يحتاج إليه، ولو اصطاد الكلب صيودا و لم يأكل منها شيئا، ثم أكل من صيد بعد ذلك، لا يوكل من الذي أكل منه؛ لأن أكله علامة جهله، ولا ما يصيد بعده حتى يصير معلما على الاختلاف الذي بيناه في الابتداء، وأما الصيود التي أخذها من قبل فما أكل منها لا تظهر الحرمة فيه؛ لانعدام المحلية.
If the baz eats from it, it is eaten; and if the dog or cheetah eats, it is not. 'If the baz eats from it, etc.' means: if the baz eats from the prey, the remaining part is eaten because refraining from eating is not considered in the training of the baz, and similar to the baz are other [birds] that are permissible to hunt with from the birds of prey. He said in 'Mulla' Miskin: The analogy would be that the remainder is forbidden, and that is one of the two opinions of al-Shafi'i. He said in 'al-Fath': The basis of the analogy is to be considered. 'And if the dog eats, etc.' means: the remainder is not eaten according to us absolutely, whether its eating is rare or habitual. For al-Shafi'i there are two opinions regarding what is eaten rarely: in one opinion it is forbidden, and in another it is permissible, and Malik holds that view. For him (the Prophet) said to Abu Tha'laba when he said: I have trained dogs, so give me a ruling regarding their hunt: 'Eat what they catch for you.' He said: 'Even if it eats from it?' He said: 'Even if it eats from it.' And our proof is what is in al-Bukhari and Muslim from his statement: 'Except if the dog eats, then do not eat.' And their version is uncommon, so it does not oppose the well-known authentic [hadith]. And if it is authentic, then forbidding is better. And if the dog drinks the blood of the prey, it is eaten because drinking blood and leaving the meat is an indication of the dog's sharpness and training; for it eats what the owner does not need and leaves what he needs. And if the dog catches several preys and does not eat from any of them, then eats from a prey after that, the one from which it ate is not eaten because its eating is a sign of its lack of training, and neither what it catches after that until it becomes trained, according to the difference we mentioned at the beginning. As for the preys it caught before, the forbiddenness does not appear in them due to the absence of a basis [for the ruling].
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq presents a ruling on a specific issue in hunting: what happens when the hunting animal eats from the prey. The matn states a clear distinction: if a baz (a bird of prey) eats from the prey, the remainder is halal; if a dog or cheetah eats, it is not. The commentator explains the reasoning. For the baz, the training does not require it to refrain from eating, so its eating does not indicate lack of training. For the dog, the Hanafi position is that any eating, whether rare or habitual, renders the remainder haram. The commentator then presents the Shafi'i position, which distinguishes between rare and habitual eating, and mentions that Malik also permits. The Hanafi proof is the well-known hadith from Bukhari and Muslim: 'Except if the dog eats, then do not eat.' The commentator also addresses the case where the dog drinks blood, which is permissible, and the effect on future catches if the dog later eats. The lesson guards against assuming all hunting animals are treated the same way.

Key Terms
بازي (bāzī): A bird of prey used in hunting, such as a falcon. In this context, its training does not require refraining from eating the prey.
كلب (kalb): Trained hunting dog. If it eats from the prey, the remainder is forbidden according to the Hanafi school.
معلم (muʿallam): Trained (hunting animal). The condition for permissibility of hunting is that the animal is trained. Eating by the animal may indicate lack of training.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a trained dog eats from the prey it caught, what is the ruling on the remaining meat?
A) It is halal because the dog is trained.
B) It is haram regardless of whether the eating was rare or habitual.
C) It is halal only if the eating was rare.
D) It is makruh but not haram.

Key Takeaway
Dog eating from prey = haram remainder; bird of prey eating = halal remainder due to different training conditions.

Hunting with Arrow: Conditions and Proof
Matn ruling with proof and intra-madhhab expansion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for hunting with an arrow and explain the proof from the hadith of 'Adi ibn Hatim.

Core Idea
Hunting with an arrow is halal only if tasmiyah is said and the arrow wounds; if found alive, slaughter is required.

This lesson unpacks the ruling for hunting with an arrow from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The passage presents a clear ruling with a direct prophetic proof. We will also examine nuanced cases such as shooting at a sound and the difference of opinion among the Sahibayn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three constituent rulings
Extract from the matn the three parts: the general condition of scaring, the core ruling of shooting with tasmiyah and wounding, and the requirement to slaughter if the animal is found alive.
The matn begins with a related but distinct ruling on scaring (zanjar).
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
Find the hadith of 'Adi ibn Hatim cited in the commentary. Note its source and how it proves the ruling.
The imperative 'dhakir' indicates wujub of tasmiyah; 'fakul' indicates ibaha.
3
Understand the exception: water
The hadith excludes an arrow that falls into water. Identify the rationale: uncertainty about the cause of death.
This exception is a key practical limit.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on shooting at a sound
Examine the views of Abu Yusuf and Zufar when the hunter shoots at a sound and hits unintended prey. Note the relied-upon position.
The commentary provides these details as an expansion.

Source Text

٣٦٨
أحكام الصيد
فانزجر حل، وإن رمى وسمي وجرح أكل، وإن أدركه حيا ذكاه، وإن لم يذكه حرم،
أي صيدا سهما
:
بخلاف الأصل الأول، ولا يقال الزجر دون الانفلات؛ لكونه بناء عليه، فلا . يرفع الانفلات، فصار كالفصل الأول، والجامع أن الزجر فيهما بناء على الأول؛ لأنا نقول: الزجر إن كان دون الانفلات من هذا الوجه، فهو فوقه من حيث إنه فعل المكلف، فاستويا، فنسخ الانفلات؛ لأن آخر المثلين يصلح ناسخا للأول، كما في نسخ الأحكام، بخلاف الفصل الأول؛ لأن الزجر لا يساوي الإرسال بوجه من الوجوه؛ لأن كل واحد منهما فعل المكلف، والزجر بناء على الإرسال، فكان دونه من كل وجه فلا يرتفع، والبازي في جميع الأحكام المذكورة كالكلب إلا أنه وضع المسائل في الكلب للاشتباه، ولو أرسل كلبه المعلم على صيد معين، فأخذ غيره، وهو على سننه، حل خلافا لمالك، ولو أرسل من غير تعيين حل خلافا لمالك، ولو أرسل على صيود بتسمية واحدة حالة الإرسال فقتل الكل حل الجميع، بخلاف ما إذا كان على التعاقب. (فتح، عيني) وإن رمى وسمى إلخ لما فرغ من بيان حكم الآلة الحيوانية، شرع في حكم الآلة الجمادية، فتقديم الأول ظاهر، يعني إذا رمى بآلة جارحة وسمى إلى صيد فأصابه وجرحه، يؤكل؛ لقوله علي العدي بن حاتم: "إذا رميت سهمك فاذكر اسم الله تعالى عليه فإن وجدته قد قتل فكل إلا أن تجده قد وقع في ماء، فإنك لا تدري الماء قتله أو سهمك"، رواه البخاري ومسلم وأحمد . تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٥٦/٨] ولا فرق في ذلك بين أن يصيب المرمى بنفسه أو غيره من الصيد كما في إرسال الكلب، وإليه الإشارة من إطلاق الشيخ حيث لم يعين المرمى ولا المصاب، حتى يدخل تحته ما إذا سمع حسا، فظنه صيدا، فرماه، فأصاب صيدا غير سمع حسه ثم تبين أنه حس صيد، يحل أكله، سواء كان الصيد المسموع حسه في الأول مأكولا أو غير مأكول، وعن أبي يوسف أنه إن كان الحس حس سبع سوى الخنزير، يؤكل المصاب، وإن كان . حس خنزير، لا يؤكل. وقال زفر: إن كان حس صيد لا يؤكل لحمه كالسباع ونحوها، لا يؤكل المصاب، وإن تبين أن المسموع . آدمي أو حيوان أهلي أو ظبي مستأنس أو موثق لا يحل المصاب؛ لأن الفعل لم يقع اصطيادا، فلا يقوم مقام الذكاة، ولو رمى إلى طائر، فأصاب غيره من الطيور، وفر الطائر لا يدري أهو وحشي أم لا، حل المصاب؛ لأن الظاهر فيه التوحش، بخلاف ما لو رمى إلى بعير فأصاب صيدا، ولا يدري أهو ناد أم لا، لا يحل المصاب، ولو أصاب المسموع حسه وقد ظنه آدميا فتبين أنه صيد حل رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٠/٢] وإن أدركه حيا إلخ: لما ذكرنا أن القدرة على الأصل تبطل الخلف. (عيني) وإن لم يذكه إلخ: يعني إن لم يذكه بعد أن أدركه حيا حتى مات حرم؛ لما روينا وبينا في الكلب من المعنى؛ لأن كل واحد منهما ذكاة اضطرارا، فيكون الوارد في أحدهما واردا في الآخر دلالة لاستوائهما من كل وجه، وقد مر الاختلاف في الكلب، فلا يحتاج إلى الإعادة تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٥٨/٨] (وغيره) ما
حسه
Book of Hunting. 368. Rulings of Hunting. If it is scared [and turns away], it is permissible. If he shoots, names [Allah], and wounds, it is eaten. If he finds it alive, he slaughters it. If he does not slaughter it, it becomes forbidden. ... [commentary continues]
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof (dalil). It begins with the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq: rulings for hunting with a trained dog and bow. The first ruling covers scaring prey (zanjar) – if the animal is scared and turns away, it is halal; this is a preliminary action distinct from releasing the dog. The second ruling: shooting with an arrow while saying 'Bismillah' and wounding the prey makes it halal to eat. The third: if the hunter reaches the prey alive, he must slaughter it (dhabh); otherwise it is haram. The commentary expands: the baz (falcon) has the same rulings as the dog throughout. Then it details: if one sends a trained dog at a specific prey but it catches another while on its course, it is halal, contrary to Imam Malik. If sent without specification, also halal. If sent at multiple prey with one tasmiyah and kills all, all are halal; if the killings are sequential with separate tasmiyah, different ruling. The proof for the arrow is the hadith of 'Adi ibn Hatim: 'If you shoot your arrow, mention Allah's name, and find it killed, eat it unless you find it fell into water...' The hadith is from Bukhari, Muslim, and Ahmad. The commentary also addresses cases of shooting at a sound: if one hears a sound, shoots thinking it is prey, and hits prey different from the sound, it is halal. Abu Yusuf adds: if the sound was a predator other than a pig, the prey is halal; if a pig, not halal. Zufar: if the sound was from a non-edible animal (predators), the prey is not halal. If the sound turns out to be a human or domestic animal, the prey is not halal because the action was not intended as hunting. The principle: when the hunter has direct ability to slaughter, the substitute (dog/arrow) does not suffice; if he finds it alive, he must slaughter.

Key Terms
صيد (sayd): The animal being hunted; also the act of hunting.
تسمية (tasmiyah): Pronouncing the name of Allah (Bismillah) at the time of shooting or sending the dog.
ذكاة (dhabh): Ritual slaughter by cutting the throat and esophagus while mentioning Allah's name.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a hunter hears a sound, shoots at it thinking it is prey, and it turns out to be a human, what is the ruling on the animal he actually hit?
A) Halal because the hunter intended hunting
B) Halal if the animal is edible
C) Not halal because the action was not an act of hunting
D) Not halal unless the hunter repents

Key Takeaway
Hunting with an arrow requires tasmiyah and wounding; if found alive, slaughter is compulsory; otherwise the prey is haram.

Hunting with Trained Dog: Intended and Unintended Prey
Ruling with explicit inter-madhhab dispute and further elaboration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits a trained dog's catch even when it catches unintended prey, and contrast this with the Maliki position.

Core Idea
A trained dog's catch is halal even if it catches unintended prey, contrary to Imam Malik.

This lesson focuses on the special case of hunting with a trained dog. The matn states that if the dog is sent at a specific prey but catches another, or even if sent without specification, the prey is halal. This ruling is directly opposed to Imam Malik's view. We will explore the Hanafi reasoning and the extent of this principle.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling for the dog
Read the matn: 'ولو أرسل كلبه المعلم على صيد معين، فأخذ غيره، وهو على سننه، حل' and 'ولو أرسل من غير تعيين حل'. Understand that the dog's catch is halal regardless of specification.
The commentary adds that the baz (falcon) has the same ruling.
2
Identify the opposing view (Malik)
The matn explicitly states 'خلافا لمالك'. Understand that Malik requires the dog to catch the intended prey; otherwise it is not halal.
This is an inter-madhhab dispute.
3
Understand the Hanafi basis
The Hanafi school relies on the generality of the proof from the dog's nature: the trained dog acts as an instrument (ala) of the hunter; the hunter's intention transfers to the dog's action. The commentary implies an analogy with the arrow hunting ruling.
No explicit dalil is given here; the basis is istihsan and general permissibility.
4
Apply the rule: tasmiyah sequence
The matn also covers a scenario where one sends the dog at multiple prey with a single tasmiyah: all killed are halal. If the killings are sequential, the ruling differs. This shows the importance of the initial tasmiyah covering the entire hunt.
This is a practical elaboration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصيد
٣٦٨
أحكام الصيد
فانزجر حل، وإن رمى وسمي وجرح أكل، وإن أدركه حيا ذكاه، وإن لم يذكه حرم،
أي صيدا سهما
:
بخلاف الأصل الأول، ولا يقال الزجر دون الانفلات؛ لكونه بناء عليه، فلا . يرفع الانفلات، فصار كالفصل الأول، والجامع أن الزجر فيهما بناء على الأول؛ لأنا نقول: الزجر إن كان دون الانفلات من هذا الوجه، فهو فوقه من حيث إنه فعل المكلف، فاستويا، فنسخ الانفلات؛ لأن آخر المثلين يصلح ناسخا للأول، كما في نسخ الأحكام، بخلاف الفصل الأول؛ لأن الزجر لا يساوي الإرسال بوجه من الوجوه؛ لأن كل واحد منهما فعل المكلف، والزجر بناء على الإرسال، فكان دونه من كل وجه فلا يرتفع، والبازي في جميع الأحكام المذكورة كالكلب إلا أنه وضع المسائل في الكلب للاشتباه، ولو أرسل كلبه المعلم على صيد معين، فأخذ غيره، وهو على سننه، حل خلافا لمالك، ولو أرسل من غير تعيين حل خلافا لمالك، ولو أرسل على صيود بتسمية واحدة حالة الإرسال فقتل الكل حل الجميع، بخلاف ما إذا كان على التعاقب. (فتح، عيني) وإن رمى وسمى إلخ لما فرغ من بيان حكم الآلة الحيوانية، شرع في حكم الآلة الجمادية، فتقديم الأول ظاهر، يعني إذا رمى بآلة جارحة وسمى إلى صيد فأصابه وجرحه، يؤكل؛ لقوله علي العدي بن حاتم: "إذا رميت سهمك فاذكر اسم الله تعالى عليه فإن وجدته قد قتل فكل إلا أن تجده قد وقع في ماء، فإنك لا تدري الماء قتله أو سهمك"، رواه البخاري ومسلم وأحمد . تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٥٦/٨] ولا فرق في ذلك بين أن يصيب المرمى بنفسه أو غيره من الصيد كما في إرسال الكلب، وإليه الإشارة من إطلاق الشيخ حيث لم يعين المرمى ولا المصاب، حتى يدخل تحته ما إذا سمع حسا، فظنه صيدا، فرماه، فأصاب صيدا غير سمع حسه ثم تبين أنه حس صيد، يحل أكله، سواء كان الصيد المسموع حسه في الأول مأكولا أو غير مأكول، وعن أبي يوسف أنه إن كان الحس حس سبع سوى الخنزير، يؤكل المصاب، وإن كان . حس خنزير، لا يؤكل. وقال زفر: إن كان حس صيد لا يؤكل لحمه كالسباع ونحوها، لا يؤكل المصاب، وإن تبين أن المسموع . آدمي أو حيوان أهلي أو ظبي مستأنس أو موثق لا يحل المصاب؛ لأن الفعل لم يقع اصطيادا، فلا يقوم مقام الذكاة، ولو رمى إلى طائر، فأصاب غيره من الطيور، وفر الطائر لا يدري أهو وحشي أم لا، حل المصاب؛ لأن الظاهر فيه التوحش، بخلاف ما لو رمى إلى بعير فأصاب صيدا، ولا يدري أهو ناد أم لا، لا يحل المصاب، ولو أصاب المسموع حسه وقد ظنه آدميا فتبين أنه صيد حل رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٠/٢] وإن أدركه حيا إلخ: لما ذكرنا أن القدرة على الأصل تبطل الخلف. (عيني) وإن لم يذكه إلخ: يعني إن لم يذكه بعد أن أدركه حيا حتى مات حرم؛ لما روينا وبينا في الكلب من المعنى؛ لأن كل واحد منهما ذكاة اضطرارا، فيكون الوارد في أحدهما واردا في الآخر دلالة لاستوائهما من كل وجه، وقد مر الاختلاف في الكلب، فلا يحتاج إلى الإعادة تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٥٨/٨] (وغيره) ما
حسه
Book of Hunting. 368. Rulings of Hunting. If it is scared [and turns away], it is permissible. If he shoots, names [Allah], and wounds, it is eaten. If he finds it alive, he slaughters it. If he does not slaughter it, it becomes forbidden. ... [commentary continues]
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof (dalil). It begins with the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq: rulings for hunting with a trained dog and bow. The first ruling covers scaring prey (zanjar) – if the animal is scared and turns away, it is halal; this is a preliminary action distinct from releasing the dog. The second ruling: shooting with an arrow while saying 'Bismillah' and wounding the prey makes it halal to eat. The third: if the hunter reaches the prey alive, he must slaughter it (dhabh); otherwise it is haram. The commentary expands: the baz (falcon) has the same rulings as the dog throughout. Then it details: if one sends a trained dog at a specific prey but it catches another while on its course, it is halal, contrary to Imam Malik. If sent without specification, also halal. If sent at multiple prey with one tasmiyah and kills all, all are halal; if the killings are sequential with separate tasmiyah, different ruling. The proof for the arrow is the hadith of 'Adi ibn Hatim: 'If you shoot your arrow, mention Allah's name, and find it killed, eat it unless you find it fell into water...' The hadith is from Bukhari, Muslim, and Ahmad. The commentary also addresses cases of shooting at a sound: if one hears a sound, shoots thinking it is prey, and hits prey different from the sound, it is halal. Abu Yusuf adds: if the sound was a predator other than a pig, the prey is halal; if a pig, not halal. Zufar: if the sound was from a non-edible animal (predators), the prey is not halal. If the sound turns out to be a human or domestic animal, the prey is not halal because the action was not intended as hunting. The principle: when the hunter has direct ability to slaughter, the substitute (dog/arrow) does not suffice; if he finds it alive, he must slaughter.

Key Terms
كلب معلم (kalb mu'allam): A trained hunting dog that obeys commands and does not eat the prey it catches.
بازي (bazi): Falcon or any bird of prey used for hunting.
على سننه (ala sananihi): On its course – the dog continues in its pursuit without deviation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A hunter sends his trained dog at a deer with 'Bismillah'. The dog catches a rabbit instead. What is the ruling?
A) Halal because the dog is trained and tasmiyah was said
B) Halal only if the rabbit was also intended
C) Not halal because the dog did not catch the deer
D) Not halal because the rabbit is not the intended prey

Key Takeaway
A trained dog's catch is halal even if it catches unintended prey, with the condition of tasmiyah and the dog being trained.

Ruling on Prey That Disappears and Is Found Dead: Conditions for Halal
Rules on prey hit by arrow, disappears, found dead later: halal if hunter pursued continuously

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which prey that disappears after being shot is halal, and apply the Hanafi principle of 'al-mawhum fi al-muharramat' to similar cases.

Core Idea
Prey is halal only if the hunter did not cease pursuit; otherwise, uncertain cause of death makes it haram.

This lesson examines a common hunting scenario: an arrow hits prey, the animal struggles and disappears, then is found dead. The ruling depends on whether the hunter continued seeking it or gave up. The Hanafi school harmonizes two hadith traditions and applies a key usul principle to balance necessity and caution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios in the matn
The matn presents two outcomes: (a) prey disappears, hunter pursues, finds dead – halal; (b) hunter ceases pursuit, then finds dead – not halal. The key difference is the hunter's action.
2
Locate the hadith proof and its condition
The commentator cites the hadith of Abu Tha'laba: 'If you shoot your arrow and it disappears for three days, then you find it, eat it as long as it has not stunk.' The hadith permits eating but adds a condition ('not stinking') which indicates concern for external cause.
3
Understand the harmonization of the two narrations
Another narration says it is disliked if the prey disappeared, with the reason 'perhaps insects killed it.' The commentator resolves: the disliked applies when the hunter ceased pursuit; the permissive hadith when he continued. This shows the importance of the hunter's continuity.
4
Grasp the usul principle: al-mawhum fi al-muharramat ka al-mutaqqaq
When something is uncertain (mawhum) regarding a prohibition, it is treated as certain if the uncertainty can be avoided. Here, if the hunter continues, the uncertainty cannot be avoided (since prey often disappears in deserts), so necessity permits; if he stops, he could have avoided the uncertainty, so the probable cause (other animal) makes the prey haram.
5
Apply the additional condition: no other wound
Even if pursuit continues, the prey must have no other injury besides the arrow's; otherwise, the cause of death is uncertain and it remains haram.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن وقع سهم بصيد فتحامل وغاب وهو في طلبه فوجده ميتا حل، وإن قعد عن الصيد الرامي طلبه ثم أصابه ميتا لا
وإن وقع سهم إلخ: يعني إذا وقع سهم بصيد، فتحامل الصيد أي تكلف المشي أو الطيران بمشقة مع وقوع السهم فيه، وغاب عن النظر، فإن لم يقعد الرامي عن الطلب، ولم يزل في طلبه حتى وجده ميتا فهو حلال، وإن قعد هو عن طلبه، ثم أصابه ميتا لا يحل؛ لقوله عليها لأبي ثعلبة: "إذا رميت سهمك فغاب ثلاثة أيام، فأدركته، فكله ما لم ينتن، وروي أنه يكره أكل الصيد إذا غاب عن الرامي، وقال: "لعل" هوام الأرض قتلته"، فيحمل هذا على ما إذا قعد عن طلبه، والحديث الأول على ما إذا لم يقعد، ولأنه يحتمل أن يموت بسبب آخر، فيعتبر فيما يمكن التحرز عنه؛ لأن الموهوم في المحرمات كالمتحقق، وسقط اعتباره فيما لا يمكن التحرز عنه للضرورة؛ لأن الاعتبار فيه يؤدي إلى سد باب الاصطياد، وهذا لأن الاصطياد يكون غالبا في البراري وتواري الصيد فيها غالب، فما لم يقعد عن طلبه حل أكله دفعا للضرورة، ولا ضرورة فيما إذا اشتغل بعمل آخر، فيحرم، وهو القياس في الكل، إلا أننا تركناه للضرورة فيما لا يمكن التحرز عنه، وبقي على الأصل فيما يمكن، ويشترط للحل أن لا جراحة سوى جراحة سهمه. (تكملة، فتح) أو جبل أو على شجر أو حائط أو حجر. (طائي)
And if an arrow hits prey, and it struggles/efforts and disappears while he is in pursuit, and he finds it dead, it is permissible. And if the hunter ceases pursuit, then finds it dead, it is not permissible. [Commentary:] 'And if an arrow hits prey etc.' means: if an arrow hits prey, and the prey struggles i.e., labors in walking or flying with difficulty with the arrow in it, and disappears from sight, then if the hunter did not cease the pursuit and remained in pursuit until he found it dead, it is halal. If he ceased the pursuit, then finds it dead, it is not halal, because of his statement (peace be upon him) to Abu Tha'laba: 'If you shoot your arrow and it disappears for three days, then you find it, eat it as long as it has not stunk.' And it is narrated that eating such prey is disliked if it disappeared from the hunter, and he said: 'Perhaps insects of the earth killed it.' This is interpreted as the case when he ceased pursuit. The first hadith applies when he did not cease. And because it is possible it died from another cause; so it is considered in what can be avoided, because the probable in prohibitions is like the certain. And its consideration drops when it cannot be avoided due to necessity, because considering it would block the door of hunting. This is because hunting mostly occurs in deserts and the prey disappearing is common. So as long as he did not cease pursuit, it is halal to avoid necessity. There is no necessity when he occupied himself with another action; then it is haram. This is analogy (qiyas) in all, except we left it for necessity where it cannot be avoided, and it remains on the principle where it can. And it is required for halal that there is no wound other than the wound of his arrow.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof (dalil) from Kanz al-Daqaiq. The matn states: if an arrow hits prey, the prey struggles and disappears, and the hunter continues his pursuit until he finds it dead, it is halal. If the hunter stops pursuing and then finds it dead, it is not halal. The commentary explains the reason: the hadith of Abu Tha'laba permits eating if the arrow disappears for up to three days and is found not stinking, provided the hunter did not cease pursuit. Another narration says it is disliked if the prey disappeared, explaining: 'perhaps insects of the earth killed it.' The commentary harmonizes: the disliked applies when the hunter stopped seeking it; the permissive hadith when he continued. The underlying principle: when the prey disappears, there is a possibility that something else killed it. In cases where the hunter can avoid that possibility (by continuing pursuit), the prohibition remains because uncertain cause is treated as certain in prohibitions. But if the possibility cannot be avoided (as in normal hunting where prey often disappears), necessity allows leniency, otherwise hunting would be impossible. So if the hunter continues pursuit, it is halal; if he occupies himself with something else, it is haram. Additionally, the prey must have no other wound besides the arrow's injury.

Key Terms
فتحامل (fatahamala): The prey makes an effort to move (walk or fly) with difficulty after being hit.
غاب (ghaba): The prey disappears from the hunter's sight after being hit.
موهوم (mawhum): A probable/uncertain cause that is considered like certain in matters of prohibition when avoidable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A hunter shoots an arrow at a deer, the deer runs into a forest and disappears. The hunter sits down to rest for an hour, then goes to look and finds the deer dead. What is the ruling?
A) Halal because he eventually found it.
B) Halal if he continued pursuit within three days.
C) Not halal because he interrupted his pursuit.
D) Not halal because the deer was not found immediately.

Key Takeaway
Prey that disappears after being shot is only halal if the hunter continues pursuit without interruption and there is no other cause of death.

Ruling on Prey Falling into Water or from a Height
Ruling with proof from Kanz al-Daqaiq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on prey that falls into water or from a height, cite the Quranic and hadith proofs, and apply the principle of avoidable uncertainty to similar scenarios.

Core Idea
If the cause of death is uncertain and avoidable, the prey is haram.

Hunting requires precise knowledge that the arrow caused the death. When the prey falls into water or from a height, the cause becomes uncertain. This lesson explains the Hanafi position based on the Quran and hadith, and the critical distinction between avoidable and unavoidable uncertainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios in the matn
Notice that the matn presents two cases: (1) prey falls into water or from a height to ground, (2) prey falls directly to ground. The ruling differs.
2
Understand the ruling for each
In the first case (fall into water or from height), the ruling is haram. In the second (fall directly to ground), it is halal.
3
Recognize the proofs
The commentary cites the Quranic verse al-Ma'idah 5:3 (al-mutaraddiyah) and the hadith of 'Adi ibn Hatim about falling into water.
The hadith explicitly says: if you find it in water, do not eat, for you do not know if water or arrow killed it.
4
Grasp the principle of avoidable uncertainty
The underlying reason (sabab) is the possibility that something other than the arrow caused death, and that possibility is avoidable. If avoidable, the prey is haram; if unavoidable, necessity allows it.
Example: falling directly to ground is unavoidable, so halal.
5
Distinguish stable life and other cases
If the animal had stable life (hayatan mustaqirrah) at the time of the fall, it is haram by consensus. If unstable, there is an earlier difference of opinion.
The commentary refers back to the ikhtilaf on sending the dog.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن رمى صيدا، فوقع في ماء، أو على سطح أو جبل، ثم تردى منه إلى الأرض حرم، وإن وقع على الأرض ابتداء حل
حرم إلخ: أي حرم الصيد في هذه الصورة؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَالْمُتَرَدِّية﴾ (المائدة: ۳) ولما روينا، ولقوله علي العدي: "إذا رميت سهمك فاذكر اسم الله تعالى عليه، فإن وجدته قتل، فكل إلا أن تجده قد وقع في ماء، فإنك لا تدري الماء قتله أو سهمك"، رواه البخاري ومسلم وأحمد، ولأنه احتمل موته بغيره؛ لأن هذه الأشياء مهلكة، ويمكن الاحتراز عنها، فتحرم، بخلاف ما إذا كان لا يمكن التحرز عنه، فهذا هو حكم المحتمل في هذا الباب وهذا فيما إذا كان فيه حياة مستقرة يحرم بالاتفاق؛ لأن موته يضاف إلى غير الرمي، وإن كانت حياته دون ذلك، فهو على الاختلاف الذي ذكره في إرسال الكلب في حاشية قوله: " وإن أدركه حيا ذكاه، فتذكره تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٦٠/٨] وإن وقع الخ: أي حل الصيد في هذه الصورة؛ لأنه لا يمكنه الاحتراز عنه فسقط اعتباره؛ لئلا ينسد بابه على ما بينا، بخلاف ما إذا أمكن التحرز عنه؛ لأن اعتباره لا يؤدي إلى سد بابه ولو وقع على جبل أو سطح أو آجرة موضوعة، فاستقر، ولم يترد، حل؛ لأن وقوعه على هذه الأشياء كوقوعه على الأرض ابتداء، بخلاف ما إذا وقع على شجر أو حائط أو آجرة، ثم وقع على الأرض، أو رماه، وهو على جبل، فتردى منه إلى الأرض، أو رماه، فوقع على رمح منصوب أو قصبة قائمة، أو على حرف آجرة حيث يحرم؛ لاحتمال أن أحد هذه الأشياء قتله بحده أو بترديته، وهو ممكن الاحتراز عنه وقال في "المنتقى": لو رمى صيدا، فوقع على صخرة، فانفلق رأسه، أو انشق بطنه لم يؤكل؛ لاحتمال موته بسبب آخر تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٦٢/٨]
And if he shoots a prey, and it falls into water, or onto a roof or mountain, then falls from it to the ground, it is forbidden. And if it falls onto the ground initially, it is permissible. [Commentary:] 'Forbidden etc.' means: the prey is forbidden in this case; because of His saying: 'and that which has been killed by falling' (Surah al-Ma'idah, 3) and what we narrated, and because of the saying of 'Adi ibn Hatim: 'If you shoot your arrow, mention Allah's name, then find it killed, eat it unless you find it has fallen into water, for you do not know whether the water killed it or your arrow.' This is reported by Bukhari, Muslim, and Ahmad. And because it is possible it died from something else, as these things are destructive, and it can be avoided, so it is forbidden. Unlike when it cannot be avoided. This is the ruling of probability in this chapter. This applies when the animal had stable life; it is forbidden by agreement because its death is attributed to something other than the shooting. If its life was less than that, then there is the difference of opinion mentioned in sending the dog in the marginal note on his saying: 'And if he finds it alive, he slaughters it'... and it is mentioned. 'And if it falls etc.' means: the prey is permissible in this case because he cannot avoid it, so its consideration drops so that the door of hunting is not blocked, as we explained. Unlike when avoidance is possible, because considering it does not lead to blocking the door. And if it falls on a mountain, roof, or a fixed brick and remains still and does not fall further, it is permissible because its falling on these things is like falling on the ground initially. Unlike if it falls on a tree, wall, brick and then falls to the ground, or he shoots it while it is on a mountain and it falls from it to the ground, or he shoots it and it falls on an erect spear, standing reed, or edge of a brick—then it is forbidden because it is possible that one of these things killed it by its sharpness or by the fall, and it can be avoided. And al-Muntaqa says: if he shoots a prey and it falls on a rock and its head splits or its belly tears, it is not eaten because of the possibility it died from another cause.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from Kanz al-Daqaiq. The matn states two cases: if the arrow hits prey and the prey falls into water, or onto a high place and then falls to the ground, it is haram (forbidden). But if it falls directly onto the ground, it is halal. The commentary provides the proof: Quranic verse al-Ma'idah 5:3 (المتردية = that which has been killed by falling), and the hadith of 'Adi ibn Hatim: if the arrow falls into water, the cause of death is uncertain (water or arrow), so it is haram. The underlying principle: when the death may be caused by something else that is destructive and can be avoided, it is haram. When it cannot be avoided (like falling to ground directly), necessity allows it. The commentary also distinguishes between 'stable life' (life after being hit) and less; if the animal had stable life at the time of falling, it is haram by consensus. Other details: if the prey falls onto a mountain or roof and stays there without falling further, it is halal like falling to ground. But if it falls from a tree, wall, or onto an object that could cause death, it is haram. Al-Muntaqa adds: if it hits a rock and splits its head, it is not eaten.

Key Terms
متردية (mutaraddiyah): An animal that dies from falling from a height; mentioned in Quran 5:3 as prohibited.
احتمل (ihtamala): Possibility that the death was caused by something other than the hunter's arrow.
حياة مستقرة (hayatan mustaqirrah): Stable life: the animal is clearly alive after being shot, not in the death throes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A hunter shoots a gazelle, which falls onto a roof and stays there. The gazelle dies on the roof. What is the ruling?
A) Halal because it did not fall to ground
B) Haram because it fell onto a roof
C) Halal because it remained on the roof (no fall to ground)
D) Haram only if it fell from roof

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on prey depends on the certainty of the cause of death; if the cause is uncertain and avoidable, the prey is haram.

Ruling on Game Disappearing After Being Shot
Ruling on game lost after shooting, with commentary and reconciliation of hadiths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on game that disappears after being shot and apply the condition of continuous pursuit to determine its permissibility.

Core Idea
Continuous pursuit justifies halal; abandonment triggers prohibition due to the possibility of another cause of death.

In hunting, an animal struck by an arrow may disappear and later be found dead. Is it permissible to eat? The Hanafi school distinguishes between two scenarios based on the hunter's persistence. This lesson examines the ruling and its proof from the Sunnah, as well as the underlying legal maxim that balances necessity and caution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: game found dead while the hunter is still pursuing, and game found dead after the hunter has abandoned pursuit.
2
State the ruling for each
Halal in the first case (continuous pursuit), haram in the second (abandonment).
3
Locate the dalil
The hadith of Abu Tha'laba permits eating if found within three days unless putrid; another hadith expresses dislike when game disappears, suggesting another cause.
4
Understand the reconciliation
The commentator reconciles by applying the permission to continuous pursuit and the prohibition to abandonment.
5
Grasp the legal principle
The maxim 'al-mawhum fi al-muharramat ka al-mutahaqqiq' (conjecture in prohibitions is like certainty) applies when avoidance is possible; necessity overrides when it is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن وقع سهم بصيد فتحامل وغاب وهو في طلبه فوجده ميتا حل، وإن قعد عن الصيد الرامي طلبه ثم أصابه ميتا لا. وإن وقع سهم إلخ: يعني إذا وقع سهم بصيد، فتحامل الصيد أي تكلف المشي أو الطيران بمشقة مع وقوع السهم فيه، وغاب عن النظر، فإن لم يقعد الرامي عن الطلب، ولم يزل في طلبه حتى وجده ميتا فهو حلال، وإن قعد هو عن طلبه، ثم أصابه ميتا لا يحل؛ لقوله عليها لأبي ثعلبة: "إذا رميت سهمك فغاب ثلاثة أيام، فأدركته، فكله ما لم ينتن، وروي أنه يكره أكل الصيد إذا غاب عن الرامي، وقال: "لعل" هوام الأرض قتلته"، فيحمل هذا على ما إذا قعد عن طلبه، والحديث الأول على ما إذا لم يقعد، ولأنه يحتمل أن يموت بسبب آخر، فيعتبر فيما يمكن التحرز عنه؛ لأن الموهوم في المحرمات كالمتحقق، وسقط اعتباره فيما لا يمكن التحرز عنه للضرورة؛ لأن الاعتبار فيه يؤدي إلى سد باب الاصطياد، وهذا لأن الاصطياد يكون غالبا في البراري وتواري الصيد فيها غالب، فما لم يقعد عن طلبه حل أكله دفعا للضرورة، ولا ضرورة فيما إذا اشتغل بعمل آخر، فيحرم، وهو القياس في الكل، إلا أننا تركناه للضرورة فيما لا يمكن التحرز عنه، وبقي على الأصل فيما يمكن، ويشترط للحل أن لا جراحة سوى جراحة سهمه.
And if an arrow strikes game, and it exerts itself and disappears while he is in pursuit of it, and he finds it dead, it is permissible. And if the archer stops pursuing it, then finds it dead, it is not permissible. 'And if an arrow strikes game, etc.' means: if an arrow strikes game, and the game exerts itself, i.e., undertakes walking or flying with difficulty due to the arrow hitting it, and disappears from sight, then if the archer does not stop the pursuit and continues seeking until he finds it dead, it is permissible. If he stops the pursuit, then finds it dead, it is not permissible, due to his (peace be upon him) statement to Abu Tha'laba: 'If you shoot your arrow and it disappears for three days, then you find it, eat it as long as it has not putrefied.' And it is narrated that he disliked eating game when it had disappeared from the archer, and he said: 'Perhaps the vermin of the earth killed it.' So this is interpreted as when he stops the pursuit, and the first hadith as when he does not stop. Also because it is possible that it died from another cause, so that possibility is considered where one can guard against it, because the conjectured in prohibitions is like the certain. And its consideration falls away where one cannot guard against it due to necessity, because considering it would lead to closing the door of hunting. And this is because hunting mostly occurs in deserts and the concealment of game is common there. So as long as he does not stop the pursuit, eating it is permitted to avoid necessity. There is no necessity when he engaged in another action, so it becomes haram. And that is the analogy in all cases, except we abandon it for necessity where one cannot guard against it, and it remains on the original principle where possible. And it is a condition for permissibility that there be no wound other than the wound of his arrow.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter of hunting, explaining the case of game that is struck by an arrow and then disappears while the hunter pursues it. The initial statement from the matn gives two scenarios: if the hunter finds it dead while still pursuing, it is halal; if he stops pursuing and later finds it dead, it is not halal. The commentary expands on this, explaining that 'فتحامل' means the animal moves with difficulty. The ruling is based on two hadiths from the Prophet (peace be upon him) addressed to Abu Tha'laba: one permits eating the game if found within three days unless it stinks, and another indicates dislike if the hunter loses sight of it, suggesting the animal may have been killed by something else. The commentator reconciles these by applying the first to continuous pursuit and the second to abandoning pursuit. The underlying legal principle ('illa') is the possibility that the animal died from another cause. Where the hunter can guard against this (by not leaving the pursuit), the possibility is considered and renders the meat haram. Where it is impossible to guard against (as in the wilderness where game often disappears), the possibility is ignored out of necessity, otherwise hunting would be impossible. The analogy (qiyas) would make it haram in both cases, but necessity overrides it in the latter. A condition is added: there must be no other wound besides the arrow's. This follows the Hanafi ruling from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
تحامل (tahāmala): The animal exerts itself in walking or flying with difficulty after being hit by an arrow.
الموهوم في المحرمات كالمتحقق (al-mawhūm fī al-muḥarramāt ka al-mutaḥaqqiq): A legal maxim: in matters of prohibition, a strong conjecture (based on possibility) is treated as certainty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the underlying legal maxim applied in this ruling?
A) al-umur bi-maqasidiha
B) al-mawhum fi al-muharramat ka al-mutahaqqiq
C) la darar wa la dirar
D) al-yakin la yazulu bi al-shakk

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of game that disappears after being shot hinges on the hunter's continuous pursuit; abandonment triggers prohibition due to the legal maxim that conjecture in prohibitions is treated as certainty when avoidance is possible.
