Ruling on Game Falling into Water or from a Height
Ruling and proof for game that falls into water or falls from a height.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for game that falls into water or falls from a height, cite the evidence from Quran and hadith, and apply the distinction between avoidable and unavoidable causes of death.

Core Idea
Game that falls into water or from a height after being shot is haram unless it falls directly to the ground or on a stable surface.

This lesson continues our study of the rules of hunting (al-sayd). The Prophet ﷺ gave clear guidance: if your arrow kills the game, eat it, but if you find it in water, do not eat because you do not know what caused its death. The Hanafi school extends this principle to other avoidable causes of death, such as falling from a height or hitting an object. Let's examine the ruling and its proofs.

Guided Path
1
State the basic ruling
The matn states that if game falls into water or falls from a height to the ground, it is haram; if it falls directly to the ground, it is halal.
This is the core distinction.
2
Examine the Quranic proof
The verse (5:3) prohibits 'al-mutaraddiya' (the one that falls from a height). Though originally about carrion, it is applied by analogy (qiyas) to hunting.
The word 'المتردية' is key.
3
Examine the hadith proof
The Prophet's instruction about water creates an exception by way of uncertainty (shakk). The same reasoning applies to other avoidable hazards.
The hadith is narrated by Bukhari, Muslim, and Ahmad.
4
Understand the underlying principle
The ruling hinges on whether the cause of death could be something other than the shot and whether that alternative cause could have been avoided by the hunter.
If avoidable, it is haram; if unavoidable, it is halal.
5
Apply the principle to various scenarios
Different outcomes for stable surfaces (like roof, placed brick) vs. unstable surfaces (tree, wall, spear). If the animal lands on a stable surface and stays there, it is halal. If it falls further, it becomes haram.
Also consider case of hitting a rock (from al-Muntaqa).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن رمى صيدا، فوقع في ماء، أو على سطح أو جبل، ثم تردى منه إلى الأرض حرم، وإن وقع على الأرض ابتداء حل. حرم إلخ: أي حرم الصيد في هذه الصورة؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَالْمُتَرَدِّية ﴾ (المائدة: ٣) ولما روينا، ولقوله عليه السلام: "إذا رميت سهمك فاذكر اسم الله تعالى عليه، فإن وجدته قتل، فكل إلا أن تجده قد وقع في ماء، فإنك لا تدري الماء قتله أو سهمك"، رواه البخاري ومسلم وأحمد، ولأنه احتمل موته بغيره؛ لأن هذه الأشياء مهلكة، ويمكن الاحتراز عنها، فتحرم، بخلاف ما إذا كان لا يمكن التحرز عنه، فهذا هو حكم المحتمل في هذا الباب وهذا فيما إذا كان فيه حياة مستقرة يحرم بالاتفاق؛ لأن موته يضاف إلى غير الرمي، وإن كانت حياته دون ذلك، فهو على الاختلاف الذي ذكره في إرسال الكلب في حاشية قوله : " وإن أدركه حيا ذكاه، فتذكره تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٦٠/٨] وإن وقع الخ: أي حل الصيد في هذه الصورة؛ لأنه لا يمكنه الاحتراز عنه فسقط اعتباره؛ لئلا ينسد بابه على ما بينا، بخلاف ما إذا أمكن التحرز عنه؛ لأن اعتباره لا يؤدي إلى سد بابه ولو وقع على جبل أو سطح أو آجرة موضوعة، فاستقر، ولم يترد، حل؛ لأن وقوعه على هذه الأشياء كوقوعه على الأرض ابتداء، بخلاف ما إذا وقع على شجر أو حائط أو آجرة، ثم وقع على الأرض، أو رماه، وهو على جبل، فتردى منه إلى الأرض، أو رماه، فوقع على رمح منصوب أو قصبة قائمة، أو على حرف آجرة حيث يحرم؛ لاحتمال أن أحد هذه الأشياء قتله بحده أو بترديته، وهو ممكن الاحتراز عنه وقال في "المنتقى": لو رمى صيدا، فوقع على صخرة، فانفلق رأسه، أو انشق بطنه لم يؤكل ؛ لاحتمال موته بسبب آخر تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٦٢/٨]
And if he shoots game and it falls into water, or on a roof or a mountain, then falls from it to the ground, it is prohibited. And if it falls on the ground initially, it is permissible. 'It is prohibited, etc.' means: game is prohibited in this situation due to the saying of Allah (the Most High): 'And the one that falls from a height' (al-Ma'ida 3), and due to what we have narrated, and due to his (peace be upon him) saying: 'If you shoot your arrow, mention the name of Allah upon it, then if you find it killed, eat, unless you find it has fallen into water, for you do not know whether the water killed it or your arrow.' (narrated by Bukhari, Muslim, and Ahmad). And because it is possible that it died from something else, since these things are destructive and it is possible to guard against them, so it is prohibited. Unlike when it is not possible to guard against it. This is the ruling of possibility in this chapter. This applies when there is stable life in the game; it is prohibited by consensus because its death is attributed to something other than the shot. If its life is less than that, then there is the difference of opinion mentioned in the chapter of sending the dog. 'And if it falls, etc.': means game is permissible in this situation because he cannot guard against it, so its consideration falls away so that its door is not closed, as we explained. Unlike when it is possible to guard against it, because considering it does not lead to closing its door. If it falls on a mountain or roof or a placed brick, and settles and does not fall further, it is permissible because its falling on these things is like falling on the ground initially. Unlike when it falls on a tree or wall or brick, then falls to the ground, or he shoots it while it is on a mountain and it falls from there to the ground, or he shoots it and it falls on an erected spear or standing reed, or on the edge of a brick, then it is prohibited because of the possibility that one of these things killed it by its edge or by its fall, and it is possible to guard against it. And it is stated in al-Muntaqa: If he shoots game and it falls on a rock and its head splits or its belly bursts, it is not eaten due to the possibility of death from another cause. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/462)
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage continues the chapter of hunting, now addressing the case where the game falls into water or falls from a height after being shot. The matn states: if it falls into water or on a high place and then falls from it to the ground, it is haram; if it falls directly to the ground, it is halal. The commentary explains the prohibition is based on the Quranic verse (al-Ma'ida 3) which prohibits 'al-mutaraddiya' (the one that falls from a height) – though the verse is about carrion, the same principle applies. Also the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him): if you find your game in water, do not eat because you do not know what killed it. The reason is the possibility of death from the water/fall, which is an avoidable cause. If the animal has stable life at the moment of hitting the water/height, it is haram by consensus. If its life is unstable (e.g., barely alive), there is a difference of opinion. The commentary then contrasts the case of falling on a stable surface (like a roof or placed brick) where it does not fall further – that is halal because the situation resembles falling on the ground. But if it falls on a tree/wall and then to the ground, or upon a spear, etc., it is haram because the cause of death could be those objects. An example from al-Muntaqa: game hitting a rock and splitting its head is not eaten. This is a Hanafi ruling based on avoiding doubt (shubha).

Key Terms
المتردية (al-mutaraddiya): Literally 'the one that falls from a height'; in the Quran it refers to an animal that dies from falling, prohibited as carrion. Here extended by analogy to game that falls from a height after being shot.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A hunter shoots an arrow at a bird. The bird falls onto a tree branch and then falls to the ground dead. Is the meat halal?
A) Yes, because it ended up on the ground.
B) No, because it first fell on the branch, which could have killed it.
C) Yes, if the arrow hit a vital organ.
D) No, unless the hunter climbs the tree first.

Key Takeaway
Game that falls into water or from a height after being shot is haram due to uncertainty about the cause of death, unless it falls directly onto stable ground or a stable surface.

Ruling on Game Killed by Mi'rad (Side Strike) or Banduq (Pellet)
Ruling on game killed by blunt instruments and definition of mi'rad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on game killed by blunt instruments and apply the hadith condition of piercing to determine the permissibility of a hunting tool.

Core Idea
A hunting weapon must pierce or cut; blunt force alone makes the meat haram.

This lesson examines the ruling on game killed by weapons that do not normally penetrate, such as the side of a mi'rad (a type of arrow) and the banduq (a clay pellet). The Prophet's hadith provides the key distinction: if the weapon pierces, eat; if it strikes with its side, do not eat. The Hanafi school applies this condition to all hunting tools: only a sharp, penetrating weapon renders the meat halal. We will go through the hadith proof, the definitions of mi'rad, and the extension to other instruments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on mi'rad (side) and banduq
The text states that game killed by the side of mi'rad or by banduq is haram. Note the specific instruments and the part of the weapon that caused death.
The ruling is absolute for these two cases.
2
Extract the condition from the hadith
The hadith says: 'If you shoot with the mi'rad and it pierces, eat; if it strikes with its side, do not eat.' The decisive condition is piercing (kharq) versus side strike ('arad).
The hadith is from Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Understand the requirement of a proper wound
The underlying principle is that the game must be killed by a wound (jareeh) that cuts or penetrates, not by blunt force (dhaqq). Blunt force makes it carrion.
This is consistent with the earlier principle that a wound is necessary.
4
Apply the same principle to other weapons
The text extends the condition to knives, swords, stones, sticks, etc.: if they have an edge and penetrate, the meat is halal; if not, it is haram.
The key is the instrument's ability to cut or pierce.
5
Note the intra-madhhab difference regarding banduq with an edge
Al-Jawhara permits banduq if it has an edge that wounds, while Qadi Khan requires it to penetrate like an arrow. The mufta bihi is the stricter view: it must penetrate.
This shows some ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قتله المعراض بعرضه، أو البندقة حرم، وإن رمى صيدا، فقطع عضوا منه أو قتلته هي طينة مدورة الصيد لا العضو وإن قطعه أثلاثا والأكثر مما يلى العجز، أي لا يؤكل العضو أي والحال أن الأكثر " هو النصف المؤخرومات الصيد " وما قتله المعراض إلخ أي الصيد الذي قتله المعراض، أو الذي قتله البندقة حرام؛ لما روي عنه عليه أنه قال: "إذا رميت بالمعراض، فخرق فكله، وإن أصابه بعرضه، فلا تأكله"، رواه البخاري ومسلم، ولأن الجرح لابد منه لما بينا من قبل، والبندقة لا تجرح، وكذا عرض المعراض والمعراض سهم لا ريش ولا نصل له، وإنما هو حديد الرأس، سمي الحديد معراضا؛ لأنه يذهب معترضا تارة، وتارة يصيب بحده، هكذا فسر المعراض في "التكملة" [٤٦٣/٨]، ومثله في "الطائي" و"ملا" مسكين"، لكن قال العيني هو سهم طويل له أربع قذذ دقاق إذا رمى به اعترض، والقذذ قذة، جمع وهي ريش السهم، وهو كما ترى مخالف للتفسير الأول. [٤٠١/٢] ولهذا قال في "الفتح" بعد نقل كلام العيني: وفيه مخالفة لما ذكره الشارح، يعني "ملا" مسكين"، فاحفظه، وإن رماه بالسكين أو السيف فإن أصابه بحده أكل وإلا فلا وإن رماه بحجر، فإن كان ثقيلا لا يؤكل، وإن جرح؛ لاحتمال قتله بثقله، وإن كان الحجر خفيفا وله حد وجرح يحل؛ لتيقن الموت بالحجر حينئذ، ولو جعل الحجر طويلا كالسهم وهو خفيف و به حدة ورمى به صيدا، فإن جرح حل؛ لقتله بجرحه، ولو رماه بمروة حديدة، فلم يبضع بضعا لا يحل؛ لأنه قتله دقا، ولو رماه بعود مثل العصا ونحوه، لا يحل إلا إذا كان له حد، فيبضع بضعا، وفي "الجوهرة": البندقة إذا كان لها حد يجرح أكل، وقال قاضي خان لا يحل صيد البندقة والحجر والمعراض والعصا وما أشبه ذلك، وإن جرح؛ لأنه لا يخزق إلا أن يكون شيء من ذلك قد حدوه وطولوه كالسهم، وأمكن يرمى به، فإن كان كذلك، وخزقه بحده، يحل أكله، كذا في الشروح.
And what the mi'rad kills with its side, or the banduq kills, is prohibited. And if he shoots game and cuts off a limb from it, or [the arrow] kills it, it is a clay pellet (i.e., the game is not eaten, but the limb is not eaten). And if he cuts it into three parts, and the majority is towards the rear, meaning the limb is not eaten. And that the majority is the posterior half, and the game dies. 'And what the mi'rad kills, etc.' means: the game that the mi'rad kills, or that the banduq kills, is prohibited, due to what was narrated from him (peace be upon him) that he said: 'If you shoot with the mi'rad and it pierces, eat it; if it strikes it with its side, do not eat it.' (narrated by Bukhari and Muslim). And because a wound is necessary as we explained before. The banduq does not wound, and likewise the side of the mi'rad. The mi'rad is an arrow that has no feathers and no blade; it is only an iron head. It is called mi'rad because it goes sideways sometimes and sometimes hits with its edge. This is how the mi'rad is explained in al-Takmila (8/463), and similarly in al-Ta'i and Mulla Miskin. But al-'Aini said it is a long arrow with four thin feathers; when shot, it goes sideways. Al-Qudhudh (feathers) is the plural of qudhah, which is the feather of an arrow. This, as you see, contradicts the first interpretation. (2/401) Therefore, in al-Fath after quoting al-'Aini, it is said: In it is a contradiction to what the commentator (meaning Mulla Miskin) mentioned, so preserve that. And if one shoots it with a knife or a sword, if it hits with its edge, it is eaten; otherwise, not. And if he shoots it with a stone, if it is heavy, it is not eaten even if it wounds, due to the possibility that its weight killed it. If the stone is light and has an edge and wounds, it is permissible, because death by the stone is then certain. And if he makes the stone long like an arrow, light, with sharpness, and shoots game with it, if it wounds, it is permissible because it killed it by wounding. And if he shoots it with an iron mace (mirwah) and it does not cut properly, it is not permissible because it killed it by crushing. And if he shoots it with a stick like a staff and similar, it is not permissible unless it has an edge that cuts properly. In al-Jawhara: The banduq if it has an edge that wounds, it is eaten. Qadi Khan said: The game of banduq, stone, mi'rad, and stick and similar is not permissible even if it wounds, because it does not penetrate, unless something of that has been sharpened and lengthened like an arrow and can be shot with it. If that is the case and it penetrates with its edge, its eating is permissible. This is how it is in the commentaries.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage deals with the instruments of hunting. The matn states that game killed by the side of a mi'rad (a type of arrow without feathers) or by a banduq (a clay pellet) is haram. The commentary explains the hadith differentiating between piercing (which requires eating) and striking with the side (which forbids). The underlying principle is that a true wound (jareeh) is necessary for the game to be considered properly killed; merely crushing or blunt force does not count. The mi'rad is defined in two ways: the Takmila says it is an arrow with an iron head but no feathers or blades, which may hit sideways or with its point. Al-'Aini contradicts this, saying it has four feathers and goes sideways. The commentator notes the difference. The passage then extends to other weapons: knife/sword: only if it cuts with its edge; stone: if heavy, haram; if light and sharp, halal; similarly, a stick may be halal if sharpened to cut. The banduq (pellet) if sharp and penetrates, it becomes permissible according to some, but Qadi Khan holds it impermissible unless it penetrates like an arrow. The Hanafi position is that the instrument must cause a wound (by cutting or piercing) to make the game halal; otherwise, it is considered killed by blunt force (dhaqq) and thus carrion.

Key Terms
المعراض (al-mi'rāḍ): A type of arrow without feathers (or with four thin feathers according to al-'Aini) that sometimes hits sideways and sometimes with its point. The hadith distinguishes between hitting with its side (not permissible) and piercing (permissible).
البندقة (al-banduqah): A clay pellet used in hunting; it does not usually penetrate, so game killed by it is haram unless it has an edge and penetrates.
خرق (kharq): Piercing or penetrating the body, as opposed to striking with the side or blunt force.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A hunter uses a sharpened stick that penetrates the game's body. According to the Hanafi position, is the meat halal?
A) Yes, because it pierced.
B) No, because a stick is not a traditional weapon.
C) Yes, only if the stick had feathers.
D) No, because the stick is not allowed as a hunting tool.

Key Takeaway
The hadith sets the criterion: permissibility of game depends on the weapon piercing, not just wounding; blunt instruments are haram.

The Limb Cut from Living Game: Carrion or Halal?
A ruling and dispute regarding a limb cut from living game

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cutting a limb from living game, explain the Shafi'i counterargument, and evaluate the Hanafi response based on the hadith.

Core Idea
A limb cut from a living animal is carrion — the hadith overrides analogy to emergency slaughter.

This passage addresses a fiqh dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools concerning a limb cut from a living game animal. Is it permissible to eat that limb if the animal later dies from the wound? The Hanafi school rules it carrion based on a clear hadith. Let's trace the arguments and understand the practical consequence of this difference.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What does the Hanafi school rule regarding a limb cut from a living game animal? Is it permissible to eat?
The answer is no; the limb is considered carrion (ميتة).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Which school disagrees, and under what condition do they permit the limb?
Imam al-Shafi'i permits the limb if the animal dies from the cut, analogizing it to emergency slaughter.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
What evidence does Imam al-Shafi'i use to support his position?
He uses qiyas: the lethal cut is like dhabh al-ḍarura, so the separated limb becomes halal.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
How do the Hanafis refute the Shafi'i analogy? What is their key evidence?
They cite the explicit hadith: 'Whatever is cut from an animal while it is alive is carrion.' Since the animal is alive at the moment of cutting, the limb is carrion regardless of later death.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical consequence of this dispute for a hunter?
Hanafis: the limb is not edible even if the animal dies. Shafi'is: it is edible if the animal dies from the cut.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا العضو إلخ وقال الشافعي له: أكل إن مات الصيد منه؛ لأنه مبان بذكاة الاضطرار، فيحل كالمبان بذكاة الاختيار، بخلاف ما إذا لم يمت؛ لأنه ما أبين بالذكاة ولنا قوله عليه السلام: "ما قطع من بهيمة وهي حية، فما قطع منها فهو ميتة"، رواه ابن ماجه ذكر الحي مطلقا، فينصرف إلى الحي حقيقة وحكما، والعضو المبان بهذه الصفة؛ لأن المبان منه حي حقيقة لقيام الحياة فيه، وكذا حكما؛ لأنه يتوهم سلامته بعد هذه الجراحة، ولهذا اعتبر هذا القدر الحياة، حتى لو وقع في الماء وفيه هذا القدر من الحياة يحرم، بخلاف ما إذا أبين بذكاة الاختيار؛ لأن المبان منه ميت حكما، ألا ترى أنه لو وقع في هذه الحالة الماء، أو تردى من الجبل لا يحرم؛ لأن موته قد حصل بالإبانة حكما، فلا يضاف إلى غيره، وإن كان حصل بذلك حقيقة تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٦٥/٨] وقال في "ملا" "مسكين" و "الفتح": هذا أي الحكم المذكور من عدم أكل العضو إذا أبان شيئا يبقى المبان منه حيا بدونه عادة كاليد والرجل والفخذ، وثلثه مما يلي القوائم، والأقل من نصف الرأس، ولو قطع يدا أو نحوه، ولم يبنه إن توهم التئامه واندماله حل؛ لأنه جرح لا إبانة، وإلا بأن كان معلقا بجلدة حل ما سواه.
'Not the limb, etc.' And al-Shafi'i said to him: It is eaten if the game dies from it because it is separated by the emergency slaughter (dhabh al-ḍarura), so it becomes permissible like that separated by optional slaughter. Unlike when it does not die, because it was not separated by dhakah. And we have the statement of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Whatever is cut from an animal while it is alive, then what is cut from it is carrion.' (narrated by Ibn Majah). He mentioned 'alive' absolutely, so it applies to being alive in reality and by legal consideration. The limb separated in this manner is because that which is separated from it is alive in reality, as life remains in it, and likewise legally because it is assumed to survive after this wound. Therefore, this amount of life is considered such that if it falls into water with this amount of life, it is prohibited. Unlike when it is separated by optional slaughter, because that which is separated is legally dead. Do you not see that if in this state it falls into water or falls from a mountain, it is not prohibited because its death has occurred by the separation legally, so it is not attributed to something else, even if it actually occurred that way. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/465) And it is stated in Mulla Miskin and al-Fath: This, i.e., the mentioned ruling of not eating the limb, applies when one separates something from which the separated part normally remains alive without it, like the hand, leg, thigh, and one third of the head towards the limbs, and less than half of the head. And if one cuts a hand or similar but does not separate it, if it is expected to heal and scar, it is permissible because it is a wound, not an amputation. Otherwise, if it is hanging by skin, what remains is permissible.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage deals with a dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding a limb cut from a living game animal. The matn states that the limb is not eaten. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that if the game dies from the cutting, the limb should be permissible because the animal is effectively slaughtered by 'emergency slaughter' (dhabh al-ḍarura), similar to how an animal killed by a clean cut is halal. The Hanafi refutation is based on the hadith: 'Whatever is cut from an animal while it is alive is carrion.' The term 'alive' is absolute, covering both actual life and legal ascription of life. The body is considered alive in the sense that it could potentially survive the injury. Therefore, the limb is treated as carrion. The commentator notes that this is analogous to the case where if such an animal (with a limb cut but still alive) falls into water, its meat becomes haram because the death could be attributed to the water, not just the cutting. In contrast, in proper slaughter (dhabh al-ikhtiyar), the animal is legally dead at the moment of cutting, so subsequent accidents do not affect its permissibility. The commentary then clarifies that this ruling applies only when the separated limb is such that the animal could normally survive without it (e.g., a hand, leg, thigh, up to one third of the head). If the cut is not completely severed but expected to heal, the meat is halal. If it hangs by skin, the rest of the animal is halal. This is a nuanced Hanafi position preserving the principle of avoiding carrion.

Key Terms
ذكاة الاضطرار (dhakāt al-ḍarūra): Emergency slaughter: when an animal is killed by a lethal wound that would normally be a valid method of slaughter (like cutting the throat), but applied by necessity. Shafi'i uses this to justify the eaten limb.
إبانة (ibānah): The complete separation of a limb from the body. If the limb is not completely separated (muta'allaq bi jildah), it is not considered ibanah and does not make the rest haram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A hunter cuts off a deer's ear, and the deer dies from the wound. According to Hanafis, is the ear permissible to eat?
A) Yes, because the deer died from the cut.
B) No, because the ear is considered carrion.
C) Yes, because an ear is not a vital limb.
D) No, because the cut was not intended to kill.

Key Takeaway
A limb cut from a living animal is carrion (murdar) and not permissible to eat, even if the animal subsequently dies from the wound.

The Principle of Maban: When Cut-Off Parts of a Hunted Animal Are Halal
Explains when cut-off parts of a hunted animal are halal or haram.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle for when the cut-off part (maban) of a hunted animal is halal and apply it to various scenarios.

Core Idea
The maban is halal if the remaining animal has life comparable to a slaughtered animal; otherwise, it is haram.

You may think any part cut from a live animal is automatically haram, but Hanafi fiqh provides a nuanced rule. This lesson unpacks the principle of maban (the cut-off part) from Kanz al-Daqaiq. You will learn to distinguish cases based on whether the remainder could survive the injury, and thus determine the ruling for both the cut-off part and the rest.

Guided Path
1
Understand the key categories
Learn the two types of maban: one from an animal that is alive both in form and ruling (haram), and one from an animal alive in form only (halal).
Alive in ruling means the animal could survive the injury.
2
Identify the decisive criterion
The cut-off part is halal if the remainder's life is comparable to that of a slaughtered animal – i.e., it cannot survive the wound.
This is the core principle (al-asl) of the madhhab.
3
Apply to clear-cut scenarios
Examples: cutting off the head or splitting the animal in half = maban halal. Cutting off a limb (hand, thigh) = maban haram.
The remainder's ability to survive determines the ruling.
4
Handle borderline cases
If the animal is cut into three parts and the majority is from the tail side, the maban is halal; if the third is from the leg side, the maban is haram.
Also, cutting off half the head or more = halal; less than half = haram.
5
Extend to slaughter scenarios
Striking the neck from the front and cutting the jugular veins makes the meat halal (though disliked). Striking from the back: if the animal dies before jugulars are cut, it is not halal.
This demonstrates the principle in action outside hunting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أكل كله. لأن المبان منه حي صورة لا حكما؛ إذ لا يتوهم سلامته وبقاؤه حيا بعد هذه الجراحة، فوقع ذكاة في الحال، فحل أكله كما إذا أبين رأسه في الذكاة الاختيارية وكذا إذا قده نصفين، بخلاف ما إذا قطع يدا أو فخذا أو رجلا أو ثلثه مما يلي القوائم أو أقل من نصف الرأس حيث يحرم المبان، ويحل المبان منه؛ لأنه يتوهم بقاء الحياة في الباقي، ولما كان الحكم بقده نصفين مثل الحكم بقطعه أثلاثا، فالظاهر أن التقييد بقوله: "أثلاثا" اتفاقي، اللهم إلا أن يقال: خصه ليتهيأ له بناء قوله: "والأكثر مما يلي العجز"، فإنه حينئذ يكون احترازيا، يخرج منه ما إذا قطعه أثلاثا والثلث مما يلي القوائم كما بينا، فإنه يحرم الجزء المبان، ويحل المبان منه؛ لإمكان الحياة فيه فوق ما يكون في المذبوح. والأصل في هذه المسألة أن المبان من الحي حقيقة وحكما لا يحل كما إذا أبان عضوا يبقى المبان منه حيا بعده، والمبان من الحي صورة لا حكما يحل، وذلك بأن يبقى في المبان منه حياة بقدر ما يكون في المذبوح، فإنه حياة صورة لا حكما كما إذا قطعه أثلاثا والأكثر مما يلي العجز، أو قده نصفين، أو قطع نصف رأسه أو أكثر منه، فاحفظ هذا الأصل، فإن المسائل المذكورة في الصورتين متفرعة عليه، وإن ضرب عنق شاة، فأبان رأسها يحل بقطع الأوداج ويكره؛ لما فيه من زيادة الألم بإبلاغه إلى النخاع، وإن ضربها من قبل القفا، إن ماتت قبل قطع الأوداج، لم تحل، وإن لم تمت حتى قطع الأوداج، حلت.
His whole is eaten. The explanation: the cut-off part from it is living in form but not in ruling because its safety and remaining alive after this wound cannot be assumed, so it becomes a valid slaughter immediately, so it is lawful to eat it just as if its head was cut off in optional slaughter. Similarly, if he cuts it in half. Unlike if he cuts off a hand, thigh, leg, or a third of it from the side of the legs, or less than half of the head, then the cut-off part is unlawful, and the remaining part is lawful because life in the remaining part is possible. And since the ruling of cutting it in half is like the ruling of cutting it in three parts, it is apparent that the restriction by his saying 'in three parts' is accidental, unless it is said he specified it to prepare for his saying 'and the majority from the side of the tail', for then it is restrictive, excluding the case where he cuts it in three parts and the third is from the side of the legs as we explained, for then the cut-off part is unlawful and the remaining lawful because life is possible in it beyond what is in a slaughtered animal. The principle in this issue is that the cut-off part from a living animal in reality and ruling is not lawful, like if he cuts off a limb while the rest remains alive after it. And the cut-off part of a living animal in form but not ruling is lawful, which is when life remains in the cut-off part to the extent that remains in a slaughtered animal, for it is life in form not ruling, like if he cuts it in three parts and the majority from the side of the tail, or cuts it in half, or cuts off half of its head or more. So preserve this principle, for the mentioned issues in the two cases branch from it. And if he strikes the neck of a sheep and cuts off its head, it is lawful by cutting the jugular veins and it is disliked because of the increase in pain by reaching the spinal cord. And if he strikes it from the back of the neck, if it dies before cutting the jugular veins, it is not lawful; and if it does not die until he cuts the jugular veins, it is lawful.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from Kanz al-Daqaiq explained by the commentator. The core ruling is that the entire hunted animal is permissible to eat. The commentary then elaborates on a key legal principle: the cut-off part (maban) of an animal that is still alive in form but not in ruling is halal, while if it is alive both in form and ruling, it is haram. The criterion is whether the remainder of the animal has life comparable to that of a properly slaughtered animal. If the wound is such that the animal could not survive (e.g., head cut off, split in half, most of the body cut from the tail side), the cut-off part is considered a valid slaughter and is halal. But if the part cut off is a limb or less than half the head and the remainder could live, then the cut-off part is haram while the remainder is halal if subsequently slaughtered. The commentator also gives the example of slaughtering a sheep from the neck versus from the back: if the head is cut off and the jugular veins are cut, it is halal (though disliked for causing extra pain); if struck from behind and it dies before cutting the jugulars, it is not lawful.

Key Terms
المبان (al-mabān): The part that is cut off or separated from the animal's body.
ذكاة (dhabh): Ritual slaughter that renders an animal halal; here includes hunting wounds that accomplish the same.
أثلاثا (athlāthan): Cut into three parts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is the cut-off part (maban) of a hunted animal halal?
A) When the remaining part can survive the injury
B) When the cut-off part is a limb like a hand or leg
C) When the remaining part has life comparable to a slaughtered animal
D) When the head is cut off but the jugular veins are not cut

Key Takeaway
The cut-off part of a hunted animal is halal only if the remainder cannot survive the injury, making the severance equivalent to a valid slaughter.

Who May Hunt: Hanafi Ruling on Game of Non-Muslims and Muhrim
Stating whose hunted game is prohibited to eat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the permissibility of eating game hunted by Magians, idolaters, apostates, and muhrim, and explain the principle that eligibility for valid slaughter in choice determines validity in necessity.

Core Idea
Valid slaughter in choice determines validity in necessity – Kitabis are eligible, Magians, idolaters, apostates, and muhrim are not.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq lays down a key rule in Hanafi fiqh: the eligibility of a person to perform valid slaughter in normal circumstances (ikhtiyar) determines whether their emergency slaughter (idtirar) is valid. It specifically addresses hunting by various categories of people. Let's explore the ruling and its reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Categories
List the five categories mentioned: Magian, idolater, apostate, muhrim (pilgrim in ihram), and Kitabi (Jew/Christian).
2
State the Ruling for Each
Game of Magian, idolater, apostate, and muhrim is haram; game of Kitabi is halal.
3
Understand the Principle
The reasoning uses the principle: if a person cannot perform valid slaughter in the state of choice, they cannot in the state of necessity. This is because the inability is inherent to their person or status.
This is a rational extension (talil) based on the nature of valid slaughter.
4
Apply the Principle
Magians, idolaters, and apostates are never eligible for valid slaughter (they are not Muslims or Kitabis), so their hunting is invalid. A muhrim is not permitted to hunt during ihram, so his hunting is also invalid.
For the muhrim, the prohibition is specific to game; he can still slaughter domestic animals.
5
Note the Exception for Kitabi
A Kitabi is eligible for valid slaughter in normal circumstances (their meat is permissible for Muslims), so their hunting is valid.
This is based on Quranic permission to eat the food of the People of the Book.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحرم صيد المجوسي والوثني والمرتد. والمحرم بخلاف الكتابي. لأنهم ليسوا من أهل الذكاة حالة الاختيار، فكذا حالة الاضطرار، وكذا المحرم؛ لأنه ليس من أهل ذكاة الاختيار في حق الصيد، فلا يكون من أهل ذكاة الاضطرار فيه، ويؤكل صيد الكتابي؛ لأنه أهل الذكاة اختيارا، فكذا اضطرارا.
And prohibited is the game of the Magian, the idolater, and the apostate. And the muhrim (pilgrim in ihram) is different from the Kitabi. Because they are not among the people of valid slaughter in the state of choice, so likewise in the state of necessity. Similarly the muhrim, because he is not from the people of valid slaughter in the state of choice with respect to game, so he is not from the people of valid slaughter in the state of necessity for it. And the game of the Kitabi is eaten because he is a person of valid slaughter in the state of choice, so likewise in the state of necessity.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with its reasoning. It declares that the game hunted by a Magian (fire-worshipper), an idolater (worshipper of idols), an apostate (one who leaves Islam), and a muhrim (person in the state of ihram for Hajj or Umrah) is prohibited to eat. In contrast, the game of a Kitabi (Jew or Christian) is permitted. The reasoning given is that valid slaughter (dhabh) in the state of choice requires the person to be Muslim or Kitabi. Since Magians, idolaters, and apostates are not eligible for valid slaughter in normal circumstances, their hunting is also invalid in emergency situations (dhabh idtirari). Similarly, the muhrim cannot perform valid slaughter of game in normal circumstances (because he is prohibited from hunting), so his emergency slaughter is also invalid. The Kitabi, however, can perform valid slaughter in normal circumstances (their meat is permissible), so their hunting is also valid.

Key Terms
مجوسي (majūsī): A Zoroastrian fire-worshipper.
محرم (muḥrim): A person who has entered the state of ihram for Hajj or Umrah and is prohibited from hunting.
وثني (wathanī): Idolater, one who worships idols.
مرتد (murtadd): Apostate, one who leaves Islam.
كتابي (kitābī): A Jew or Christian, considered People of the Book.
ذكاة (dhakāt): Valid ritual slaughter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT among those whose game is prohibited according to this passage?
A) A Magian
B) An idolater
C) A Kitabi (Jew/Christian)
D) A muhrim

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the game of a Magian, idolater, apostate, and muhrim is prohibited, while that of a Kitabi is permissible, based on the principle that eligibility for valid slaughter in normal circumstances determines validity in necessity.

Two Hunters and Fatal Wounding: Ownership and Permissibility
Determining ownership and permissibility when two hunters hit the same animal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when a hunted animal belongs to the first or second hunter, apply the concept of 'fatal wound' (ithkhan) to determine permissibility, and distinguish the intra-Hanafi dispute on remaining life after a fatal wound.

Core Idea
A game animal belongs to the hunter who removes it from the sphere of resistance; a fatal wound transfers ownership and makes it haram for others.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq deals with a common hunting scenario: two hunters shoot the same animal. Who owns the game? Is it halal? The Hanafi school has detailed rules based on whether the first shot was fatal, and a subtle dispute between Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad on how much life must remain after a fatal wound. We will walk through the legal analysis step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two basic scenarios
Read the matn carefully: if the first shot does not fatally weaken (athkhana), the animal belongs to the second and is halal; if the first shot does fatally weaken, it belongs to the first and is haram for the second. Distinguish these based on whether the animal remains in the 'sphere of resistance' (hayy al-imtina').
Athkhana means wounding so the animal cannot escape.
2
Understand the key concept: sphere of resistance
A game that can still escape is in the 'sphere of resistance'; slaughter by a later hit is emergency slaughter (dhabh idtirari) and valid. Once fatally weakened, it leaves the sphere, and voluntary slaughter becomes possible; the first owner must slaughter it, and if not, the second becomes a killer and the meat is haram.
This explains the underlying reasoning (ta'lil).
3
Apply the exceptional case: fatal wound but life like a slaughtered animal
The commentary adds: if the first shot is fatal but the animal still has as much life as a freshly slaughtered animal (e.g., head cut off), it is halal. Here the first's ownership is established but the meat is not haram because the animal is effectively dead.
This is not a dispute; it is an exception mentioned in the commentary.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on extended life
If the fatal wound leaves the animal alive for a day or less (more life than a slaughtered animal), Abu Yusuf says the second's kill is not haram (since the remaining life is not considered valid), while Muhammad says it is halal (since the animal is still alive). The text does not give a tarjih (preferred view).
Thamarah al-khilaf: the ruling differs based on which imam's view is followed.
5
Examine the compensation rules
If the second kills an animal already owned by the first (due to fatal wound), the second must pay the first the full value minus the depreciation caused by the first's wound. If death results from both wounds, a complex three-part guarantee applies: compensation for first's wound, half the wounded value, and half the slaughtered value.
These detailed rules ensure justice between the hunters.

فهو للثاني وحل، وإن أثخنه فللأول وحرم. ثم قال: لأنه هو له، وقال علي: "الصيد لمن أخذه، وإنما حل؛ لأنه لما لم يخرج بالأول من حيز الامتناع كان ذكاته ذكاة الاضطرار، وهو الجرح في أي موضع كان، وقد وجد تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٦٥/٨] فللأول وحرم إلخ لأنه لما أثخنه الأول، قد خرج من حيز الامتناع، فصار قادرا على ذكاته الاختيارية، فوجب عليه ذكاته لما روينا ولم يذكه الأول، وصار الثاني قاتلا له فيحرم، وهو لو ترك ذكاته مع القدرة عليه يحرم، فبالقتل أولى أن يحرم، بخلاف الوجه الأول تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٦٥/٨] هذا إذا كان الرمي الأول بحال ينجو منه الصيد، أما إذا كان الرمي بحال لا ينجو منه الصيد، فإن بقي فيه من الحياة بقدر ما يبقى في المذبوح كما لو أبان رأسه، يحل وإن كان الرمي الأول بحال لا يعيش منه الصيد غير أنه يبقى من الحياة أكثر مما يكون في المذبوح بأن كان يعيش يوما أو دونه، فعند أبي يوسف لا يحرم؛ لأن هذا القدر من الحياة غير معتبرة عنده خلافا لمحمد يحله. وضمن الثاني للأول قيمته غير ما نقصت جراحته. كلها وقت إتلافه أي جراحة الأول. وضمن الثاني إلخ أي ضمن جميع قيمة الصيد غير ما نقصته جراحة الأول؛ لأنه أتلف صيدا مملوكا للغير؛ لأنه ملكه بالإثخان فيلزم قيمة ما أتلف وقيمته وقت إتلافه كانت ناقصة بجراحة الأول، فيلزمه ذلك، بيانه: أن الرامي الأول إذا رمى صيدا يساوي عشرة فنقصه درهمين ثم رماه الثاني فنقصه درهمين، ثم مات، يضمن الثاني ثمانية، ويسقط عنه من قيمته در همان؛ لأن ذلك تلف بجرح الأول، هذا إذا علم أن القتل حصل بالثاني بأن كان الرمي الأول بحال يجوز أن يسلم الصيد منه، والثاني بحال لا يسلم الصيد منه ؛ لكون القتل مضافا إلى الثاني، وإن علم أن الموت حصل من الجراحتين أو لم يدر، ضمن الثاني للأول ما نقصته جراحته، ثم يضمن نصف قيمته مجروحا بجراحتين، ثم يضمن نصف قيمة لحمه ذكيا، هكذا في "العيني"، ومثله في "التكملة" و"ملا مسكين" و"الفتح"، ودلائل هذه الضمانات الثلاثة المذكورة في قوله: "وإن علم أن الموت حصل من الجراحتين" مذكورة في "العيني" و"الفتح"، تركناها لطولها، من شاء فليرجع إليها.
So it is for the second and lawful, and if he weakened it fatally, then it is for the first and prohibited. Then he said: Because it is his, and 'Ali said: 'The game is for the one who takes it, and it is lawful only because since it was not removed from the sphere of resistance by the first, its slaughter is emergency slaughter, which is wounding in any place, and it has occurred.' [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/465] 'For the first and prohibited etc.' because when the first weakened it fatally, it has left the sphere of resistance, so it became capable of voluntary slaughter, so he was obligated to slaughter it as we narrated, and the first did not slaughter it, and the second became its killer, so it is prohibited, and if he abandons its slaughter while having ability, it is prohibited, so by killing it is more appropriate to be prohibited, unlike the first case. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/465] This is if the first shot was such that the game could escape from it. However, if the shot was such that the game could not escape from it, and there remains in it life to the extent that remains in a slaughtered animal, like if he cut off its head, it is lawful. And if the first shot was such that the game cannot live from it but there remains in it more life than what is in a slaughtered animal, such that it lives a day or less, then according to Abu Yusuf it is not prohibited because this amount of life is not considered by him, contrary to Muhammad who allows it. And the second is liable to the first for its value minus what was decreased by his wound. The whole at the time of its loss, i.e., the wound of the first. 'And the second is liable etc.' meaning he guarantees the entire value of the game minus what was decreased by the first's wound, because he destroyed a game owned by another; because he owned it by fatally wounding it, so he owes its value at the time of destruction, which was decreased by the first's wound. Explanation: If the first shooter shot a game worth ten and it decreased by two dirhams, then the second shot and decreased it by two dirhams, then it died, the second guarantees eight, and two dirhams are dropped from its value because that was lost by the first's wound. This is if it is known that the killing was caused by the second, meaning the first shot was such that the game could have survived from it, and the second such that it could not survive, because the killing is attributed to the second. If it is known that death resulted from both wounds or it is not known, then the second guarantees the first what his wound decreased, then he guarantees half its value wounded with two wounds, then he guarantees half the value of its meat as a slaughtered animal. Thus in al-'Ayni, and similar in al-Takmila and Mulla Miskin and al-Fath. The proofs of these three guarantees mentioned in his statement 'if it is known that death resulted from both wounds' are mentioned in al-'Ayni and al-Fath; we omitted them for their length, whoever wishes may refer to them.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage describes the ruling on a hunted animal that is shot by two different hunters. The matn states: if the first hunter's shot does not fatally wound (i.e., the animal could still escape), then the second hunter who kills it gets the game and it is halal. If the first's shot is fatal (weakens it to the point it cannot escape), then the game belongs to the first and it is haram for the second. The commentary explains the reasoning: in the first case, the animal was still in the sphere of resistance, so the second's killing is an emergency slaughter (dhabh idtirari). In the second case, the first's fatal wound removed the animal from the sphere of resistance, making it capable of voluntary slaughter; since the first failed to slaughter it, the second becomes a killer and the meat is haram. The commentary then discusses a scenario where the first shot is fatal but the animal still has life comparable to a slaughtered animal (as if the head was cut off): then it is halal. There is a dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: if the animal lives a day or less after the fatal wound, Abu Yusuf does not consider it haram (since the remaining life is not considered), while Muhammad considers it halal. Finally, the passage details compensation: if the second kills, he must pay the first the value of the animal minus the depreciation caused by the first's wound. If both wounds caused death or it is unknown, a complex three-tier guarantee applies: compensation for first's wound, half the wounded value, and half the slaughtered value.

Key Terms
إثخن (athkhana): To wound fatally such that the animal cannot escape or resist effectively.
حيز الامتناع (ḥayy al-imtināʿ): The sphere or state of resistance/ability to flee from the hunter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the first hunter's shot does not fatally weaken the animal (it can still escape) and the second kills it, what is the ruling?
A) The game belongs to the first and is halal
B) The game belongs to the second and is halal
C) The game belongs to the first and is haram
D) The game belongs to the second and is haram

Key Takeaway
A game animal belongs to the hunter who removes it from the sphere of resistance; a fatal wound transfers ownership and makes it haram for others, with a nuanced intra-Hanafi discussion on the threshold of remaining life.
